THE CORRUPTION OF CO-DESIGN

Designers are o
---
ten depicted as social change agents that serve the good in the world.
Similarly, co-design tends to be described as a democratic mode o
---
 creativity that is
somehow beyond reproach. But is change a virtue in itsel
---
, and do participatory
practices always produce socially beneﬁcial outcomes?
Such questions are becoming more pressing as co-design has emerged as a
dominant practice in planning and urban design, while also in
---
orming corporate
management and public administration. In this book, Otto von Busch and Karl
Palmås suggest that designers tend to overemphasize the place o
---
 ideals in design,
leaving them ill-equipped to deal with a social world o
---
 power-wielding and
zero-sum games. Seeking to reorient the concerns o
---
 the Scandinavian tradition
o
---
 participatory design, they suggest that co-design processes are ri
---
e with
betrayals, decay, and corruption, and that designerly empathy has morphed into a
new 
---
orm o
---
 cunning statecra
---
t.
In putting 
---
orward Realdesign as an alternative conception o
---
 design practice,
von Busch and Palmås ask: What hard lessons about the social must today’s
designers learn 
---
rom realists like Machiavelli?
Otto von Busch is Pro
---
essor o
---
 Integrated Design at Parsons School o
---
 Design,
The New School, New York.
Karl Palmås is Associate Pro
---
essor at Chalmers University o
---
 Technology,
Gothenburg.

“Who knew critical design theory could be 
---
unny?”
Lucy Kimbell, Pro
---
essor, University o
---
 the Arts London, UK

ories to correct the too-o
---
ten naïve expansion o
---
 their material practice to social
challenges. The Corruption o
---
 Co-Design manages to be a robust political argument
that is nevertheless attentive to the experimental particularities o
---
 design practice.
It marks a welcome step-change in what responsible co-designing entails.”
Cameron Tonkinwise, Pro
---
essor, University o
---
 Technology Sydney, Australia
“Machiavelli 
---
or democratic designers! A contradicto in adjecto? Not so 
---
or von
Busch and Palmås. Realdesign, the healing cure they ordinate 
---
or the participative
designer is bitter and it hurts, but it is most timely and help
---
ul since codesign and
design thinking today are 
---
ully integrated into neoliberal and public management
agendas, reducing the ‘good’ designer to an (unconscious) hypocritical moralist.
‘Good’ maybe dead, but the suggested remedy 
---
or the participatory designer does
not necessarily mean to give up co-created play
---
ul democratic utopian dreams,
but rather to learn how to consciously take into account the rationality o
---
 power,
to be able to seriously deal with betrayal, corruption, cunning and hypocrisy as
we play along. The Corruption o
---
 Co-Design is a good place to start!”
Pelle Ehn, Pro
---
essor Emeritus, Sweden

INTRODUCTION
The problems o
---
 participatory design

Let’s peek into the design studios and see what is going on.
In studio one, a designer sets out a participatory city planning process, using an
online avatar world. Here citizens can suggest or build their own structures o
---

how they want the city to look and discuss visions o
---
 their 
---
uture city.
In another studio, a designer puts up a noticeboard in the stairs o
---
 an apartment
building to listen to what the people in the neighborhood want designed, asking

---
or comments and ideas.
In a third studio, a designer participates in a suggested development o
---
 a local
market. It is situated in an area o
---
 the city neglected by planners, and the designer
considers how to engage the in
---
ormal transactions taking place.
In a 
---
ourth studio, a designer sets out to engineer an app to tackle traﬃc
congestion, seeking data that can geolocate cars across the city and 
---
oster
community through carsharing.
In a ﬁ
---
th studio, a designer starts a social enterprise with the aim o
---
 doing good

---
or a marginalized population, inviting volunteers to work 
---
or the community.
In a sixth studio, a designer sets out to “hack” into a local economy by suggesting
an alternative currency system.
And in a seventh studio, a designer wants to disrupt the status quo o
---
 homeless
shelters, starting a system that will rate shelters and guests to allocate beds and

---
unding better.
Perhaps you have been around some time in the world o
---
 design and recognize
the social and relational material shaped in the examples above. Maybe you can
see dark clouds gathering in each o
---
 the studios. Like so many other materials a
designer works with, the social realm has its recalcitrant properties. But instead o
---

irregularly swelling wood, twisting ﬁbers, or a stalling kiln, you may have
encountered how “real li
---
e” comes to usurp even the best-planned social
DOI: 10.4324/9781003281443-1

2

Introduction

scenarios. O
---
 course, you may say, the avatar world became a cesspool o
---
 abuse,
the 
---
riendly noticeboard was ﬁlled with insults, unsolicited goods were traded at
the market, the alternative currency became a tool 
---
or traﬃcking, real estate
moguls exploited the social enterprise, and so on and so 
---
orth, and all the rest o
---

it. Something is going on when designers engage in social sculpture as the rela­
tional materials o
---
 humans turn out as unruly as other natural materials. We must
ask: What happens along the way with all the good intentions?
Indeed, i
---
 you have 
---
ollowed the ﬁeld o
---
 design over the years, it would not be
uncommon that you could have encountered any o
---
 the cases above in one 
---
orm or
another. Perhaps you have even suggested or instigated one along these lines. The
examples above are quick glimpses o
---
 how the discipline o
---
 design has moved 
---
rom a
pro
---
ession in proximity to engineering, with reasonably modest aspirations and
straight
---
orward prompts, into a ﬁeld with lo
---
ty ambitions 
---
or understanding and
shaping societal processes, i
---
 not even claiming to redesign cultures themselves.
While some o
---
 the glances into these everyday scenarios o
---
 design may be stepping
into ethical dilemmas or more or less methodological mineﬁelds, they all also share a

---
undamental premise 
---
or the social settings o
---
 design. The “social” is a material with
certain propensities. The social realm is a material that can be shaped towards vir­
tuous ends, designers suggest. Relationships can be modiﬁed and recircuited with
design. People are basically good. And they all want more design. In the end, the
social world is just another material, waiting patiently to be shaped under the sensible
hands o
---
 the empathetic designer.
Under the banner o
---
 design thinking, there are 
---
ew limits to the endeavor o
---

design. Design makes humans more godlike, and each object or service is just an
instance on the trajectory towards a bright and virtuous 
---
uture. Under its pro­
gressive course, design is a practice that shapes the 
---
uture towards the ideals we hold.
But the pro
---
ession seldom asks who holds these ideals. Even though much o
---
 the
world is broken and un
---
air, and has been designed that way, designers still tell
themselves unreluctantly that more design is the tool to ﬁx what design originally
le
---
t unsatis
---
actory. It is seldom reﬂected that it could be the designers themselves
who are misled or ill-equipped to act in and aﬀect power dynamics. But this is
hard to see, as design keeps ﬁlling its vocabulary with terms wrapped in virtuous
connotations. A
---
ter all, don’t we all want more participation, 
---
unction, and beauty?
Who does not want more democratic design? Are these not the unparalleled paths
towards peace and justice? Even i
---
 not spoken aloud, these terms 
---
orm the
underlying values that shape design today, making some o
---
 the opening scenarios
such a recurring sight in design studios.
But there may also be another perspective. People are headstrong, making
social relations challenging to manage in ways 
---
oreign to the traditional materials
o
---
 design processes. The underlying 
---
orces that give values, loyalties, and habits
their 
---
orm are unlike those that shape the materials and commodities o
---
 con­
sumerism. The human material kicks back against designerly intentions in ways
that may undermine even the most righteous course o
---
 action. Virtues are

Introduction 3

corrupted as they 
---
ace the hell o
---
 other people. While the plasma o
---
 social rela­
tions may be intangible or more abstract than the traditional materials o
---
 the
design workshops, it is real just the same. Indeed, the social world o
---
 design may
just be too damn real: Real as in realist.
There is an urgency in unpacking the properties o
---
 social design, as collaboration,
participation, and co-design. Not only do they seep into design practices on a wide
scale, but they are commonly seen as essential tools in handling the critical need to
tackle the injustices o
---
 the world. With current resurgence in issues around social
justice it has become apparent that the social systems across societies are not broken
by unintended ﬂaws, but designed to exclude, extract, and plunder. It is becoming
apparent to designers that these are more than “wicked problems”. They are real
issues. Systemic, and also social, issues. And even more; they are realist issues.
This more skeptical perspective on the design o
---
 social matters may not be news to

---
eminist and decolonial practitioners. Indeed, as some o
---
 the case studies presented in
this book will show, the anti-social eﬀects o
---
 supposedly “social” designs are o
---
ten at
their clearest when viewed through 
---
eminist or decolonial lenses. Further, some
essential concepts are recurring in the social designers’ vocabulary – such as
questioning decision-making through the “ladder o
---
 citizen participation” (Arnstein
1969), and warnings against the “tyranny o
---
 structurelessness” o
---
 horizontal organi­
zation (Freeman 1972) – stem 
---
rom these traditions. Outside the ﬁeld o
---
 design,

---
eminist thinkers with realist inclinations have long highlighted conﬂict as a core
political mechanism which is commonly silenced to uphold consensus and power.
Some o
---
 these scholars appear in design school reading lists, such as Sara Ahmed
(2017), bell hooks (2000, 2014), and Chantal Mouﬀe (2000, 2005). As we will see,
Mouﬀe’s work in particular is a central re
---
erence in the contemporary discussion
on design and the political. Having said that, realism comes with substantial his­
torical baggage. While contemporary realist thought is slowly becoming less
male-dominated – with thinkers like Judith Shklar having become a part o
---
 the
canon – the realist tradition tends to be associated with a long list o
---
 white men,
and an unbecoming amalgam o
---
 cynicism and conservatism.
This 
---
act is all the more problematic when introducing this tradition in the
domain o
---
 Scandinavian participative design, which treasures inclusion and care as
its leitmoti
---
. Nevertheless, it is this very territory – where participation and political
realism meet – that this book will venture into. To set us oﬀ on our journey, let
us take a step back and look closer at the context and course o
---
 events that have
put the honorable Scandinavian design tradition to the test.

Scandinavians mugged by reality
Gothenburg, 15 June 2001: The trigger o
---
 an SIG Sauer handgun is pulled, and a

---
ate
---
ul bullet blasts out in the summer evening, only a block away 
---
rom the
University o
---
 Gothenburg’s School o
---
 Design and Cra
---
ts. For the ﬁrst time in
seventy years, the Swedish police 
---
orce uses live ammunition against protesters.

4

Introduction

During these peace
---
ul seventy years, the country had established itsel
---
 as a model
social democracy, based on consensual agreements and nonviolent bargaining
between the representatives o
---
 capital and labor. This is a perspective that has also
shaped its perspective on participatory design. The “consensual spirit” (sam
---
ör­
ståndsandan) that underpinned this politico-economic arrangement had served the
citizens well: During the post-war era, Sweden held the enviable position o
---

being one o
---
 the world’s wealthiest nations, while simultaneously enjoying the
position as the world’s most equal and equitable societies.
However, on that day in June, when US President George W. Bush visited the
city in conjunction with a European Council meeting, Gothenburg became the
stage 
---
or an animated dissensus. Drawing inspiration 
---
rom protests at the 1999 World
Trade Organization (WTO) Ministerial Con
---
erence in Seattle and the 2000
International Monetary Fund (IMF) and World Bank summit meeting in Prague,
organizations associated with the Global Justice movement, or the so-called “anti­
globalization” movement, used the event to demonstrate their discontent with the
Washington consensus-based, corporate-led, neoliberal globalization. While there,
they joined ranks with anti-EU campaigners inspired by protests during the previous
European Union (EU) summit, held in Nice in late 2000.
Intent on avoiding the violence o
---
 Seattle, Prague, and Nice, the Swedish
authorities, local activists, and academics had prepared a strategy 
---
or the Gothenburg
event. This implied that protesters would have a chance to engage in a dialogue with
the people in power. Despite these eﬀorts, a shot was ﬁred at a protester who had
attended a Reclaim the City party. The event had been attacked by a parallel protest,
causing the police to disband the party, which in turn sparked a violent standoﬀ.
Clashes between protesters and the police had started earlier that day, when the
Black Bloc had rioted on the central Avenue, literally a stone’s throw away 
---
rom the
park where the party was staged, just a block or two 
---
rom the School o
---
 Design
and Cra
---
t. The organizers o
---
 the dialogue would later state that they had been too
naïve when preparing 
---
or the event, underestimating the violent potential o
---

both protesters and the police.
The bullet hit the protester in the stomach. He survived but lost one kidney
and his spleen. On a ﬁgurative level, that shot also torpedoed the Swedish
authorities’ ambition to demonstrate the power o
---
 the “Gothenburg model” o
---

dialogue. The assembled world leaders could scarcely see how the outcomes o
---

the Swedish dialogue approach diﬀered 
---
rom other, less romantic approaches to
dealing with the threat o
---
 violence. The assembled protesters, on the other hand,
who had taken the promise o
---
 a dialogue at 
---
ace value, concluded that it was all a
charade. Many o
---
 them have subsequently admitted to becoming more cynical
regarding politics and more skeptical towards Sweden’s liberal democratic insti­
tutions. It was as though the violence had done irreparable damage to the dream
o
---
 building a democratic society on dialogue and broad participation.
This shattered dream 
---
orms the backdrop when assessing the Scandinavian
participative design tradition today. Indeed, in a 2016 lecture at the Rhode Island

Introduction 5

School o
---
 Design, design scholar Pelle Ehn suggests that he construes it as a
“melancholic design”. This outlook ties the Scandinavian participative design
tradition to the Nordic Noir literary genre. Both bodies o
---
 work are set against a
metanarrative o
---
 a once-great democratic and social model that seems to be
withering away. Yet, despite this incipient darkness, the antihero criminal
inspector Kurt Wallander – lead protagonist in Swedish author Henning Man­
kell’s crime ﬁction – insists that “we must stand up 
---
or democracy”. Ultimately,
Ehn suggests, Scandinavian participatory design is concerned with that very
challenge – how to cope with the political realities o
---
 a democratic and social
system in retreat. To put it bluntly: It is a design tradition that has emerged 
---
rom
the experiences o
---
 Scandinavians mugged by reality, a phrase borrowed 
---
rom
Irving Kristol, who 
---
amously deﬁned a neo-con as “a liberal mugged by reality”.
This general sense o
---
 being mugged by reality is expressed in a particular
manner 
---
or the generation that became socio-politically aware in the late nineties.
Irony is the word that comes to mind here: Given the violence involved, it may
seem glib to call these events “ironic” rather than “tragic” but, 
---
ollowing Rein­
hold Niebuhr (1952: 2), “tragedy elicits tears o
---
 admiration and pity 
---
or the
hero”, whom we believe to be virtuous and benign. Irony, on the other hand,
allows us to understand tragic outcomes as emanating 
---
rom the protagonist’s

---
oolishness, vanity, or pretentiousness. Irony sheds light on the comic absurdities
o
---
 social li
---
e, yet does not shrink to the 
---
act that so many dreams o
---
 the 
---
uture
tend to be cruelly re
---
uted by history.
With the beneﬁt o
---
 hindsight, we see two signiﬁcant ironies that highlight how
history tends to debunk political dreams. The ﬁrst irony is evident when com­
paring the political demands o
---
 the 2001 protesters with the 
---
uture world order
they would soon ﬁnd themselves in. Today, we have arguably passed the end­
point o
---
 the era o
---
 virtually uncontested liberal globalization, and this upending o
---

the previous order has moved at a bewildering pace. The gradual shi
---
t 
---
rom the
“Washington consensus” to a “Beijing consensus” was evident midway through
the noughties, and by 2008 the World Bank lending power was superseded by
that o
---
 China. However, the outcome o
---
 the 2016 US election punctured the
Bretton Woods-based international trade regime. Moreover, that same year, the
UK started the process o
---
 exiting the European Union. Fi
---
teen years on 
---
rom
Gothenburg, the protesters got their wish: A gradual evaporation o
---
 the post-89
liberal world order. The resulting mood, however, seems to be one o
---
 con­
sternation rather than jubilation. With the 2022 invasion o
---
 Ukraine, the outlook
has turned even gloomier.
The second irony relates to the 
---
ate o
---
 the modus operandi o
---
 political tactics.
The Reclaim the City party that preceded that 
---
ate
---
ul gunshot took its cue 
---
rom
the Reclaim the Streets parties that had become revered within the global justice
movement (see 
---
or instance Klein 1999). For “RTSers”, the act o
---
 staging a party
was seen as congruent with the Situationist idea o
---
 constructing situations as a
means to reclaim the urban space colonized by corporate power. Reclaiming

6

Introduction

involved a two-pronged approach, 
---
using the carnivalesque with guerilla gardening
and other constructive and “designerly” elements o
---
 protest. In the terminology o
---

de Certeau (1984), the RTSers had a clear and eﬀective tactic to counter the strate­
gies o
---
 corporations and urban planners. What happened to these tactics? Again, 
---
ast
---
orwarding into the present, municipal urban planners use those same ideas as
inspiration 
---
or “tactical urbanist” methods. Indeed, in the park where the Reclaim
the City party was staged, the municipality would go on to assist guerilla gardeners in
planting potatoes. The Situationist “situation” had morphed into the “pop-up”
phenomenon, and the Certeauian demarcation between the tactical and the strategic
no longer seems tenable. Paris ’68 slogans like “Beneath the tarmac … a 
---
orest” and
“Beneath the cobblestones … a beach”, deemed radical in 2001, today sound like
copy 
---
rom the policy dossiers o
---
 planning authorities.1
This 
---
ate o
---
 protest tactics relates to a wider concern within radical social
theory. Has the ’68 celebration o
---
 transgressive play
---
ulness become one o
---
 the key
imperatives o
---
 contemporary capitalism? Here, we can point to the argument o
---

Luc Boltanski and Ève Chiapello, who suggest that the “artistic critique” o
---
 the
capitalist order has morphed into a new spirit o
---
 capitalism, and that the qualities
that guarantee success in contemporary capitalism are “taken directly 
---
rom the
repertoire o
---
 May 1968” (Boltanski & Chiapello 2005: 97). In 
---
act, we already
have done so: In a pamphlet titled Abstract Hacktivism (von Busch & Palmås 2006),
we argued that the tactics that were 
---
ashionable around the turn o
---
 the century
were invariably le
---
tovers 
---
rom the counterculture o
---
 the late 1960s. Our problem
was not that the tactics increasingly represented a 
---
aux radicalism; one that had
gone 
---
rom being a threat to the system to being the system. On the contrary, we
saw them as harmless and – more importantly – pointless.
In Abstract Hacktivism, we suggested that the 
---
undamental problem o
---
 these
approaches was that they were 
---
ounded on a social ontology that understands and
relates to the world as i
---
 it were a motor. Hence the notion o
---
 “culture jam­
ming” – the idea o
---
 changing the system by jamming the motor that is society.
Re
---
erencing philosopher Michel Serres, we pointed to the connection between
the conceptual models through which we understand the world, on the one
hand, and the everyday technologies that surround us, on the other. Thus, the
motor worldview originated 
---
rom the motor era o
---
 the nineteenth and twentieth
centuries. Distancing ourselves 
---
rom this worldview, we wondered what political
action would look like i
---
 the world is a computer, and the activities o
---
 hackers
could be adopted 
---
or uses outside o
---
 the computer context. The idea o
---
 hacking
as general practice, “abstracted” 
---
rom its original mani
---
estation, represented a
more designerly and technologically in
---
ormed view o
---
 how to produce social
change. Indeed, the world o
---
 computers had provided us with an abundance o
---

terms and ideas – copyle
---
t, mesh network, open source, peer-to-peer, protocol –
that seemed to open a world o
---
 possibilities.
Fittingly, these technological concepts resonated with key ideas circulating
within a certain domain within radical theory – with Deleuzian rhizomatics and

Introduction 7

with the post-workerist notion o
---
 “exodus”. Exodus is the political strategy in
which change is achieved through the exit 
---
rom mainstream institutions, and the
simultaneous building o
---
 parallel institutions. These parallel institutions are ideally
non-hierarchical, democratic, and based on the idea o
---
 the common. Exodus thus
implies a de
---
ection rather than protest (Virno 1996: 199). In the terminology o
---

Michael Hardt and Antonio Negri, it represents a creative ﬂight o
---
 the multitude,

---
rom capital to absolute democracy. Moreover, the idea o
---
 abstract hacktivism
also chimed with the post-Seattle, post-Prague, and post-Gothenburg sensibilities
o
---
 activists: Much like the politics o
---
 the street had once usurped the politics o
---

the workplace, the politics o
---
 the street was usurped by the politics o
---
 creating
“commons”, either online (such as engaging in ﬁle-sharing) or oﬀ-line (such as
organizing community gardens and hacker/maker spaces). Again, 
---
ast-
---
orwarding
to the present time, this approach has also 
---
ailed to deliver. The commons to
which one may plan one’s exodus pale in signiﬁcance when compared to the
rhizomatic peer-to-peer in
---
rastructures and plat
---
orms constructed by companies
such as Uber. The hacker ethic o
---
 citizens is increasingly mobilized by either
corporate-led hackathons, or public sector initiatives to co-produce or crowdsource policies. The exodus path seems to have meandered its way back into the
status quo. In other words, much like the dreams o
---
 the 2001 street protesters,
our political dreams o
---
 the early noughties seem to have been cruelly re
---
uted by
history, or at least by 
---
orces in the social reality we would call “realist”.
In adherence to Ehn’s proposition that Scandinavian participative design is a
response to the grim realities o
---
 an imperiled democratic and social system, this
book will explore the nature o
---
 this darkened reality, and discuss how designers
may cope with it.

The reality o
---
 social design
What is the moral o
---
 the 2001 Gothenburg dialogue story, and in what way is it sig­
niﬁcant 
---
or design thought? The key political lesson o
---
 the story can be gleaned 
---
rom
the 
---
act that it highlights a set o
---
 tensions that are 
---
undamental to the understanding o
---

politics and society. Should politics be evaluated on the basis o
---
 intentions, like the
ones that led the Gothenburg authorities to pursue the dialogue agenda, or is it the
outcomes o
---
 actions that count? Is the political sphere a domain that thrives on delib­
eration and a consensual spirit, or does it inevitably involve dissensus, conﬂict, and even
the threat o
---
 violence? Should we strive 
---
or utopias, or should we rather worry about
the corruption o
---
 noble ideas and the decay o
---
 the social order? What is the place o
---

naïve belie
---
, and o
---
 cynical disbelie
---
, in politics? Political philosophers generally debate
these tensions with re
---
erence to “idealism” and “realism”, and the distinction between
these two 
---
undamental positions is central to the argument o
---
 this book. Indeed, the
argument starts 
---
rom the assertion that the theory and practice o
---
 design tends to be
based on an idealist account o
---
 the world. The next chapter will explore these positions
and the suggestion that design tends to be idealist, in 
---
urther detail.

Introduction

Be
---
ore going into the speciﬁcs o
---
 the idealist-realist debate, however, one may
ask a more 
---
undamental question: Why should designers, or design theorists 
---
or
that matter, care about the logic o
---
 social change and political action? One answer
is 
---
ound within the discussions on social design and design activism. Aligning itsel
---

with those subﬁelds, this book will argue that social change should be at the heart
o
---
 design. Secondly, given the 
---
act that design techniques and design thinking are
increasingly mobilized 
---
or the (re-)shaping o
---
 key 
---
unctions o
---
 government,
design is invariably implicated in the reinventing o
---
 democratic institutions, and
o
---
 democracy itsel
---
. Indeed, as inﬂuential design visionaries Tim Brown and
Roger Martin have suggested, design thinking can possibly be used as a tool 
---
or
making liberal democracy work better (Brown, Martin & Berger 2014). The
stakes are high – again, liberal democratic institutions have recently been sub­
jected to severe criticisms, 
---
rom supporters as well as detractors – but so are risks.
Engagement with the harsh realities o
---
 power may be perilous. Indeed, that is
another o
---
 the takeaways 
---
rom the story o
---
 the 
---
ailed dialogue in Gothenburg.
Despite such complexities, the interest in socially engaged design is soaring. With
exhibitions such as the 2007 “Design 
---
or the Other 90%” at Cooper-Hewitt
National Design Museum in New York, the virtuous design 
---
or human dignity and
human rights has over the last decade taken center stage and popularized a new
wave o
---
 humanitarian design (Smith 2007), largely building on Victor Papanek’s
the persuasive classic Design 
---
or the Real World (1971/1985). As evidenced in inﬂu­
ential books, like John Thackara’s (2005) In the Bubble and Emily Pilloton’s (2009)
Design Revolution, design seeks to move 
---
rom “Good Design” towards a “Design

---
or Good”. This implies solving real problems and dealing with well-being and
sustainability in a way that neither the market economy-driven design, nor state
actors, can manage. Here, designers are to work 
---
or the poor and marginalized,
with ecological and social issues, with minimizing “unintended” consequences o
---

top-down design, and with pushing “toward activism and humanitarian values”
(Pilloton 2009: 22).
This desire 
---
or designers to get involved in societal issues is hardly a novel phe­
nomenon. On the contrary: A 
---
rustration with the impotence to take on social
controversies and issues has echoed through the design ﬁeld as long as it has existed.
For instance, Adrian Forty (1986) recognizes how the division o
---
 labor already 
---
rom
the beginning o
---
 industrialism alienated the designer 
---
rom the needs o
---
 workers and
society. The 
---
rustration with such alienation is, in turn, a continuation o
---
 similar
critiques presented by the Arts and Cra
---
ts movement, by the Bauhaus, and by
observers within the industrial design pro
---
ession. When Bonsiepe (2006) deﬁnes
industrial design as a creative activity aimed at building a coherent, harmonious
environment, the resulting product is meant to 
---
ulﬁll the material and spiritual
needs o
---
 humanity, as well as reduce the possibility o
---
 domination.
The term “social design”, however, is a more recent phenomenon, which also
re
---
erences social practices within art, notably the social sculpture o
---
 Joseph Beuys.
I
---
 one accepts Ilpo Koskinen’s suggestion that social design is a process in which

Introduction 9

“the main design object is social” – that it represents design processes which use
the “social as its material” (Koskinen 2016: 18) – how does that square with the
lessons 
---
rom the Gothenburg dialogue story? Given the unpredictable and
recalcitrant nature o
---
 this social “material”, considerably less malleable than silk,
plywood, or Styro
---
oam, it is worth reviewing how theorists re
---
erenced within
social design conceive o
---
 “the social”. Here, we discuss this question solely in
relation to social design. We will return to this later in the book regarding the
introduction o
---
 co-creation and design thinking in the public sector.
What is the nature o
---
 the reality “out there” in society? In a text o
---
ten cited
within the social design ﬁeld, Victor Papanek suggests that designers must work
with the “real” world and “real problems”. This implies more substantial pro­
blems than the ones designers usually address with styling or their band-aid solu­
tions. Depth is the key metaphor utilized to describe the real world; real world
problems are much more pro
---
ound than those that the market 
---
ocuses on.
Indeed, in order to illustrate “the design problem” Papanek uses the ﬁgure o
---
 a
triangle with only the iceberg tip on the top as the traditional problem o
---
 design,
“the designer’s share”, whereas the massive bottom part is “the real problem”, that
is, the real needs or urgencies o
---
 the world (Papanek 1971/1985: 57).
Papanek’s triangle is not a design-oriented rehearsal o
---
 the classic sur
---
ace-depth
trope that the humanities and social sciences have inherited 
---
rom Freud and
Marx. The ﬁgure is not to be interpreted as a re
---
erence to 
---
alse consciousness, or

The designer’s share

The real problem

10 Introduction

to bases and superstructures, or to the broader theme o
---
 deceptive appearances
that disguise the true nature o
---
 reality. It merely suggests a reorientation o
---
 our
vision, speciﬁcally related to problem o
---
 design: Beneath the shallow deﬁnition o
---

a design problem, a chasm o
---
 real and contended social issues opens. Small,
market-oriented tweaks won’t do. The designer needs to make social considera­
tions in order not to perpetuate exploitation and injustices, class systems and social
status hierarchies. Instead, they must start addressing deeper social and ecological
concerns.
In addressing such concerns, “design should begin with people” (Papanek
1983: 7). To Papanek, the silver bullet to engaging with the deep complexity o
---

the real world – to organize the “human” – is democratization. His work thus

---
ollows a long tradition o
---
 designers and architects that have tried to anchor the
purpose o
---
 design in democratic ideals, while also countering some o
---
 the
authoritarian elements in their practices. Architects have long since recognized the

---
act that their structures shape their inhabitants, and tried to come to grips with
the notion that such structures are originally shaped by ideology. Thus, the ethics
concerning architecture and design has a long history, with highly inﬂuential
ideas stretching not least 
---
rom John Ruskin (1849, 1860), as well as William
Morris (1903, 1934). Some o
---
 these ideas still resonate and intersect with more
socioeconomic works such as Schumacher (1973).
Furthermore, politically explicit schools o
---
 architecture, such as the Bauhaus,
made clear their intentions 
---
or their political subjects. A
---
ter the break with
authoritarian regimes, architects explored how to make their processes and pro­
jects more democratic, using ideas o
---
 “open” works and systems (Alexander 1977;
Chermayeﬀ 1982). This desire to transcend the authoritarian and expert-ruled
past, and instead implement the ideals o
---
 democracy is also visible in design: The
more universal and participatory, the better. In an article about the seven princi­
ples o
---
 Universal Design, Rosemarie Rossetti suggests, “Simply put, universal
design is good design!” (Rossetti 2006). Across the ﬁeld, ideal-driven design,
applied to the social world, is good.
Yet, beyond the call 
---
or democratized and virtuous design, little is said about
the treacherous nature o
---
 the “real world”. The same can be said 
---
or other, more
radical approaches to how designers should address the social. Tomas Maldonaldo
suggests that a new designer role is necessary; one that breaks with the com­
modity-producing technocrat, as well as the normative planning o
---
 “technocratic
utopianism” proposed by modernists like Buckminster Fuller (Maldonaldo 1972:
29). A radical like Maldonaldo instead suggests a “revolution by design” which
corresponds to a “design by revolution”, rejecting political nihilism to instead
release a new “operative political praxis” o
---
 design (Maldonaldo 1972: 29). In a
more modest take, Victor Margolin and Sylvia Margolin suggest there must be a
“social model” 
---
or design, parallel to the typical market-oriented model, which
has been the dominant model 
---
or designers to address the world:

Introduction 11

The primary purpose o
---
 design 
---
or the market is creating products 
---
or sale. Con­
versely, the 
---
oremost intent o
---
 social design is the satis
---
action o
---
 human needs.
(Margolin & Margolin 2002: 25)
In order 
---
or designers to answer such social calling, they would need training that
teaches them to act beyond a design brie
---
:
Design skills cut across all situations, but skills in relating to vulnerable or
marginalized populations rather than to a brie
---
 
---
rom a manu
---
acturer need to
be developed by 
---
uture social designers.
(Margolin & Margolin 2002: 29)
Dean Nieusma (2004) draws similar conclusions, proposing an “alternative design
scholarship” that aims to support marginalized social groups, building on universal
design, participatory design, ecological design, 
---
eminist design, and socially
responsible design. Alternative design scholars analyze how technologies and
other designed arti
---
acts are implicated in larger social problems, such as rampant
consumerism, sexism, ecological abuse, lack o
---
 user participation and autonomy,
and restricted access to built environments, among others. Through these eﬀorts,
alternative design scholarship oﬀers designers an opportunity to think about how
their work might be directed as wisely and 
---
airly as possible (Nieusma 2004: 13).
Ezio Manzini, a leading proponent o
---
 social innovation, who represents a
design perspective that merges ideas o
---
 individual empowerment, entrepreneur­
ship and social change, has similar goals o
---
 betterment. With his perspective on
small, local, open, and connected (SLOC) design services, he suggests such per­
spective on design may even lead to “an emerging civilization” o
---
 “beauti
---
ul
islands o
---
 applied cultural and socioeconomic wisdom” (Manzini 2015: 26). Even
i
---
 these islands are scattered in a sea o
---
 unsustainable ways o
---
 being and doing, he
suggests social innovation may be a new rising continent o
---
 emerging potentiality.
Manzini cites Indian writer Arundhati Roy’s 
---
amous statement: Another world is
not only possible, she is on her way. On a quiet day, I can hear her breathing.
For Manzini, social innovation serves to make this 
---
uture visible; it is what makes
this 
---
uture multiply on a worldwide scale (Manzini 2015: 26). This optimism is
tempered by a recognition o
---
 some o
---
 the impediments 
---
or change. He acknowl­
edges the existence o
---
 “power
---
ul 
---
orces that are ﬁghting against the emergence o
---
 a
new, sustainable world”, pointing to how they strive to “protect their existing
interests”, and how they are “leading us in the wrong, unsustainable direction”
(Manzini 2015: 27). However, he also states that it is not his ambition to 
---
ocus on
those 
---
orces. Instead, he wants to “oﬀer an oriented overview o
---
 the state o
---
 things,
meaning a view that aims to trigger, support, and orient possible design actions”
(italics in original). Thus, in its de
---
ault mode, his design 
---
or social innovation is a
virtuous endeavor that produces examples o
---
 more sustainable ways o
---
 being in the
world, but nevertheless disregards the contestations o
---
 power.

12 Introduction

These perspectives all point to diﬀerent aspects o
---
 the societal context that
designers increasingly engage with. There is a recognition o
---
 the existence o
---
 “real”
problems with “depth”, o
---
 marginalized groups, and o
---
 
---
orces that resist change.
However, o
---
 the authors mentioned above, Nieusma goes the 
---
urthest in high­
lighting the treacherous aspects o
---
 the social, arguing that conﬂicts are inherent in
participatory processes. He is there
---
ore skeptical towards the user-centered approach,
where the social context is reduced to primarily take the side o
---
 the user, thus losing
some important grounding. Nieusma suggests an alternative design scholarship that is
more attuned to conﬂicts, called “appropriate design” which accounts 
---
or diversity,
conﬂict, and disagreement. Furthermore, Nieusma warns that a social design should
not be too conﬁdent in “solving” issues, as most social tensions are never solved but
only temporarily displaced. Thus,
we also should recognize the dangers o
---
 
---
eeling satisﬁed that alternative
design scholarship has 
---
ound the correct path: that it has arrived. Like
democracy, appropriate design is an ongoing activity that can never be 
---
ully
or ﬁnally achieved.
(Nieusma 2004: 24)
This democratically oriented perspective on design, recognizing contestation,
social tensions, and compromises, has subsequently been 
---
urther elaborated upon
by design scholars such as Björgvinsson, Ehn and Hillgren (2012a), DiSalvo
(2012), Keshavarz and Maze (2013), Markussen (2012), and McCarthy and
Wright (2015). These conversations on agonistic approaches to design serve as a
point o
---
 departure 
---
or the argument that we will pursue in Chapter 3; the ﬁrst
substantive chapter o
---
 the book. These somewhat more cautious approaches to
social design – which in eﬀect places a question mark over the notion o
---
 the
social as an object to be designed – resonates with the political-theoretical realist
perspective on politics and society. Arguably, such realism may provide a richer
description o
---
 the nature o
---
 the social that designers are increasingly engaging
with. That, at least, is what this book hopes to show.

The structure o
---
 the argument
Apparently, Deleuze once told a 
---
riend that any worthwhile book must per
---
orm
three 
---
unctions (Culp 2016: 1). First, it must start 
---
rom polemics: The book must
show that other scholarship commits an error. Secondly, it must involve recovery,
leading to a rediscovery o
---
 an essential insight overlooked within that other
scholarship. Thirdly, the argument must contain an element o
---
 creativity; the
creation o
---
 new concepts.
As regards this particular book, the reader will already have a sense o
---
 the polemic
that will be laid out in the 
---
ollowing pages. Borrowing 
---
rom the terminology o
---

political theory, we are suggesting that design scholarship has put an undue emphasis

Introduction 13

on idealist understandings o
---
 what design is about. However, we also hope to
show that topics and phenomena discussed within the design discipline may be
reconstrued in a realist manner. We will per
---
orm this recovery operation through
revisiting classical case studies 
---
rom the ﬁeld, as well as through discussing new
empirics. In the end, we will present a new concept o
---
 design, which we hope
practitioners may ﬁnd use
---
ul.
Accordingly, this argument is laid out in three main parts. The ﬁrst part
contains two opening chapters: This introductory chapter will be 
---
ollowed by a
chapter that ﬂeshes out the polemic against idealist design in greater detail.
Thus, Chapter 2, titled “The realist challenge”, unpacks the 
---
undamentals o
---
 the
distinction between idealism and realism in political theory and international
relations, and transposes these positions onto design. It also speciﬁes the nature
o
---
 the challenge posed by realism: How can one be a realist without getting
bogged down with a sense that the world is 
---
ull o
---
 impossibilities? How can one
ﬁnd a utopianism that is not moralist? How does one reclaim the creative element
inherent in realism?
The second part o
---
 the book consists o
---
 
---
our substantive chapters that discuss
design thinking and practice in the context o
---
 the public sector, civil society, and
social enterprise. More speciﬁcally, the 
---
ocus will be on the design o
---
 organized
services, and on the design o
---
 environments 
---
or living (Buchanan 1992: 9–10).
Each o
---
 the 
---
our chapters discuss these phenomena by engaging with themes and
problematics sourced 
---
rom the realist lexicon: Betrayal, corruption, cunning, and
hypocrisy.
Chapter 3, “Betrayal”, continues the interrogation o
---
 the political stakes o
---
 a
dialogue, as introduced above. However, rather than discussing protesters on
the streets, it explores the notion o
---
 dialogue in processes o
---
 co-design. The
chapter ﬁrst reviews how citizen participation – used as a governance techni­
que – has been challenged 
---
rom political theorists on the “agonist” end o
---
 rea­
lism, and 
---
rom critics that dismiss such processes as “post-political”
choreographies o
---
 neoliberal governance. Discussing the case o
---
 a citizen dialo­
gue in the context o
---

actor-network theory as an alternative account o
---
 how post-political eﬀects may
result 
---
rom participatory design processes. This theoretical shi
---
t implies shi
---
ting
the 
---
ocus 
---
rom ideology critique to a close examination o
---
 the material modes
o
---
 co-design. The chapter argues that the notion o
---

---

---

abstraction: First, there is a sense in which citizens’ and co-designers’ interests
are betrayed in the process o
---

---

---
act that policy­

---

---

---

its notion o
---

---

ideas 
---

---

14 Introduction

Chapter 4, “Corruption”, continues the discussion on the tendency towards
dri
---
t, co-option and betrayal in design. However, the 
---
ocus will be on how the
socially oriented institutional designs decay over time. That process o
---

not inevitable. However, in order to prevent such corruption, idealist-creative
inclinations o
---

tells the story about participatory budgeting (PB): The beacon o
---

---

Global Justice movement, which 
---

being a standard item in the World Bank policy toolbox. The chapter introduces
the work o

---

---

study the trajectory o
---

---

spread across the globe and ultimately 
---

the corruption o
---

pet idea within global development circles – has led to more disastrous out­
comes than that o
---

innovation and corruption as a means to help well-intentioned designers avoid
unintended outcomes.
Chapter 5, “Cunning”, orients the view towards the place o
---
 design in emer­
ging modes o
---

---
rom a case description o
---

“design 
---
or government” is construed in the context o
---

deployed as a means to trans
---

Building upon the idea o
---

thought has historically viewed design as a kind o
---

---
ocus
will be on how certain 
---

---

---

rom the oﬃcial

the context o
---

o
---

---

---
ormed government in Finland, tying it to cybernetic

---

---

---

in notions o
---

---

---

nevertheless seems to have reinvented how sovereigns govern their subjects.
Chapter 6, “Hypocrisy”, attempts to ﬁnd a suitable sensibility 
---
or the realist
designer who recognizes that design is ridden with betrayal, corruption, and
cunning. Indeed, can a realist designer practice design in good 
---
aith? Can that
designer avoid charges o
---

examines the stakes o
---

and other virtues came to play a dominant role in design. These sentiments are
traced back to the work o
---

---

technocracy and the counterculture serves as an entry point to the question o
---

hypocrisy in design. The vice o
---

---

o
---

Introduction 15

reexamination o
---

---
amous case o

---

which kind o
---

---

---

like Hobbes and Bentham. Through these thinkers, the chapter lays the 
---
ounda­
tion 
---
or a sensibility that maintains a skeptical position, while at the same time
steering clear o
---

ductive diﬀerence in the world.
The third and last part contains one chapter. Chapter 7 sketches the outlines o
---

realism-inspired mode o
---

not so much to repeat the argument o

not moralist, and reclaim the creative element inherent in realism.

THE REALIST CHALLENGE
Power and possibilities

The design critique at the School o
---
 Design and Cra
---
ts in Gothenburg was just like
any other o
---
 the classic 
---
ormat I had earlier encountered at other design schools.
The students had prepared projects about 
---
uture scenarios around the theme o
---

mobility in the city. Posters and models were set up along the walls o
---
 the studio
and the class pro
---
essors were browsing through the little exhibit o
---
 materials be
---
ore
the presentations began. The mood was polite, even i
---
 the students had turned
even more apprehensive as the visiting architecture pro
---
essor 
---
rom Chalmers Uni­
versity o
---
 Technology solemnly dressed in black turned up, 
---
ollowed by a visiting
guest pro
---
essor and a planner 
---
rom the city. Now, this was no longer just the
average class crit. With outsiders coming in to evaluate and give 
---
eedback, the
stakes o
---
 projects suddenly turned more real.
The presentations 
---
ollowed the usual 
---
ormat. Projects explored transportation
in the city, 
---
rom tram accessibility, trans
---
ormed parking spaces, and public bikesharing systems. Students presented their situation and the research they had
done, 
---
ollowed by ideas and models, highlighting how their design process had
evaluated many options to ﬁnally reach and render what they considered the best
outcome. The renderings showed something looking almost as i
---
 it existed at the
chosen place in the city; the more high-resolution and realistic the presentations
the easier it was to recognize and evaluate the pros and cons o
---
 the design. Some
projects were more critical, questioning the brie
---
, challenging some parameters,
making something barely 
---
unctional, but suggesting interesting twists and turns
on the principles guiding the design process. But in the presentations, the students
all said a variant o
---
 the same 
---
ormula; “look here, there is an issue, I researched
the concerns o
---
 these stakeholders, made these surveys, here are some ways to

---

What stands out in the presentations and critiques, and still plagues most project
critiques we have participated in, is the lack o

virtuous, it is the ideal solution to the issue. In the end, designers are the good

Design projects, even those with critical or political aspirations, work in worlds seen
through idealist-tainted glasses. The world may be corrupt, citizens betrayed, and
politicians hypocritical. Capitalism may be cruel and laws unjust, but design is a

bad guys, that come with the whips. Design is always only a matter o

never en

The realist challenge
What the scenario above points to is a realist challenge to the idealism that serves
as the dominant paradigm within contemporary scholarship on design. During
the argument, it will be evident that there are sound reasons why design scholars
and design practitioners tend to adopt an idealist position. Indeed, while an una­
bashed idealist may well choose to become a designer, it is more unlikely that a
doctrinaire realist would engage with design. Idealism trades in possibilities, causing
arch-realists to accuse them o
---
 being naïve about whether those possibilities are
attainable, and about the outcomes o
---
 realizing idealist ambitions. Realism, on the
other hand, trades in impossibilities, in highlighting the limited space o
---
 the 
---
ea­
sible, pointing to all the ways in which the “real” world crushes the ambitions o
---

the idealist. Constantly obsessing about power, and the ﬂawed conditions and
relations between human beings, the arch-realist is accused o
---
 being hopelessly
cynical. Indeed, political realism is highly contested, even loathed, 
---
or this sup­
posedly cruel cynicism. Michael Williams states:
To some, being a Realist represents the height o
---
 wisdom: the mark o
---
 a
clear-sighted ability to understand the world the way it is, a willingness to
con
---
ront the dynamics o
---
 power and interest that are held to govern world
politics. To others, Realism is a mark o
---
 
---
ailure: morally obtuse and his­
torically anachronistic, it represents a lack o
---
 political understanding and
imagination that is misleading at best, pernicious and destructive at worst.
(Williams 2005: 1)
This rendering o
---
 the idealist-realist debate may suggest that design thought, as a
creative and visionary endeavor, invariably must be anti-realist. Indeed, such a

The realist challenge 19

proposition seems plausible when browsing the classics thinkers o
---
 design. Note

---
or instance Herbert Simon’s touchstone 
---
or distinguishing design sciences 
---
rom
the natural sciences: Whereas the “natural sciences are concerned with how
things are”, design “is concerned with how things ought to be” (1996: 114).
Thus, natural sciences seem to be inherently realist, while design sciences must be
inherently idealist. Note also how design is wedded to the notion o
---
 intentionality
in contemporary interpretations o
---
 design thinking, where empathy becomes the
main skill o
---
 the designer to attune their inquiry with the needs and ambitions o
---

the users. Again, given that the arch-realist cares only about outcomes, and is
skeptical towards the virtue o
---
 good intentions, one may surmise that design
thinking must be antithetical to realism. Finally, note Buckminster Fuller’s 
---
amous
words o
---
 encouragement, directed at innovators: “Dare to be naïve”. Surely, that
clinches the argument against realism within design thought? Doesn’t realism just
hamper the visionary possibilities o
---
 design?
It is essential to not limit realism to a conservation o
---
 the status quo and zero-sum
games. While these are recurring traits, they are not the only prism o
---
 the realist
perspective. Both idealists and realists engage in games o
---
 designs and imagination. As
realist thinker Raymond Geuss (2010) highlights, imagination stands behind all 
---
orms
o
---
 politics, spanning 
---
rom recurring ideas such as a “natural order” to the triumph o
---

“justice”. What drives politics, Geuss argues (and we could certainly add design
here), is “the 
---
antasy o
---
 [the world’s] plasticity, o
---
 a potentially inﬁnite process o
---

change and improvement, o
---

---
ormation o
---
 the world into some­
thing more per
---
ect and more to our taste” (Geuss 2010: viii). While it may be easy to
think that conserving the current order o
---
 things does not require creativity, Geuss
puts it the other way: That “even the deepest kind o
---
 political con
---
ormism and any
de
---
ense o
---
 the status quo require acts o
---
 imagining o
---
 some kind, albeit a particular
kind o
---
 productive imagination” (Geuss 2010: x). Humanity keeps inventing new
brute realities; they are not reserved to Hobbes’ natural condition o
---
 an imagined
past. And design certainly plays an important role in the execution o
---
 brutality.
As we will see, there are constructive ways to present the realist challenge to
design thought. This is because elements o
---
 realist thought already reside within
contemporary design thought. Moreover, the idealist and realist schools o
---

thought are more nuanced than a quick introduction may suggest. The debate
between realists and idealists in political thought has its disciplinary home in two
related ﬁelds: Political theory and international relations. While those two ﬁelds
are distinct and do 
---
eature somewhat diﬀerent approaches to realism, the inter­
disciplinary conﬂicts between the two ﬁelds are not o
---
 major relevance to the
main argument o
---
 this book. Moreover, there are schisms and 
---
ractions, such as
realists standing against neorealists, debating i
---
 the impossibilities originate within
the nature o
---
 human beings, or 
---
rom without, in relations and power games. In a
similar vein, de
---
ensive realists argue against oﬀensive realists, tying their perspec­
tives to the many driving 
---
orces behind security competition in the unpredictable
anarchy o
---
 international political systems where intentions and capabilities are

20 The realist challenge

obscured and gamed with. With its aﬃrmative and constructive stance, design easily
misses the competitive and dangerous world o
---
 realism: Uncertain to discern the
true intentions and capacities behind the actions o
---

the social varnish o
---

---

---
 not 
---
ear.
Our point is not to 
---

---

bring attention to the blindness design shares with idealist perspectives, a wil
---
ul
ignorance and rejection o
---

---

Just to give an example, a poignant observation o
---

sheimer (2018) is how the international politics driven by the US liberal agenda has
traditionally strived to remake the world in its image (such as protecting human
rights, establishing democratic systems, promoting peace), but has in reality become
a highly militarized state ﬁghting global wars that undermine peace, harm human
rights, and even cripples liberal values also within its borders. In a similar vein, the
promotion o
---

---
or development also rebounds
to undermine the civic agency it sets out to promote. We will return to this in the
coming chapters.
As a general 
---
ramework, realism is associated with the dark arts o
---
 politics laid

---

statesmen that is labeled realpolitik. This chapter will later return to the notion o
---

realpolitik, and ﬁrst introduce realism by way o
---

Machiavelli? Here is one introduction:
Machiavelli, a republican at heart, established the 
---
oundations o
---

in his Discourses on the First Decade o
---

---

---
ten taken as

however, the two works are one and the same: i
---

the harsh realities o
---

(Latour 1988)
In these opening sentences 
---
or his well-cited chapter on Machiavelli, Bruno Latour
captures two key points o
---
 entry into the thought o
---
 the Florentine diplomat and
political theorist. The most obvious one relates to Machiavelli’s reputation 
---
or being
a cynic 
---
or pointing out the harsh realities o
---
 power. Indeed, writing in the early
sixteenth century, he was an early exponent o
---
 understanding politics studying the
“eﬀectual truth” o
---
 political li
---
e, rather than our “imagination o
---
 it” (Machiavelli
1998: 61). While this approach may seem reasonable, it nevertheless clashed with
the dominant view o
---
 his contemporaries, who argued that politics must invariably
start 
---
rom high-minded ideals; 
---
rom the cardinal virtues. As we shall see, the pro­
position that politics should be understood in terms o
---

today. Secondly, the quote highlights the seeming contradiction between The
Prince – a manual 
---
or power wielders, addressed to a monarch – and the post­

---
ounding text o
---

republicanism. This disparity has been debated among scholars: Did Machiavelli’s

The realist challenge 21

political views change over time, or did he simply lack solid political convictions?
I
---
, like Latour suggests, he was always “a republican at heart”, was he insincere
when writing The Prince, under political pressure to please the ruling monarch? For
Latour – and 
---
or realist authors such as Leo Strauss – there is no contradiction to
explain. The two works are presenting the same argument, representing diﬀerent
perspectives on the same essential truth. The democratic order rests on a clear
understanding o
---
 how power really operates. Obversely, a reluctance to accept the
harsh realities o
---
 power represents a threat to the democratic order.
Thus, as political scientist Eckard Bolsinger puts it, realists like Machiavelli insist
on highlighting the “unpleasant” and “somber” sides o
---
 politics, and “bluntly point
to the central structural 
---
eatures o
---
 modern power and politics that are normally
hidden in a myriad o
---

Lenin and Carl Schmitt as 
---
urther examples o
---

the uncom
---
ortable necessity 
---
or political thought to con
---
ront its critics:
Much as one might reject [Lenin’s and Schmitt’s] views o
---
 politics and state
as politically dangerous or morally repelling, political theory has to come to
grips with the problems that they raised and the solutions that they oﬀered i
---

it wants to understand the whole experience o
---
 political li
---
e.
(Bolsinger 2001: xvi)
In contrast, idealists – which political philosopher Bernard Williams re
---
ers to as
“moralists” – base their analysis o
---
 politics on moral claims related to terms like justice
and rights. Thus, they represent “the priority o
---
 the moral over the political,” making
“the moral prior to the political” (Williams 2005: 2). Moralist political theory can
thus be understood as a 
---
orm o
---
 “applied moral philosophy” or “a branch o
---
 legal
philosophy”. Realism, on the other hand, sets oﬀ 
---
rom use o
---
 “distinctively political
concepts, such as power, and its normative relative, legitimation” (77). This does not
mean that realists are uninterested in moral issues, or that moralists are uninterested in
power – the demarcation between the two indicates where the moralist and realist
start their analysis o
---
 political li
---
e. It also indicates what categories – power and
legitimation, or rights and justice – they choose to emphasize. “To think politically”,
political philosopher Raymond Geuss suggests (2010: 53), “is to think about agency,
power, and interests, and the relations between these”.
Given that categories such as justice and rights tend to be general and allencompassing, moralists tend to lean towards a universalist understanding o
---

political virtues. Realists, on the other hand, tend to pay closer attention to
the particular situation in which politics un
---
olds. Thus, moralists believe that
general conceptions o
---
 justice and rights cut across local speciﬁcities related to
particular power arrangements. Realists, on the other hand, insist that the
particularities o
---
 a given power relation override universalist moral claims.
Thus, the political truths are valid, as Bernard Williams phrases it, “now and
around here” (Williams 2005: 8).

22 The realist challenge

More speciﬁcally, they argue that universalist concepts always have a history –
such abstractions always emerge at speciﬁc points in time, 
---
or speciﬁc reasons – and
that their supposed “naturalness” can be interrogated through a genealogical
method. To take a 
---
amous example in design, Victor Papanek’s inﬂuential book
Design 
---
or the Real World (1971/1985), has overall been primarily read as a discourse
and method o
---

---
rom the narrow demands o
---

---

---

---

o

beaux arts aesthetics, a new generation o
---

---
or a
design with a humanist 
---
ace. But as elegantly suggested by Alison Clarke (2021),
Papanek’s “real” needs also align with a turn in political conceptions o
---
 power at
the time; a turn 
---
rom militaristic means 
---
or power projection, towards the so
---
t
values o
---

---
ronts 
---
or liberal and US-dominated
power dynamics. By turning design 
---
rom merely a provider o
---
 rational goods

---
or the people into a social and moral judgment, the inherent virtues o
---

design appeared at center stage, suggesting true design being democratic and
anti-totalitarian, thus playing along as yet another theater o
---
 conﬂict in the
cold war. Under the guise o
---

suggests a turn 
---
or design institutions towards a Realist world.
In practice, moralists and realists see institutions in a diﬀerent light. For the
moralist, the institutional arrangements o
---
 practical politics can be derived 
---
rom
moral principles. In other words, institutions are primarily understood as means to
realize more abstract conceptions o
---
 justice. For realists, institutional 
---
rameworks
are more than mere instruments 
---
or the realization o
---
 ideals. Institutions, they
argue, can in themselves inﬂuence the trajectories o
---
 political li
---
e. Crucially,
institutions too have histories, and may take on a li
---
e o
---
 their own, shaping poli­
tical agendas, cementing power structures, and so on. Given that institutions have
such lives o
---
 their own – outside moral categories – the purposes o
---
 an institution
may dri
---
t over time. More speciﬁcally, the institution may be corrupted, and may
eventually perish.
The ﬁnitude o
---
 political institutions is also evident in the anti-utopian sentiments
o
---
 realism. Rather than looking 
---
orward to the positives that the 
---
uture may bring,
the realist 
---
ears all the negatives. The realist thus emphasizes the possibility 
---
or poli­
tical regress, which means that the securing o
---
 order – rather than seeking justice –
represents the “ﬁrst question” o
---
 politics. Again, realists are not dismissing the virtue
o
---
 justice: Rather, their anti-utopian inclinations cause them to believe that the
sacriﬁcing o
---
 order when seeking justice may produce disastrous outcomes. Conse­
quently, realists are skeptical towards good intentions that do not deliver good out­
comes. This skepticism towards mere good intentions ultimately stems 
---
rom a
pessimistic view o
---
 human nature. Realists see humans are 
---
undamentally ﬂawed,
arguing that political developments are driven by the protagonists’ passions and
emotions, rather than the rationality that idealists tend to assume. Moreover, when
rationality prevails, the realists would question the extent to which rationalism can

The realist challenge 23

tame the wild contingency o
---
 the real world. Given that humans are seen as driven
by “baser” motives, realists see the political as a sphere characterized by dissensus and
conﬂict. Again, this can be contrasted with the idealist concept o
---
 the political as a
space 
---
or consensus-seeking.
The realist is, however, not turning a blind eye to the question o
---
 intentions.
Certainly, intentions are needed 
---
or action to take place. But the realist is mind
---
ul
that intentions are merely the direction or vision that leads action – it is the
outcomes that count. Here, we may revisit – and revise – Papanek’s model o
---
 the
design problem. While designers traditionally 
---
ocus on intentions, rendering their
best visions as convincing as possible, the realist takes the ﬂawed conditions o
---

human relations into account to make sure outcomes are best assured. A realign­
ment o
---
 the vision o
---
 the designer is needed; a 
---
ocus on outcomes engenders a
sober perspective on power. Thus, Papanek’s triangle may be redrawn as 
---
ollows:

Intentions

Outcomes

FIGURE 2.1 The intentions or outcomes problem.

We may now return to Machiavelli. The points above – the interest in the
particular institutional arrangements, the 
---
ear o
---

---

tions, the 
---
ocus on securing order, the emphasis on outcomes over good inten­
tions – are all evident in Machiavelli’s thought. As argued by Pocock (1975: viii),
his work can be grasped through the triad o
---
 virtue (virtú), 
---
ortune (
---
ortuna), and
corruption (corruzione). Virtue is that which is employed in order to counter two
threats – the element o
---
 chance introduced by 
---
ortune, and the general tendency
towards decay and corruption o
---
 institutions. There
---
ore, virtue is not a matter o
---


24 The realist challenge

possessing the excellence o
---
 character, in the Aristotelian sense o
---
 the word
(Mansﬁeld 1996: 45). Rather, it responds to the situation at hand, in particular
the power relations invested in the situation. Thus, virtue is something “alert, on
the make; it is not a habit”. Virtue is never in and o
---
 itsel
---
, but exists 
---
or the sake
o
---

---
 virtue. In response to the threat o
---
 decay and unintended
chance events, true virtue in politics is to know when to break with general
conceptions o
---
 good deeds. A 
---
ourth concept that is central to Machiavelli’s
thought is innovation (innovazione). Ultimately, The Prince is “an analytic study o
---

innovation and its consequences” (Pocock 1975: 156), inasmuch as it assesses the
possibilities and threats 
---
acing an innovative prince. The upside o
---

that it may assist virtue in imposing 
---
orm on 
---
ortune. The downside, however, is
that the introduction o
---

uncontrolled consequences in time” (156). In other words, the innovating prince
may reintroduce the perilousness o
---
 
---
ortuna. As we shall see in Chapter 4, the
disruptive potential o
---

come with unseen corrosive eﬀects.
Bruno Latour, who introduced Machiavelli above, makes a similar argument in
one o
---

---
or an abandonment o
---

---

used to include the mystery o

(Latour 2008: 6), he suggests that designers could be the harbingers o

---

---

---

the inﬂuential works o

Though not strictly a realist in the political theory sense o
---
 the word, Latour is
certainly no stranger to the classics within the realist canon. As suggested above,
Machiavelli 
---
eatured prominently in his early work on science and technology

philosopher, Hobbes was his earliest source o

(Harman 2014: 5). Harman goes on to argue that there is “a realist note that is
clearly audible throughout his political theory” (15), and cites Latour’s assertion

progressive ones” (Latour, Harman, & Erdélyi 2011: 96). Harman is care
---
ul to
stress that Latour himsel
---

realist label is problematic in relation to Latour’s work, which is also engaged in a
debate on the merits o
---

describes Latour’s de
---

be understood through re
---
erencing a transcendent truth, because how truths are

The realist challenge 25

claimed are in themselves outcomes o
---

distinguishes three phases in Latour’s political philosophy: The early Latour is
inspired by Hobbes and Machiavelli; the middle Latour draws upon Walter
Lippmann and John Dewey; and the late Latour involves an inquiry into a
distinct political mode o
---

---

---

---

rom his earlier writings.

The idealism o
---
 design thought
Given the increased interest 
---
or design to engage with and reshape social matters, it
is crucial 
---
or designers to ﬁnd ways o
---

---
or the deceptive, unpredictable,
and recalcitrant nature o
---

political theory may serve as a use
---
ul resource – especially as design thought tends
to be aligned with the alternative, idealist position. To 
---
urther explicate how ide­
alism serves as the dominant perspective o
---

Simon’s inﬂuential text on the sciences o
---
 the artiﬁcial. Indeed, a series o
---

propositions may be in
---
erred 
---
rom Simon’s o
---
t-cited idea that a designer is someone
who devises courses o
---

---
erred
ones (Simon 1996: 111).
One should not read Simon’s comment as isolated to design in a narrow sense,
but across the practices o
---

---

human intentions. In Engineering the Revolution, Ken Alder’s (1997) study o

Towards real realpolitik
At this point, the broad outlines o
---
 realism should be relatively clear. The realist
perspective avoids the “
---
air weather” approach to politics which takes political
order and stability as a given, and downplays the prevalence o
---
 conﬂicts and
contestations. It assumes that humans are selﬁsh – or, alternatively, that they dare
not act selﬂessly – and resigns to the 
---
act that in many practical aspects o
---
 social
li
---
e “might is right”. On a more abstract level, it is a concerted move away 
---
rom
the perspective o
---
 politics as a mere application o
---
 rational ethics and moral phi­
losophy. Thus, realism is interested in the brute 
---
acts o
---
 power, struggle, and
violence, thus separating the political 
---
rom the moral.
However, as hinted above, it is wrong to assume that realism is uninterested in
moral matters. For instance, Morgenthau shows how, 
---
ollowing Hobbes, the
security o
---
 the sovereign state as the 
---
undamental presupposition o
---
 moral experi­
ence. That is, the “national interest” is a 
---
undamental concern 
---
or security, and its
pursuit is indispensable to the realization o
---
 moral li
---
e (Morgenthau 1951: 33).
Moreover, realism is expressed in diﬀerent 
---
orms, which implies that there are
several ways o
---
 accounting 
---
or the place o
---
 morals. Reinhold Niebuhr’s Christian
realism is a “sober, not somber realism” with a modest 
---
aith in human progress
(Kau
---
man 1996: 314). Though the world may be a bleak place, it is ultimately 
---
aith
that impels us to engage in the struggle to make the world a better place. Michael
Williams (2005) outlines a similar approach to realism, or what he calls “wil
---
ul
realism”. It has three deﬁning 
---
eatures, the ﬁrst one being skepticism, which is a
rational attitude questioning the limits o
---
 reason as a political 
---
orce. Relationality is
the second 
---
eature, which puts emphasis on how constitution, maintenance, and
trans
---
ormation shape the relations between political orders which are not necessarily
in opposition but still guided by political principles o
---
 diﬀerence. Thirdly, power
politics is central to understanding any 
---
orm o
---
 realism, engaging with the eﬀective
mobilization and exercise o
---
 material power. This includes the clashing o
---
 multiple

---
orms o
---
 knowledge claims, 
---
orms o
---
 subjectivity, and structures o
---
 authority and

30 The realist challenge

action. Wil
---
ul realism posits that the recognition o
---
 the centrality o
---
 power in pol­
itics does not imply a reduction o
---
 politics to pure power, or to the capacity to
wield violence. It seeks, on the contrary, a politics o
---
 limits that recognizes the
destructive and productive dimensions o
---
 politics, and that maximizes its positive
possibilities while minimizing its destructive potential (Williams 2005: 7).
A positive program 
---
or action may also be 
---
ound i
---
 one ventures 
---
rom realist
political theory to the realism o
---
 the international relations discipline. In the
context o
---
 the latter, the term realpolitik tends to be associated with the unscru­
pulous statecra
---
t o
---
 Henry Kissinger. However, this use o
---
 the term is a mis­
representation o
---
 its original meaning. In 
---
act, the term was coined in the pursuit
o
---
 moral objectives, by August Ludwig von Rochau, a disillusioned liberal in
mid-nineteenth century Germany. He too had been “mugged by reality”: He
had seen the outcomes o
---
 the 
---
ailed liberal revolution o
---
 1848, and interpreted it
as a warning example o
---
 aimless enthusiasm, misused idealism, and deluded naï­
veté. For him, the revolution represented a mix o
---
 hubris and idealism, where
political romantics “dreamed up superstructures but 
---
orgot the base” (Bew 2016:
26). Crucially, von Rochau agreed with the liberal enlightenment idea that there
is no moral 
---
oundation 
---
or the proposition that might is right. However, he did
not believe that the real world o
---
 politics had changed, so as to accommodate these
new ideals. The power o
---
 the strong had been shown to be unjust, but still – as he
writes in his Grundsätze der Realpolitik (1853) – the law o
---
 power governs the world
o
---
 states just as the law o
---
 gravity governs the physical world. In practice, sovereignty
is not a right, but a result o
---
 power. It cannot be contracted out to a constitution, as
power only obeys greater power.
Given these origins o
---
 the term, historian John Bew (2016) thus argues that
realpolitik should not be understood as the crude and unscrupulous exercise o
---

power politics. “Real realpolitik”, as Bew calls it, was cra
---
ted as a practical tool 
---
or
coping with the contradictory political problems produced by modernity;
a combustion o
---
 new ideas about liberty and social order alongside rapid
industrialization, class antagonism, sectarianism, the rise o
---
 nationalism (in
both civic and ethnic 
---
orms), and increasing international rivalry.
(17)
This was the context 
---
or which realpolitik was devised, and some o
---
 the character­
istics o
---
 that time seem to resonate with today’s world. It is there
---
ore hardly surprising
that “realpolitik is back in 
---
ashion – again” (1). A
---
ter the 
---
all o
---
 the Berlin wall and the
idealism o
---
 the 1990s, history and realist politics has returned with a vengeance. In
the wake o
---
 ideological exhaustion, economic downturn, military overstretch and a
succession o
---
 crises, the “real” seems to usurp the “utopian” (3).
Still, what does realpolitik oﬀer by way o
---
 a positive program 
---
or action? First,
there is something liberating in the stark message o
---
 von Rochau. Consider the

---
ollowing: Realpolitik does not move in a 
---
oggy 
---
uture but in the present’s ﬁeld

The realist challenge 31

o
---
 vision. It does not consider its task to consist in the realization o
---
 ideas, but in
the attainment o
---
 concrete ends. And it knows, with reservations, to content itsel
---

with partial results, i
---
 their complete attainment is not achievable 
---
or the time
being. Ultimately, von Rochau argues, Realpolitik is an enemy o
---
 all kinds o
---

sel
---
-delusion.
The 
---
ocus on the here and now, the attentiveness to the matters at hand, and
the lowering o
---
 expectations may be read as a call 
---
or practical action. Indeed,
those who indulge in what von Rochau calls Ge
---
uhlspolitik (sentimental politics)
and Prinzipenpolitik (principled politics) run a considerably higher risk o
---
 becom­
ing disillusioned. Secondly, von Rochau couples his matter-o
---
-
---
actness with an
outlook that does not get carried away with trying to negate the 
---
orces o
---
 the
world. On the contrary, Realpolitik (real politics) seeks to harness them, reorient
them, and use them 
---
or one’s own purposes. This implies that the cold-headed
analysis o
---
 the 
---
orces o
---
 the world is not 
---
or analysis’ own sake. Rather, it merely
provides the groundwork 
---
or the exercising o
---

---

emerging 
---
orces. Indeed, “the 
---
orce which the state does not incorporate, will
necessarily become its opponent” (von Rochau in Bew 2016: 37). Summing up
von Rochau’s arguments, Bew (300) articulates 
---
our points that may 
---
orm a
schematic heuristic 
---
or coping with the real world:
1.
2.
3.
4.

Who holds power in any given circumstances? Note that this is distinct 
---
rom
who claims sovereignty or the right to hold power.
What are the social and economic conditions that underlie the political
system and how are they changing the distribution o
---
 power?
What is the prevailing cultural context and what are the most important
ideological undercurrents o
---
 the society in question?
Considering all these things together, what is the room aﬀorded 
---
or political
action to 
---
orward one’s interests or one’s ideals, and what are the risks?

Towards the end o
---
 this book, we will return to this heuristic, and the general
question o
---
 how to act in the world. Though much o
---

around ways o
---
 grasping the nature o
---
 power in relation to design practices, the
aim will be to translate this analysis into an assessment o
---
 the room aﬀorded 
---
or
action, ultimately prompting creative action. Indeed, realist modes o
---
 creativity
tend to be overlooked, as realists are routinely caricatured as blunt aﬃrmers o
---
 the
“might is right” slogan. Here, there are two key points. For one, a political rea­
lism may be as “innovative” as idealism; the capacity 
---
or creation and imagination
is not a purely idealist trait. As Geuss (2010) points out, one example may be how
the Bush administration invented weapons o
---
 mass destruction – producing 
---
ancy
renderings o
---
 chemical labs in trailers – in order to 
---
rame the attack on Iraq.
History may be 
---
ull o
---
 similar examples, and Geuss’ point is to put our attention
to the creative agency o
---
