about preserving the status quo; it has the capacity to break out o
---
 its bias towards

32 The realist challenge

impossibilities, and actively produce new worlds. Geuss captures this realist
imagination as a “distinct ability o
---
 practical imagination, inventiveness, or
creativity, o
---
 coming up with new possibilities” 
---
or politics that does not deny
the potency o
---
 power (Geuss 2010: 14).
Secondly, and crucially, this realist mode o
---
 creativity may be put to non-reactionary
uses. As E.R. Carr (1946) suggests, there is a tendency to associate idealism with the
visionary imagination o
---
 true utopianism. Instead, as Geuss posits in his
interpretation o
---
 Carr, it is the inherent moralism o
---
 idealism that obscures its
perspective. Thus, utopianism must be salvaged 
---
rom its artiﬁcial constructs o
---

ideal types, which are too bound to internal consistencies or aﬃnities, and
instead be given more radical political agency by becoming realist (Geuss
2016: 26
---
). We must – as Geuss suggests in his reading o
---
 Carr – start prac­
ticing a “utopian realism”, a context-bound and value-judging practice,
instead o
---
 impotent and moralization under the guise o
---
 universalism. Such
utopian realism in turn resonates well with von Rochau’s original realpolitik.
This is not a realism that is limited to preserving the current state o
---
 aﬀairs, or
the unimaginative condition o
---
 “being realistic”, not about accepting existing

---
rameworks deﬁning the possible and impossible. A sensible realist project is
“one o
---
 ‘wish
---
ul visions’ (rather than ‘wish-dreams’)” (Geuss 2016: 48). We
will return to this argument in the ﬁnal chapter.
As we will see in the coming chapters, realism oﬀers a sordid yet still imaginative
perspective on the power dynamics design ties into. Whereas designers are taught
to use design thinking to “empathize” with users, while still silently moralizing
their irrational or debased behaviors to nudge them into a more virtuous direction;
realism shi
---
ts perspective 
---
rom the Ge
---
uhlspolitik and Prinzipenpolitik o
---
 social and
humanitarian-inspired design towards the possibilities and impossibilities that back
up design initiatives with power. Our task ahead is to shed idealist blinders, and
unpack the deceptive traps laid out by popular participatory design methods.

BETRAYAL
Post-political participation

There had been months o
---

---

designers, architects, and planners had collaborated, 
---
using a multitude o
---

---

visions. A
---

citizens’ visions 
---
or the 
---
uture Gothenburg. This was good news 
---
or democracy,
and possibly also good news 
---
or the newspapers to report.
Our team, consisting o
---

on a vision that in many ways questioned the brie
---
. Not only were we making
inclusive plans 
---
or the old harbor area o
---
 Gothenburg (enough o
---

---
é
chains!) but also proposing bicycle routes over the water. We also set out to
expand what kind o
---

envisioned ﬂexible in
---
rastructure that would support pop-up bazaars, street mar­
kets and citizen-run events, and we proposed that a segment o
---
 the old harbor
should be turned into a bird habitat and wetland. Ideas o
---

plemented the ambition to unmake a bit o
---

predominant in planning.
A radical idea had become a key element in our overall proposal; could a part o
---

the area have its own constitution, a more ﬂexible program, allowing part o
---
 the area
to remain open 
---
or planning dynamics over time? Like a 
---
ree-trade zone, but 
---
or
citizen-generated initiatives instead o
---
 corporations? Governed by a non-dividend
public interest company, supplied with in
---
rastructure that could support many types
o
---
 civic activities? The whole team was very excited about the idea, and we made
sure it had a signiﬁcant presence in the public presentation.
So it came as a bit o
---

the presentations 
---
rom the diﬀerent teams. What had caught the attention o
---
 the
DOI: 10.4324/9781003281443-3

34 Betrayal

journalists and experts was that our team had suggested creating a “swamp” in the
harbor (Granath 2005: 82). How did our elaborate scheme we 
---
elt was so visionary
become reduced to mud? How did the civic-driven process o
---

become a morass o
---
 
---
ailed intentions?
This chapter starts 
---
rom an examination o
---
 existing streams o
---

participatory design (PD) tradition that invoke realist thinking, more speciﬁcally
agonistic and adversarial design. The argument explores this in relation to the realist
political theory that underpins it, and then pivots to the associated notion o
---

---

---
t is signiﬁcant – rather than

---

chapter interrogates how co-design practices may actually suppress contestability. It
thus examines how participative design and co-design – when used in the context
o
---

---

chapter accepts the existence o
---

account o
---

---

---

proposed by actor-network theory, and the theoretical notion o
---
 translation, it
charts how policy-making – no matter how participative – involves a suppression
o
---

inevitably involves a betrayal.

Agonistic and adversarial design
One o
---
 the key tensions between idealism and realism outlined in the two opening
chapters o
---
 this book concerns the possibility 
---
or – and virtue o
---
 – achieving nor­
mative or practical consensus. This tension serves as a use
---
ul point o
---
 departure 
---
or
explicating the stakes o
---
 political realism in the context o
---
 design, especially as there
is already a considerable body o
---
 work within design that draws upon the idea
that dissensus is central to politics. Indeed, over the past 
---
ew decades there have
been calls 
---
or the exposing o
---
 disagreement and so-called “agonism” in design,
and critiques o
---
 the tacit consensus sought and desired by designers. Instead, this
body o
---
 work suggests, designers must acknowledge disagreement and the
contestation o
---
 interests.
The notion o
---
 “agonism” is borrowed 
---
rom political theorist Chantal Mouﬀe, a
“leading arch-realist” (Galston 2010: 396) whose work can be understood through
the delineation between “politics” and “the political”. While the 
---
ormer implies
“the set o
---
 practices and institutions through which order is created”, the latter
denotes “the dimension o
---
 antagonism” that she sees as “constitutive to human
societies” (Mouﬀe 2005: 9). Whereas the institutionalized practices o
---
 
---
ormal poli­
tics tend to be studied by political scientists, political theorists debate the nature o
---

the political. While some theorists see politics as a domain o
---
 
---
reedom and public
deliberation, 
---
or Mouﬀe it is a space o
---
 power and conﬂict.
However, much the theorists o
---
 the mainstream “liberal” tradition wish to
believe that such disagreement can be eradicated, she represents the realist

Betrayal 35

contention that it is naïve – indeed dangerous – to believe that this is possible. I
---

dissensus is ignored or suppressed, it is bound to re-emerge in an uglier guise. For
instance, Mouﬀe consistently strives to show how political arrangements based on
centrist consensus that leaves little room 
---
or contestation tends to produce popu­
list or extremist movements. Here, there is a risk that respect
---
ul agonism – which

---
or Mouﬀe serves as the basis o
---
 a properly 
---
unctioning democracy – is usurped,
and replaced by a violent antagonism that threatens to undermine the very

---
oundation o
---
 democracy itsel
---
. So, democratic theorists and politicians must stop
“trying to design the institutions which […] would reconcile all conﬂicting
interests and values” and instead 
---
ocus on “the creation o
---
 a vibrant ‘agonistic’
public sphere o
---
 contestation where diﬀerent hegemonic political projects can be
con
---
ronted” (Mouﬀe 2005: 3).
It is this ambition that design theorists have translated into notions o
---
 agonistic
design and adversarial design. What would it mean 
---
or designers to distance them­
selves 
---
rom the ideals o
---
 a cozy consensual world, and instead base their practice on a
harsh reality o
---
 a world in disagreement? Can design disclose a world o
---
 power
asymmetries hidden 
---
rom view in seemingly consensual discourses, systems, and
material arrangements? Can it expose wrongs that are committed within such
arrangements, and put agreements to the test? Building on Mouﬀe’s agonistic
democracy, these scholars construe design as a power
---
ul and colonial tool that wields
power, which in turn needs to be questioned and resisted. An agonistic design
practice hopes to expose tensions and contradictions to highlight how society is not
based on tacit consensus – that all interested parties are agreeing, or that their inter­
relations run smoothly – and instead reveal how the designed object is a ‘thing’; an
assembly o
---
 conﬂicting parts, a product o
---
 imbalanced compromises (Björgvinsson,
Ehn & Hillgren 2012b). Building on Noortje Marres and Bruno Latour, Ehn (2008)
exposes how the thing is both an object and a parliament o
---
 diﬀerent opinions,
coming together to address the political. The thing is thus a conﬂict o
---
 
---
orces, wills,
populations, aﬀects, trajectories, and ethics. Ehn orchestrates these trajectories into a
process o
---
 contested ‘thinging.’
Similarly, design theorist Carl DiSalvo sees agonism as a central concept in his
ideas o
---
 a practice o
---
 adversarial design. This notion implies a critical design
practice that exposes inconsistencies and disagreements, and becomes a type o
---

political design, based on contestation (DiSalvo 2012: 2). This also implies that
design becomes an “inquiry into the political condition” (5). DiSalvo’s work
chimes with Mouﬀe’s point about ﬁnding ways o
---
 preventing agonism 
---
rom
slipping into outright antagonism. Design may be used as a means to generate
nonviolent discursive disagreement, which exists in a realm o
---
 the political.
Antagonism is contained through discursive agendas in contestation with each
other, even as these take physical 
---
orm in design interventions and arti
---
acts.
Indeed, the ambition o
---
 agonism-inspired design scholars can be summed up with
re
---
erence to how Evgeny Morozov (2013) introduces the work o
---
 DiSalvo.
Bluntly put, the agonistic approach can be said to represent a shi
---
t in purpose o
---


36 Betrayal

design – beyond the creation o
---
 
---
unctional arti
---
acts, design is also about creating
“conversation starters” (Morozov 2013: 329).
The agonistic critique o
---
 participatory design proposed by Björgvinsson, Ehn
and Hillgren (2012b) also revolves around the creation o
---
 a discursively oriented
space 
---
or contestation. Their approach thus 
---

---
 par­
ticipation; the selection o
---

---
or voicing
dilemmas. Their agonistic participatory design thus implies
a love towards practices where diﬀerences and controversies are allowed to
exist, and dilemmas are raised and possibilities explored. The design
researcher role becomes one o
---

---
rastructuring agonistic public spaces mainly
by 
---
acilitating the care
---
ul building o
---

---
 heterogeneous par­
ticipants, legitimizing those marginalized, maintaining network constellations,
and leaving behind repertoires o
---

conducting innovative trans
---
ormations.
(Björgvinsson, Ehn & Hillgren 2012b: 143)
These agonism-inspired design theorists have, however, been subject to criticisms.
For instance, Markussen (2012) uses Jacques Rancière’s ideas about the politics o
---

aesthetics as counterpoint in his critique o
---
 DiSalvo. So, while DiSalvo’s notion o
---

political design manages to “reveal and contest existing conﬁgurations and condi­
tions o
---
 society” (Markussen 2012: 43, italics added), this perspective neglects the
aesthetico-political ambition proposed by Rancière: How design may make new
voices audible and present new visibilities, and thus shi
---
t the power arrangement
that Rancière (2004) calls “distribution o
---
 the sensible”. This implies a “policed
order,” based on consensus and control over what is sensible, that “allocates roles
and 
---
unctions to people to keep order” (McCarthy & Wright 2015: 42). From this
perspective, Markussen argues, it is evident that the agonism-inﬂuenced design o
---

DiSalvo does not directly intervene in the composition o
---
 a given power arrange­
ment, which “mani
---
ests itsel
---
 as a struggle between two or more groups that has as
its goal a reordering o
---
 the relation o
---
 power between the existing groups” (Mar­
kussen 2012: 45). Thus, a more Rancière-oriented manner o
---
 thinking about
political design would be to imagine design that could provide “a nonviolent
unsettling o
---
 the sel
---
-evidence, with which existing systems o
---
 power can control
and restrict the un
---
olding o
---
 our everyday behavior and interaction”.
While relating to Rancière’s work, this chapter will not pursue this line o
---

critique. Rather, it will continue 
---
rom the work o
---
 Ehn and DiSalvo, but reorient
the gaze. First, it will shi
---
t 
---
ocus – 
---
rom hopes o
---

mobilized 
---
or supporting contestability, to 
---
ears o
---
 participative design being
mobilized 
---
or quelling contestability. Indeed, as hinted above, much o
---
 Mouﬀe’s
work seeks to show how contestability is suppressed, and outline the contours o
---

the “post-political” Zeitgeist that haunts contemporary liberal democracies.
Second, drawing upon actor-network theory, it will shi
---
t the attention 
---
rom the

Betrayal 37

level o
---
 the discursive realm, to a more situated exploration o
---
 the circulation o
---
 a
more general set o
---
 representations.

Post-politics
What does the term ‘post-politics’ entail? The term – which Žižek (1999: 248)
attributes to Rancière, who in turn denies ever using it (Rancière 2009: 116) –
designates a stiﬂing politics o
---
 consensus that has emerged 
---
rom the 1990s
onwards. A
---
ter the proverbial ‘end o
---
 history’, the post-politics thesis argues,
democracy has been rendered a mere chimera. In other words, the collapse o
---
 the
Soviet system resulted in ‘an internal weakening o
---
 the very democracy that was
assumed to have triumphed’ (Rancière 2004: 3). For David Harvey, the rich
world has seen the rise o
---
 a neoliberal state apparatus, which is proving increas­
ingly authoritarian. In Harvey’s (2005: 78) terms, this process implies “a radical
reconﬁguration o
---
 state institutions and practices”, where the power o
---
 the
executive tends to usurp the power o
---
 representative democracy. Civil society
organizations act as an integral part o
---
 this institutional setup, but 
---
ail to present a
countervailing power to the neoliberal state. Instead, they have to yield to man­
agerialist approaches to public administration.
This holds particularly true to the speciﬁc case o
---
 urban politics, and the struggles
associated with David Harvey’s (2008) 
---
amous claims to “the right to the city”. Erik
Swyngedouw (2005) argues that cities have become post-political spaces. The
mechanisms o
---
 such post-politics are “new governance innovations”, such as dia­
logues, consultations, and negotiations. Citizens and civil society organizations are
held hostage when they participate in such processes:
Ironically, while these technologies are o
---
ten advocated and mobilised by
NGOs and other civil organisations speaking 
---
or the disempowered or socially
excluded, these actors o
---
ten 
---
ail to see how these instruments are an integral
part o
---
 the consolidation o
---
 an imposed and authoritarian neo-liberalism.
(Swyngedouw 2005: 1998)
Thus, argues Swyngedouw, ‘“participation” is invariably mediated by “power”’,
which implies that processes o
---
 participation serve the purpose o
---
 cementing the
power o
---
 elites. Indeed, these elites seem to promote techniques o
---
 supposed codetermination, co-production and co-design as deliberate co-optive strategies.
The power o
---
 these elites tends to be exercised through the ‘rule o
---
 experts’
(Harvey 2014: 122, citing Mitchell 2002). As Žižek (1999: 198) phrases it: “In
post-politics, the conﬂict o
---
 global ideological visions … is replaced by the col­
laboration o
---
 enlightened technocrats (economists, public opinion specialists…)”.
In this way, Žižek argues, democracy is not so much “repressed” as “
---
oreclosed”.
Citizens may be invited to participate in so-called democratic processes, but the
very setup o
---
 the mechanisms o
---
 participation precludes any signiﬁcant inﬂuence.

38 Betrayal

Thus, attempts to engage citizens in politics tend not to politicize the status quo.
As Mouﬀe phrases it:
There is much talk today o
---
 ‘dialogue’ and ‘deliberation’ but what is the
meaning o
---
 such words in the political ﬁeld, i
---
 no real choice is at hand and i
---

the participants in the discussion are not able to decide between clearly di
---
­

---
erentiated alternatives?
(Mouﬀe 2005: 3)
What is the essence o
---
 this status quo? As hinted above, the idea o
---
 post-politics
stems 
---
rom a politico-economic concern with institutional 
---
ramework o
---
 neo­
liberal capitalism. Ultimately, this very system – and the ways in which it is
legitimated – represents the main culprit in the post-politics argument. So,
i
---
 the problem with today’s post-politics (‘administration o
---
 social aﬀairs’) is
that it increasingly undermines the possibility o
---
 a proper political act, this
undermining is directly due to the depoliticization o
---
 economics, to the
common acceptance o
---
 Capital and market mechanisms as neutral tools.
(Žižek 1999: 353)
To sum up, the post-politics discussion 
---
eatures at least 
---
our key propositions. First,
the present post-political situation emerges 
---
rom the rise o
---
 neoliberalism, which in turn
is the ideological outcome o
---
 the so-called “end o
---
 history”. Second, post-politics
implies the deliberate deployment o
---
 a set o
---
 “new technologies o
---
 government”
(Swyngedouw 2005: 1992–1995), which renders opposition 
---
utile against an ‘aseptic
post-political administration o
---
 public aﬀairs’ (Žižek 1999: 210). Third, this admin­
istration amounts to an expert rule, on behal
---
 o
---
 an “autocratic elite technocracy”
(Swyngedouw 2005: 2003). Fourth, the thing that post-politics ultimately under­
mines is a critique o
---
 capitalism, as it operates in symbiosis with the ideology o
---
 bourgeois
economics, which presents market solutions as natural and apolitical. Now, let’s turn to
an actual case o
---
 participative design to see how post-politics operates in practice –
the one brieﬂy introduced in the beginning o
---
 this chapter.

The agent o
---
 betrayal: Introducing the Dialogue Process in
Gothenburg
During the autumn o
---
 2005, we participated in a citizen consultation, or so-called
Dialogue Process, on the redevelopment o
---
 the harbor area in Gothenburg,
Sweden. The city in question is Sweden’s second largest, with a population o
---

approximately hal
---
 a million. Like many other harbor cities, Gothenburg was hit
by the dock crisis during the 1980s, and large areas on the water
---
ront have been
exploited and become attractive housing and oﬃce areas. In order to 
---
acilitate this
process, the municipality started a company in the 1990s, Älvstranden AB, which

Betrayal 39

led the redevelopment o
---
 the harbor area. This company also launched the
ambitious six-month Dialogue Process, meant to generate “visions” 
---
or the 
---
uture
development 
---
or the area.
As members o
---
 one out o
---
 six teams – all consisting o
---
 designers, architects, and
citizens – we partook in “participatory urban planning” or “partnership planning”
(c
---
. Hamdi 1991; Hamdi & Goethert 1997). The originator o
---
 the process (
---
rom
here on re
---
erred to as “the Dialogue Process”) was a municipal company, which
sought to generate a harmonious negotiation between citizens and developers.
However, as the process had drawn to a close, we – along with other partici­
pants – were struggling to ﬁnd traces o
---
 our input in the ﬁnal outcomes o
---
 the
process. Moreover, we 
---
ailed to politicize the development o
---
 the area: Our input
did not contribute to the opening o
---
 a dissensus regarding the 
---
uture o
---
 the
harbor (Mouﬀe 2005). In the end, we were reluctantly resigned to the 
---
act that
very little was at stake in this process o
---
 urban co-design.
The 
---
ailings o
---
 the Dialogue Process have been discussed by sociologist Catharina
Thörn (2008). She too was involved in the dialogue, and describes how the radical
proposals presented by her team were ignored, as these propositions implied “a
politicisation o
---
 the process”. Ironically, such a politicization was not “possible to
handle within the 
---
ramework o
---
 the political-administrative discourse” (65). Thörn
thus suggests that
the 
---
act that the proposal was dismissed because it was too political laid bare
the conditions related to the discourse o
---
 city politics, and the unquestioned
values they contain.
(66)
Drawing upon the post-politics argument sketched above, Thörn suggests that the
unquestioned values are, in essence, the neoliberal mode o
---
 urban development.
Where does that leave us, the designers and academics participating in the
process? We were intent on deepening democracy in the context o
---
 urban design,
but to what degree did we – as Swyngedouw suggests – become hostages in the
process? To what degree did we become complicit, even co-conspirators, in it?
Did we betray our own interests, or the interests o
---
 the wider public? One way to
speak about how betrayals may inﬂuence co-design processes is to invoke the
terminology 
---
rom the power games o
---
 WWII. Participants in co-design processes
can be imagined as collaborateurs, a term that denotes an agency that collaborates
with invading powers against the interest o
---
 the resisting population. Whereas
some studies o
---
 WWII 
---
ocus on the explicit 
---
orms o
---
 collaboration (c
---
. Gordon
1980), Rings (1982) diﬀerentiates between several modes o
---
 collaboration along a
continuum: On one extreme, there is “unconditional collaboration”, that is,
collaborating with the 
---
ormer enemy 
---
or what is seen as a common cause; on the
other, there is “tactical collaboration”, where a minimal collaboration is actually a
cover 
---
or purpose
---
ul resistance. There is also a stronger term, “Quisling”, which is

40 Betrayal

used to re
---
er to radical betrayal, a person who collaborates in the 
---
ormer sense, an
arch-collaborateur engaging in a “collusion with the enemy be
---
ore invasion, as
well as a
---
ter” (Davies 2004: 94).
A key concern here is to study the precise mechanisms by which participants in
co-design processes betray the interests o
---
 their peers. Who is the culprit in the
story o
---
 the Dialogue Process? Traditionally, the post-politics literature would

---
ocus on the normal cast o
---
 power
---
ul actors – the technocratic public oﬃcial, the
scheming politician, and ultimately the corporate managers that serve as the
incarnation o
---
 capital. However, the remainder o
---

---
 post-political outcomes o
---

participative design processes that 
---
ocuses on the design aspects o
---
 betrayal. As we
shall see, actor-network theory allows us to highlight an unexpected culprit in the
story o
---
 the Dialogue Process – as it turns out, the role o
---
 the “Quisling” is played
by a non-human. It should thus be noted that we use the term in an abstract
manner, where the meaning o
---
 the term is detached 
---
rom the original Vidkun
Quisling. In other words, this argument is about agencies o
---
 betrayal, not about a
historical person’s relation to National Socialism. Along with the claim that the
Quisling agency is non-human, we will 
---
urthermore argue that it is deceptive to
point to a distinct, pre-existing power structure to which co-design participants
capitulate. The identity o
---
 the Quisling agency can simply not be captured through
standard social-scientiﬁc models o
---
 (human) agency. Again, actor-network theory
(ANT) provides an alternative and complementary view o
---
 how post-politics
operates in practice.

Actor-network theory on betrayal
Approaching the Dialogue Process 
---
rom an ANT perspective 
---
orces us to
reorient the scope o
---
 the analysis. Indeed, in Reassembling the Social, Bruno
Latour posits that “the ﬁrst source o
---
 uncertainty” 
---
or any scholar wishing to do
ANT relates to the radical agnosticism regarding group 
---
ormation (Latour 2005:
29). In other words, an analysis o
---
 the Dialogue Process cannot start 
---
rom a
preexisting social-scientiﬁc account o
---
 the supposed role o
---
 capital, labor, and
the state in a stylized account o
---
 the “neoliberal state”. This is a reﬂection o
---

ANT’s proposition o
---
 “radical indeterminacy o
---
 the actor” (Callon 1999: 181),
and the idea that interests are merely “temporarily stabilised outcomes o
---
 pre­
vious processes o
---
 enrolment” (Callon & Law 1982: 662). It also reﬂects the
Garﬁnkelian heritage (c
---
. Latour 2013: 6), which postulates that the concepts o
---

the actors are allowed to be stronger than those o
---
 the analysts (Latour 2005:
30). So, while the “sociologist o
---
 the social” may proceed 
---
rom general cate­
gories, “imposing some order be
---
orehand” (23), the “sociologist o
---
 associations”
must pay closer attention to the situation at hand – in our case the Dialogue
Process – and conduct an open-ended examination o
---
 the modes by which
groups are assembled.

Betrayal 41

Another key source o
---
 divergence 
---
rom the post-politics discussion relates to the
role o
---
 ideology critique: ANT precludes an easy delineation between scientiﬁc 
---
act
and ideological delusion (Latour 2004). Thus, items one and 
---
our on the list o
---

propositions 
---
rom the post-politics discussion have to be omitted 
---
rom the analysis:
The inquiry may not start 
---
rom the proposition that we live in a neoliberalism
borne 
---
rom the idea o
---
 the end o
---
 history, nor can it depart 
---
rom an a priori
assumption that a supposedly “bourgeois” economics produces post-political eﬀects.
An ANT analysis is nevertheless amenable 
---
or explaining how processes like the
Dialogue Process may prove democratically deﬁcient. Latour (2005: 22) locates a
second source o
---
 uncertainty in the nature o
---
 actions; “in each course o
---
 action a
great variety o
---
 agents seem to barge in and displace the original goals”. This is
demonstrated in Latour’s (1999: 80–112) description o
---
 how the work o
---
 physicist
Frédéric Joliot is propelled 
---
orward by aligning his interests with those o
---
 the
French state, more speciﬁcally the National Centre 
---
or Scientiﬁc Research. Where
Joliot’s goal is to be the ﬁrst to master a nuclear chain reaction, the state’s repre­
sentative, Raoul Dautry, has another goal – to secure national independence in the

---
ace o
---
 
---
oreign military threats. By aligning their interest, they 
---
orm an alliance
around a new composite goal: To establish a nuclear physics laboratory. Thus, in
such “translation” o
---
 goals, there is always “a dri
---
t, a slippage, a displacement” (88).
As shown in Michel Callon’s (1986) classic study on scallops, ﬁshermen, and
researchers in St Brieuc Bay, such translation processes are ri
---
e with betrayals. This
chapter will suggest that when participating in co-design processes, there is a risk
that one’s goals are being betrayed or translated away through successive enrol­
ments – though the bene
---
actors o
---
 this process can never be known in advance.
Indeed, Latour (2013) re
---
ers to translation as “translation/betrayal” (see 
---
or
instance 341), and the notion o
---
 translation as betrayal is ultimately an ontological
position. All representations are betrayals o
---
 the thing represented, and thus “the
idea that translation is also a betrayal is built into the charter o
---
 actor-network
theory (i
---
 we may allow ourselves to imagine that it has a charter)” (Law 1997).
There is also a Machiavellian aspect o
---
 translation. In his book on Latour, The
Prince o
---
 Networks, Graham Harman has stated that “
---
or Latour every actor is a Joliot,
a medium o
---
 translation able to link the most 
---
ar-ﬂung objects” (Harman 2009:
102). Susan Leigh Star 
---
amously questioned such a “managerial and entrepre­
neurial model o
---
 actor networks” (Star 1991: 26). By 
---
ocusing on success
---
ul
Joliots, it neglects “the world o
---
 the non-enrolled” (49), and may ultimately cause
researches to side with the power
---
ul. While not speciﬁcally studying those who
are altogether Other to such enrollments, this argument shares an interest in all
those actors who are not success
---
ul Joliots. Indeed, some people may become
losers in the process o
---
 translation, causing their original objectives to be sub­
stantially marginalized. More speciﬁcally, when it comes to co-designed urban
planning, there is a ﬂip side to the translation o
---
 goals, in which participating
citizens and civil society groups may see their original goals becoming diluted in
the process o
---
 goal translation.

42 Betrayal

An ANT perspective would not, however, present such dismal outcomes as
preordained. The Machiavelli-inspired, micro-oriented conception o
---
 power pro­
posed by ANT (Callon 1986; Latour 1988; Clegg 1989: 203) suggests that there
the proverbial “powers that be” may not always control citizens through the new
modes o
---
 participatory governance. It would, there
---
ore, pace Žižek, be misleading
to state that democracy is always “
---
oreclosed” in processes o
---
 participation – that is
determined by process o
---
 translation. This aspect o
---
 ANT also places a question
mark over items two and three on the list o
---
 post-politics propositions: One cannot
assume that technocratic elites will always be 
---
ully in control o
---
 the outcomes
o
---
 co-design, and that this is a deliberate strategy on behal
---
 o
---
 the powers that
be. Moreover, as Callon (1991: 143) insists, that there is no such thing as
“translation ‘in general”’. The ANT researcher must pay close attention to the
material media in which such translations o
---
 goals are “inscribed: roundtable
discussions, public declarations, texts, technical objects, embodied skills”, and
so on. Hence, in the next section, this chapter will analyze the co-design
process by studying such inscriptions.
This leads us to a 
---
urther key point o
---
 divergence 
---
rom the post-politics
approach that stems 
---
rom the third source o
---
 uncertainty – that o
---
 the nature o
---

objects. Accepting that “objects too have agency” (Latour 2005: 63), 
---
orces the
analyst o
---
 co-design processes to interrogate the role played in the paraphernalia
o
---
 arti
---
acts that 
---
eature in such processes. Indeed, these arti
---
acts may prove inﬂu­
ential in the democratic deﬁcit o
---
 co-design. As a preliminary illustration o
---
 how
such a deﬁcit emerges, one may compare the ANT approach – and our 
---
ocus on
translation and betrayal – with Rancière’s notion o
---
 “mésentente”, which is closely
aligned with the above-mentioned notion o
---
 “the distribution o
---
 the sensible”.
For Rancière, democracy is intimately tied to this French term, which means
“quarrel, disagreement”, but also “the 
---
act o
---
 not hearing, o
---
 not understanding”
(Rancière 2004: 5). Democracy means breaking down the delineation between
the reasonable “voice” articulated by the patrician, on the one hand, and the
incomprehensible “noise” coming 
---
rom the plebeian, on the other. This chapter
too deals with the problematic o
---
 how co-design processes cause some voices to
be treated as “noise”, causing them to be ﬁltered out. However, whereas Ran­
cière’s argument comes 
---
rom a context o
---
 pre-existing social divisions among
humans – that is, masters and slaves – our argument is more interested in how
non-human entities contribute to this very sorting operation. In other words,
our inquiry is into the role o
---
 the quasi-objects as protagonists in this story.
More speciﬁcally, we are interested in the quasi-objects that cause voices to be
interpreted as noise.
Here, we will borrow the term “protocol” 
---
rom media theory. On a basic level, a
communications protocol is a system o
---
 rules that regulate data exchange, the most

---
amous being the TCP/IP which governs the internet. Alexander Galloway, how­
ever, deﬁnes it more abstractly as “a technique 
---
or achieving voluntary regulation
within a contingent environment” (Galloway 2004: 7). As such, protocols are not

Betrayal 43

power-neutral, as they produce “a distributed management system that allows con­
trol to exist within a heterogeneous material milieu”. Thus, protocol “is to control
societies as the panopticon is to disciplinary societies” (13). Thus, though these
re
---
erences point towards Deleuze (1988) and Foucault (1977) rather than ANT, the
notion o
---
 protocol is nevertheless use
---
ul 
---
or the purposes o
---
 this chapter. Protocol
allows us to explore mundane and practical modes o
---
 co-design interaction as arti­

---
actual modes o
---
 ordering – more speciﬁcally 
---
or sorting out noise 
---
rom voice. Cru­
cially, it allows us to do so while retaining the ANT proposition that groups are to be
studied as emerging rather than as predeﬁned categories o
---
 “the social”. (See the ﬁrst
source o
---
 uncertainty, discussed above.) It also allows 
---
or a depiction o
---
 this control as
an interlacing between humans and non-humans, with a close attention to quasiobjects – to objects with agency. Thus, we may speak o
---
 the protocol as an agent o
---

betrayal, and the assemblage o
---
 citizens and arti
---
acts as an “assembly o
---

In the next section, we will make use o
---
 this conceptual apparatus when analyzing
the empirical case o
---
 the Dialogue Process.

Translation and betrayal in the Gothenburg Dialogue Process
The team that we participated in, “Team Rhizom”, endeavored to assume what
Thorpe and Gamman re
---
er to as a “responsive” role. This “assumes that as a
designer you do what you can, working with other social actors to leverage the
community’s assets to eﬀect positive change, but do not seek to take it upon
yoursel
---
 to try to do it all, 
---
or other social actors” (Thorpe & Gamman 2011:
221). Yet, in the end, the 
---
ormat o
---
 quasi-subjects (the co-design participants) and
quasi-objects that constituted the protocol (the material 
---
ormats 
---
or processing the
communication o
---
 development proposals) turned the team into an assembly o
---

collaborateurs.
The process o
---
 becoming collaborateurs was not evident at the time. During the
initial design process, the Rhizom team generated a series o
---
 “visions” that indeed
seemed to represent “outside the box” thinking. One key idea was that urban
planning is about more than the physical mani
---
estation o
---
 the built environment;
the team wanted to introduce more abstract propositions, such as experimental
governance arrangements, and new economic models that would sa
---
eguard social
justice. These ideas got ﬁltered out as the proposal was rendered into the draw­
ings and plans that were to be communicated to politicians, the city planning
oﬃce, and the public. To state that this ﬁltering was related to a supposed neo­
liberal consensus, or by bourgeois ideology, would be to simpli
---
y this process.
Again, the outcome can be accounted 
---
or by paying close attention to the pro­
tocols o
---
 participation. It is here, by highlighting the Machiavellian micropower
o
---
 the protocols, that the ANT perspective may complement the post-politics
argument.
In other words, when tracing how post-political outcomes emerge, the devil
really is in the details. However, be
---
ore delving into the speciﬁcs o
---
 this protocol,

44 Betrayal

we may ﬁrst make a general point regarding the modus operandi o
---
 protocols. As
noted by Galloway, “Protocol is not a superego (like the police); instead it always
operates at the level o
---
 desire, at the level o
---
 ‘what we want”’ (Galloway 2004:
241). In the case o
---
 our urban co-design process, this subtle 
---
orm o
---
 power is
mani
---
ested in the 
---
ollowing manner: I
---
 the process is conceived as a chain o
---

sequential data exchanges, each link is governed by desires o
---
 the receiver, implying
that the non-desire is treated as noise. In what 
---
ollows, we will ﬁrst describe the set
o
---
 protocols that governed the design process o
---
 the team, sorting out voice 
---
rom
noise, and then 
---
ocus on the protocols that guided outside audiences’ processing o
---

the design proposal. We will do so by examining the inscriptions (Latour &
Woolgar 1986: 45–53) generated within the design process, thus construing the
protocol as something that guides the chain o
---
 successive inscriptions.
The design process started oﬀ 
---
rom the generation o
---
 inscriptions in the 
---
orm
o
---

---

Rhizom 2005). The Post-it note-generating brainstorming session constitutes a
prime example o
---

---
 
---
ea­
tures a 
---
orm o
---

---

---

avoid negative ideas or to criticize the ideas o
---
 others. “De
---
er judgment” is the
ﬁrst rule o
---
 brainstorming, as design agency IDEO suggests; “creative spaces don’t
judge” (OpenIDEO 2011). Negative notes stiﬂe the creative process, and thus a
word o
---
 warning may be “ﬁltered” out 
---
rom the process and a person in the team
who is critical is seen as “negative” in the group and marginalized. Though not
deployed in our team during the Dialogue Process, the Appreciative Inquiry
method is another technique that may produce similar outcomes, 
---
ocusing on
appreciation rather than critique (Grant & Humphries 2006).
Some o
---

sessions were elaborated upon through inscriptions such as word documents and
emails. This material dealt at length with the more abstract ideas mentioned
above, such as re
---
ormed governance structures o

---
or
the planned area. It also included microﬁnance 
---
or local entrepreneurship, as well

---
or enabling spontaneous and small-scale activation o

(though events, 
---

---

---
 the area
being developed on the principle o

trajectory would be subject to constant revising, and housing programs would not
be set in stone. These ideas were, however, severely truncated as they 
---
ed into the
next step in the chain o
---

had to be translated into the visualizable. This designerly mode o
---

an obligatory step in communicating the internal work o
---

audiences – that is, the wider public, as well as the municipal planning pro
---
es­
sionals. In other words, the team management is not to be blamed 
---
or the

Betrayal 45

ﬁltering o

operation, the planners respond that they can only cater to “the views that are directly

---
or
the 
---
uture development o
---

In conclusion, we may also point to the limited techno-managerial investment o
---

the team, which made the protocols so eﬀective in treating more radical ideas as

---

---
or politically challenging the design brie
---
. As designers, our belie

would automatically be “good” (and not corrupted), led the team to become

---

by design. As constituent elements o
---

Analyzing the Dialogue Process
There are three themes that emerge 
---
rom our encounter with the Dialogue
Process, and 
---
rom the attempt to deploy ANT as a means to account 
---
or the
potential democratic deﬁcits o
---
 co-design. First, we will speci
---
y what we mean
when we speak o
---
 a democratic deﬁciency in methods o
---
 participation. Second,
we will outline the merits o
---
 introducing ANT in this case, juxtaposing it with
the type o
---
 critique presented by the “post-politics” discussion. Third, we will
expand on the beneﬁts and perils o
---
 using the term “protocol”.

Betrayal 47

First, the issue o
---
 democratic deﬁcits. In this chapter, we have tried to move
beyond critical accounts that tend to dismiss all kinds o
---
 engagement with the
world o
---
 
---
ormal politics. (Note Mouﬀe’s above-mentioned distinction between
politics and the political.) There
---
ore, we must be clear that terms like “democratic
deﬁciencies” must be used against a pre-understanding o
---
 the inevitable 
---
act that

---
ormal politics must be “anti-political”. Formal politics is about reducing a plethora
o
---
 dissonant voices into one consensus, thus 
---
acilitating legitimate decision-making.
In Political Machines, Andrew Barry writes: “Indeed, it is a 
---
eature o
---
 parliamentary
party politics (with its concern 
---
or party discipline, 
---
or instance) that it will have
anti-political elements. […] To say this is not to denounce party politics or to draw
a 
---
alse opposition between the anti-political nature o
---
 party politics and the political
character o
---
 alternative 
---
orms o
---
 direct action” (Barry 2001: 195). Our concern in
this chapter, though, is the manner in which this consensus is reached. The expo­
sition o
---
 translations and dri
---
t presented above suggests that the material 
---
orms o
---

participation – that is, the protocols o
---
 co-design – 
---
acilitate a consensus-making
that systematically ﬁlters out certain types o
---
 voices.
Second, as stated above, there are 
---
orms o
---
 Machiavellian micropower struggles
that may be exercised in co-design processes. These 
---
orms o
---
 power, some critics
o
---
 the process suggested, need to be addressed in 
---
urther detail. One o
---
 the great
beneﬁts o
---
 ANT is that its very mode o
---
 operation starts 
---
rom a detailed study o
---

these very 
---
orms o
---
 micropower, not 
---
rom preexisting social “structures” o
---

power. (Remember, as highlighted in the theoretical sections on post-politics and
ANT, this is one o
---
 the main themes o
---
 doing actor-network research.) Another
beneﬁt is the 
---
ocus on the mundane use o
---
 arti
---
acts that invariably inﬂuences
agency. In this way, it puts the spotlight on entities that might otherwise escape
our attention. Having experienced a democratically deﬁcient process o
---
 partici­
pative planning, one tends to search 
---
or culprits among human actors – the
power-hungry politician, the boring bureaucrat who turns politics into adminis­
tration, or some other villain. ANT, instead, renders other problematics visible –
in our case, the problem o
---
 democratic deﬁcits seems to be related to the material
modes o
---
 collaboration that we call protocols.
By pointing to such new “culprits”, ANT may shi
---
t the debate on participation.
Indeed, the post-politics argument may create an unproductive schism between the
pro
---
essions involved in urban planning and design, on the one hand, and the critics
o
---
 these pro
---
essions. Here, it is worth bearing in mind that diﬀerent theories are more
or less use
---
ul in particular contexts. Whereas ANT was use
---
ul 
---
or analyzing demo­
cratic deﬁciencies in our case, the post-politics argument seems apt 
---
or capturing
Thörn’s (2008) experience. Nevertheless, the ANT position, as operationalized in
this chapter, suggests that some o
---
 the democratic deﬁciencies o
---
 co-design may be
assuaged by a reengineering o
---
 the protocols o
---
 collaboration.
This leads us to the third and ﬁnal point, about the notion o
---
 “protocol”. We
have previously used this term 
---
or reimagining design management: Protocols
may be engineered to 
---
acilitate decentralized modes o
---
 co-design that do not

48 Betrayal

require top-down regulation (Palmås & von Busch 2011). However, as this
chapter argues, the notion o
---
 protocol ought to be understood as a non-norma­
tive, neutral term. Organizations or processes governed by protocols are not
necessarily “good”. The existence o
---
 a protocol merely suggests that the organi­
zation or process takes place in an open setting, and is based on voluntary colla­
boration. However, 
---
or this very reason – that protocols can be engineered in
ways that both liberate and restrain – we believe that they should be at the heart
o
---
 the discussion on co-design.
It is our conclusion that in processes like these, as designers, we need to
remind ourselves o
---
 three points. First, it shi
---
ted 
---
rom the prescriptive hopes o
---

agonistic design – that design may be mobilized to 
---
acilitate dissensus – to a
description o
---
 how participative design may quell such dissensus. Second,
having made this point with re
---
erence to the literature on the post-political
condition, it introduced ANT as an alternative mode o
---
 understanding the postpolitical outcomes o
---
 participative design. This, in turn, implied that our
description 
---
ocused on the material modes o
---
 co-design that produced the postpolitical outcomes. Using the term translation rather than Rancière’s mésentente
implied a concomitant reorientation away 
---
rom studying the discursive realm, to
a 
---
ocus on a more general circulation o
---
 representations. It also implied that we
substituted the post-politics authors’ notion o
---
 power – which tends to 
---
ocus on
hegemonic ideologies – to a Latour-in
---
used Machiavellian understanding o
---

power. This, in turn, privileges the micro-political struggles o
---
 machines as well
as machinations.
There is, however, one 
---
undamental point that comes out 
---
rom the ANT’s
notion o
---
 translation, and that is the inevitability o
---
 betrayal. Again, all repre­
sentations are betrayals o
---
 the thing represented, which complicates the idea that
participative design may accurately represent the wills and dreams o
---
 the partici­
pating actors. Indeed, it may be both the strength and weakness o
---
 design to be
disentangled 
---
rom the political conﬂicts it tries to address, but as sociologist
Robert Nisbet (1953) posits: “rootless men always betray”. The concluding
chapters (6 and 7) will reengage with this issue – how can participative design
learn to live with the inevitability o
---
 betrayal?
In summary, this chapter has shown that co-design processes invariably reﬂect
the tension between the political, on the one hand, and politics, on the other.
While the 
---
ormer necessitates the emergence o
---
 a dissensus, the latter implies a
process o
---
 turning dissensus into consensus. While there are those who argue that
co-design processes tend to be post-political charades that merely disguise the 
---
act
that decisions tend to be made by technocratic elites, the chapter has construed
the process o
---
 co-design as one o
---
 translation. By drawing on this actor-network
theory, it has shown how such a process o
---
 translation can be traced, through the
material modes o
---
 collaboration. More generally, the notion o
---
 translation denotes
a process o
---
 enrolment o
---
 diverse interests, which implies that objectives dri
---
t over
time. In other words, interests will invariably be betrayed.

Betrayal 49

The next chapter will explore another aspect o
---
 how design objectives may be
challenged by social processes. As such, it will move 
---
rom inevitable betrayal o
---

participants’ interests in co-design practices, to studying how the original inten­
tions o
---
 socially oriented institutional designs may be betrayed.

4
CORRUPTION
Design and decay

In the introduction to this book, we told a story about the protests o
---
 the Global
Justice Movement. However, 
---
rom a design perspective, the 
---
ate o
---
 the key policy o
---

the movement is even more interesting. In Fast Policy: Experimental Statecra
---
t at the
Thresholds o
---
 Neoliberalism, Jamie Peck and Nik Theodore (2015) tell the story o
---
 the
cruel 
---
ate o
---
 the movement’s pet policy: Participatory budgeting (PB). Though 
---
or­
mally a contribution to the ﬁeld o
---
 critical policy studies, Fast Policy is also a study o
---

how designs – speciﬁcally policy designs that deviate 
---
rom mainstream political
models – tend to decay over time. Thus, they claim that “(original) ‘designs’ rarely
hold” as “the institutional and technical character o
---
 policies evolves, mutates, and
o
---
ten degrades as ‘travel’ occurs” (Peck & Theodore 2015: xx). The case o
---
 PB shows
how such decay can play out: The hope
---
ul experiment o
---
 the anti-Davos activists
degraded into becoming a standard 
---
eature in the Davos establishment’s toolbox.
How did this happen?
This chapter 
---
ollows the discussion 
---
rom the previous chapter: Here, too, we
are interested in processes o
---
 dri
---
t, co-option, and betrayal. However, there is a
key diﬀerence: The previous chapter studied the inevitable betrayal o
---
 partici­
pants’ interests in co-design practices; this chapter will 
---
ocus on how the original
intentions o
---
 socially oriented institutional designs may be eroded and betrayed.
That process o
---
 corruption is not, we shall argue, inevitable. However, in order
to prevent such corruption, idealist inclinations o
---
 designers must be tempered
with realist intuitions. Such realism ought to involve a wariness towards the
capacity o
---
 economic incentives and other social 
---
orces to corrode the original
aims o
---
 social designs.
The argument runs as 
---
ollows: A
---
ter completing the story about participatory

Another world is possible!
During the ﬁrst 
---
ew years into the new millennium, the Global Justice (or
“anti-globalization”, or “alter-globalization”) Movement was at its prime. It was
propelled by the idea that a world beyond the neoliberal status quo was possi­
ble, and sought political inspiration 
---
rom the World Social Forum – an annual
meeting ﬁrst held in Porto Alegre in 2001. In their 
---
oreword to the edited
volume titled Another World is Possible, Michael Hardt and Antonio Negri
(2003) describe the signiﬁcance o
---

---
orum:
The World Social Forum in Porto Alegre has already become a myth, one o
---

those positive myths that deﬁne our political compass. It is the representation
o
---

a new intellectual nomadism, a great movement o
---

the positive elements o
---

---
rom the beginning as a great network to bring the members o
---

Workers’ Party (PT) together with the ‘globalization’ protest movements,
the local administrators experimenting in new 
---
orms o
---

democracy together with the utopian schemers o
---

(Hardt & Negri 2003: xvi)
Among the “new 
---
orms o
---

Alegre, there was one idea and practice that stood out as the star o
---

that o
---

collectively decide how to allocate part o
---

---
unds to certain projects
deemed urgent by the community, instead o
---

politicians or oﬃcial bureaucrats. In various 
---

cities worldwide, o

---

o

---
rom social service programs to
children’s councils (Lerner 2014).

52 Corruption

We will soon

This development is ironic, given the

As we shall see below, this very slippery connectivity and

The travels o
---
 participatory budgeting
The story starts in 1980s Brazil, in which democratization had contributed to an
accelerating participation in voluntary organizations, especially among the poor.
This, in turn, can be placed in a longer tradition o
---
 popular education, liberation
theology, associationalism, and “sundry precursors to what would later be known
as horizontalidad” (Peck & Theodore 2015: 149). In the 1990s, the idea o
---
 parti­
cipatory budgeting was the key program promoted by Brazil’s Workers’ Party,
which was then in government in the Municipality o
---

gram implied a deep participation o
---
 local, grassroots association in diﬀerent
phases o
---
 the preparation o
---
 the budget. This, in turn, created a process which
paid particular attention to the redistributive aspects o
---
 budgeting and public
investments (148).
More speciﬁcally, scholars have pointed to ﬁve beneﬁcial outcomes o
---
 PB, as
operationalized in Porto Alegre. (155) First, citizens – particularly the poor –
were mobilized to a great degree: Over the course o
---

---
 thousands
across the city participated in the budgeting. Second, the civic sphere was ener­
gized through the emergence o
---

Corruption 53

quality o
---
 the provision o
---
 in
---
rastructure and other public services increased. Fourth,
clientelism and corruption decreased, by virtue o
---

---

priority setting and budget allocation. Last, neighborhood-level decision-making
implied new spending priorities that 
---

socio-spatial redistribution.
These outcomes were noted by the nascent global justice movement in the
rich Global North. For a movement struggling to decolonize its modes o

---

However, this hope would sometimes underestimate the site-speciﬁc aspects o

Later, as the PT lost power in the municipality, the practice o

Meanwhile, on an international level, the early 2000s saw considerable eﬀorts

Social Forum (WSF) served as a conduit

more in line with the post-PT version o

Framed in this way, PB no longer seems like a policy design that truly chal­

Resisting corrosive environments
Peck and Theodore’s account raises questions about whether there was something
inherently problematic with the PB design. Was there a design 
---
ault that made it
lose its integrity? Indeed, diﬀerent designs carry diﬀerent aﬀordances – they can
be more or less susceptible to the “corrosive eﬀects” o
---
 the political environment.
On the other hand, the political environment itsel
---
 may be more or less corrosive.
A tendency towards degradation is not inevitable, partly because the politics that
surround these traveling designs is “complex, multidirectional, and unpredictable”.
Thus, Peck and Theodore advocate a position o
---

political 
---
oreclosure” (xxi).
As hinted above, one reason

---

Against disruption: Mantenere lo stato
So 
---
ar, we have encountered several ways o
---
 describing design projects whose
trajectories have shi
---
ted as they have moved through the social world. The pre­
vious chapter spoke the language o
---

---
t”, and this chapter has
used the language o
---

“recuperation”. These contemporary ways o
---
 labeling design 
---
ailures can be
placed alongside the language o
---

vant-turned-political theorist who was active some ﬁve hundred years ago.
As already mentioned in Chapter 2, Machiavelli did not say much about design
as such, but he did discuss innovation. Indeed, as John Pocock (1972, 1975)
suggests, Machiavelli’s The Prince is not only a manual 
---
or how to gain and retain
power – it is also a book about innovation. This concern goes beyond the o
---
trepeated quote about the perils o
---
 innovation – “that there is nothing more di
---
­
ﬁcult to take in hand, more perilous to conduct, or more uncertain in its success,
than to take the lead in the introduction o
---
 a new order o
---
 things”. Rather, the
question o
---

---
 the argument, to be placed alongside the
more (in)
---
amous treatment o
---
 power.
As noted earlier, one must understand The Prince through the triad o
---
 virtú,

---
ortuna, and corruzione (Pocock 1975: viii; von Busch & Palmås 2016: 286). The
ﬁrst term, virtú, suggests a connection to the term “virtue”, but in the case o
---

Machiavelli, one must be cautious with that connotation. As we shall see later,
The Prince is a satire o
---
 previous accounts o
---
 princely virtues. At this point, it
suﬃces to say that virtú is that which a prince employs in order to counter two
threats. The ﬁrst o
---
 the threats is the element o
---
 chance introduced by 
---
ortuna, the
second is the general tendency towards institutional decay; corruzione.
This tendency towards decay is central to Machiavelli’s worldview. Writing in
the context o
---
 a republic under strain, his inclination was to 
---
avor stability over
what he saw as its alternative – some unknown 
---
orm o
---
 despotism. Machiavelli
suspected that – in contrast to the D:Ream hit and subsequent New Labour
anthem – things can only get worse. He was in a mindset in which the phrase
“another world is possible” constitutes a threat rather than a promise. Thus, the
actions o
---
 the prince should 
---
ollow one overriding concern: How to maintain the
state o
---
 things; mantenere lo stato. This sentiment can be transposed onto Papanek’s
triangle: Designers trained to 
---
ocus on “desired ends” and “pre
---
erred situations”
may lose sight o
---
 all the undesired outcomes that their “programs” and “courses

Corruption 57

Better

Worse

FIGURE 4.1 The better or worse problem.

o
---
 action” may generate. While design interventions must always strive 
---
or a
better world, designers must equally be care
---
ul not to recklessly leave the world
in a worse situation than they 
---
ound it.
Here, in relation to 
---
ortuna and corruzione, innovation (innovazione) enters as a

---
ourth concept. This is what the prince can deploy as a means to restore order in
a turbulent world. As Benoit Godin points out:
To Machiavelli, innovation serves to stabilize, not to revolutionize. The
world is changing constantly, hence the need 
---
or innovation to stabilize
it. There is need to stabilize a ruler’s power and secure it through inno­
vation […] In contrast to modern theorists, the world is too stable and
needs innovation to change it, even revolutionize it.
(Godin 2015: 69)
Machiavelli is thus a healthy antidote to what Jill Lepore (2014) has called “the
gospel” around the “disruptive” innovation. For Lepore, a historian, the concept
o
---
 disruption says something 
---
undamental about our time: Just as “every age has a
theory o
---
 rising and 
---
alling, o
---

---

“also has a theory about the past and the present, o
---

notion o
---
 time”. Thus, disruption is construed as the benevolent 
---
orce that gen­
erates progress. However, she also points out that it is a ﬂawed concept that rests
on “shaky evidence”. Stemming 
---
rom the work o
---
 Clayton Christensen (1997),

58 Corruption

and 
---
rom a particular understanding o
---
 the work o
---
 Schumpeter, it is a relatively
novel conception o
---
 innovation. Prior to Schumpeter’s casting o
---
 innovation as the
creation o
---
 new products and services, Lepore argues, the term had chieﬂy negative
connotations. The pre-schumpeterian approach to innovation associated it with
“excessive novelty, without purpose or end”, and Machiavelli can productively be
placed in that lineage.
Strangely enough, Peck and Theodore’s discussion on PB makes use o
---
 the
contemporary meaning o
---

originally operationalized by the Worker Party in Porto Alegre, is disruptive
inasmuch as it constitutes a break with the status quo. However, this idea sits
uneasily with their main lesson 
---
rom the 
---
ailure o
---
 PB. Again, alternative
designs are necessary but not suﬃcient, as the corrosive eﬀects o
---

environment cause designs to decay. Creating a disruption is one thing, sus­
taining it is another. Let’s review another example o
---
 this problematic, be
---
ore
concluding the chapter by reverting to Machiavelli.

Microﬁnance: The quintessential social innovation
Much like in the case o
---
 PB, there is a 
---
ounding myth – an origin story – that
animated the phenomenal propagation o
---
 the practice in the 1980s and 1990s.
This origin story starts in 1976 in Bangladesh, when Muhammad Yunus 
---
ounds
Grameen Bank. In the introduction to his bestselling biography Banker o
---
 the Poor,
Yunus explains how the idea 
---
or the bank emerged when researching and
teaching economics at Chittagong University:
The poor taught me an entirely new economics. I learned about the pro­
blems that they 
---
ace 
---
rom their own perspective. I tried a great number o
---

things. Some worked. Others did not. One that worked well was to oﬀer
people tiny loans 
---
or sel
---
-employment. These loans provided a starting point

---
or cottage industries and other income-generating activities that used the
skills the borrowers already had. […] I never imagined that my micro-lend­
ing program would be the basis 
---
or a nationwide ‘bank 
---
or the poor’ serving
2.5 million people or that it would be adapted in more than one hundred
countries spanning ﬁve continents.
(Yunus 1999: ix–x)
But that it did. Indeed, microﬁnance became the policy instrument that preceded
PB as the “big idea” in development circles. The idea can be described as 
---
ollows:
Microﬁnance involves practices related to providing underserved communities
with ﬁnancial services. Most notably, these practices have been deployed in lowincome economies, and in relation to poorer clients, who would otherwise not
have access to ﬁnance. Thus, microﬁnance has been hailed as a means to mitigate
the exclusion o
---
 these individuals 
---
rom the ﬂows o
---
 credit, enabling them to

Corruption 59

become sel
---
-suﬃcient while at the same time generating development 
---
or the
economies they operate in. The credit tends to be provided in the 
---
orm o
---
 small
loans, so-called microloans. Microﬁnance institutions diﬀer 
---
rom traditional banks by

---
ocusing on providing ﬁnancial services 
---
or these communities, which – given that
they tend to lack collateral, steady employment, and veriﬁable credit histories – are
unlikely prospective recipients o
---
 traditional loans. Instead o
---
 relying on collateral in
the 
---
orm o
---
 real assets, the banks instead rely on social 
---
orms o
---
 collateral – such as an
individual’s membership in borrower groups and other social networks – in order to
assess the creditworthiness o
---
 clients and to ensure the repayment o
---
 the loans.
Indeed, one o
---
 the main innovations o
---
 the pioneers o
---
 microﬁnance is to develop
the speciﬁc banking practices that enable microﬁnance institutions to deviate 
---
rom
the rules o
---
 lending usually adhered to by traditional banks.
One o
---
 the concepts that made microﬁnance such a popular idea was that o
---

social innovation. In the rhetoric on social innovation that emerged in the 2000s,
microﬁnance – and the origin story o
---
 Yunus and Grameen Bank – was billed as
“the quintessential social innovation” (Phills et al. 2008: 36). In the 2010s, scholars
ﬂocked around the study o
---
 societal innovation processes, with publishers scram­
bling to put out major edited volumes that promote the concept (Franz et al. 2012;
Nicholls & Murdock 2012; Moulaert et al. 2013). In many ways, the academic
study o
---
 social innovation built upon previous work on social entrepreneurship
(Leadbeater 1997; Nicholls 2006), and there are signiﬁcant overlaps between the
two literatures.
During the 2010s, microﬁnance also became the go-to example that policymakers
used when introducing the notion o
---
 social innovation (European Commission
2013a: 34). Arguably, the strong interest in social innovation partly stems 
---
rom the

---
act that the public sector – notably the European Commission – threw its weight
behind the concept. Indeed, social innovation can be understood as a “quasi con­
cept” (European Commission 2013b; McNeill 2006); though it may be 
---
ound
wanting in its analytical and empirical 
---
oundations, it nevertheless provides a dis­
cursive space in which academics and policy makers can meet. Thus, some o
---
 the
handbooks and guides to the concept are published by public bodies and think tanks
(Murray et al. 2010; European Commission 2013a). The Commission’s intervention
on the topic is use
---
ul, as it describes social innovations as “innovations that are social
in both their ends and their means”. “Social means” implies that the innovation is a
process o
---
 co-creation in a social or collective manner, involving a set o
---
 stakeholders.
“Social ends”, on the other hand, implies that social innovation produces socially
beneﬁcial outcomes.
As we shall see, these two objectives – achieving “social ends” through “social
means” – do not always align with each other. Though there are many proponents
o
---
 “social practice” or “social process” – not least within the design pro
---
ession – the
deployment o
---
 such design processes does not necessarily produce socially beneﬁcial
outcomes. Indeed, the leveraging o
---

---
oreseen
negative societal outcomes. In reviewing the case o
---

60 Corruption

the rhetoric that posits that socially beneﬁcial results will

rom socially

Social innovation in design
The imperative o
---

have the conﬂicts and politics involved in such collaborative endeavors,
involving users (c
---

Björgvinsson, Ehn & Hillgren 2012a). The use o

---
or actors who wish
to highlight the ethical quality o
---

oriented, and sometimes the social o
---

arti
---

---
or “real world cases” with,

---

Dunne and Raby, however, explicitly distance their endeavor

---
or the humanitarian

by Architecture 
---

with glossy commodities. Indeed, it seems designers are ﬁnally heeding Papanek’s

How, then, do social design and social innovation relate to one another?
Design theorist Ezio Manzini lays out a delineation between the two concepts,
suggesting that social design primarily addresses particularly problematic social
situations, o
---
ten through a charity 
---

market and the state 
---

on the other hand, aims to produce “solutions based on new social 
---
orms and
economic models” (Manzini 2015: 64). However, as Manzini acknowledges, the
distinction between the two gets more blurred,

Corruption 61

social design is increasingly oriented toward social innovation, recognizing
that this oﬀers the only chance 
---
or solving the problems it traditionally deals

---
or social innovation, 
---
acing the extensions o
---

nomic crisis, is more and more 
---
requently involved in initiatives that involve
socially sensitive issues.
(Manzini 2015: 65)
The movement Manzini addresses is also reﬂected in how entrepreneurs and business
are turning extreme social situations, not least poverty, into new markets at the
“bottom o
---

---

Such events involve designers and programmers working on solutions

Social innovation also tends to be discussed in relation to how creativity or

the UK-based design ﬁrm Participle proposes that social innovation implies a shi

---

Indeed, Leadbeater’s (1997) landmark The Rise o

---
urther

---
orms o
---

Social capital, a term 
---

---

key components 
---
or building local community and democratic 
---

---

to be a wholly benevolent 
---

62 Corruption

and “trust” can ob

---

---

---
or better governance may also enable organized

As Gilbert suggests, one must diﬀerentiate between the “group-speciﬁc” social

---

This insight – that there is a dark side o

In order to discuss these problematics in 
---

Anti-social innovation
There is a large and diverse literature on microﬁnance, in which some authors laud
the innovation, while others criticize it sharply. In order to disturb the notion that
social processes produce socially beneﬁcial outcomes, the three sections that now

---
ollow will 
---
ocus on some o
---
 the more critical voices on microﬁnance. In so doing,
it will point to three problematics o
---
 this social innovation, outlining the anti-social,
anti-political and anti-inventive eﬀects produced by its spread across the world.
Among the numerous 
---
orms o
---
 critique directed at microﬁnance, some relate
to economic matters. For instance, economists have argued that the economies o
---

scale generated by large business entities are much more eﬃcient than micro­

Corruption 63

entrepreneurship (c
---
. Karani 2007). A larger employer creates jobs and increases
worker productivity, with a higher possibility o
---
 sustaining business and employ­
ment. A larger employer also adds more to the overall development o
---
 social and
physical in
---
rastructure than the smaller entrepreneurs. Another 
---
orm o
---
 critique
stems 
---
rom the 
---
act that microloans may be used to “smooth consumption” –
tiding a borrower over in times o
---

---
 the loan
to make non-business expenditures, even purchases on status items, as opposed to
the investments that the loan is supposed to be used 
---
or. A third 
---
orm o
---

economic critique argues that the mechanisms o
---

ladder towards the ones already wealthy, exchanging non-monetized assets into
more debt 
---
or the poor.
However, the literature also points to a problematic that emerges 
---
rom the 
---
act
that – as mentioned above – this innovation draws upon social means. More
speciﬁcally, this problematic emanates 
---
rom the proposition o
---
 leveraging social
capital as a substitute 
---
or collateral when ﬁnancial means and real assets are scarce.
Critics argue that this idea can become corrupted to the point where bonds o
---

trust and other modes o
---
 reciprocity are being exploited 
---
or monetary gain. This,
in turn, allows the above-mentioned “dark side” o
---

sur
---
ace. By cutting into the social capital o
---

microﬁnance may stretch social commitments, undermining the very commu­
nities that they are supposed to support. Thus, the leveraging o
---
 “social” processes
produces anti-social eﬀects. This obviously jars with the ambition o
---

tions to leverage the reservoir o
---

---

networks – in order to enhance it, not deplete it.
How does this breakdown o
---

---
orms o
---
 col­
lateral may be created by a joint liability among the poor. In such arrangements,
anthropologist Aminur Rahman explains, “the group as a whole accepts
accountability 
---
or repaying the individual loans o
---

1999: 78). In this way, the group-lending scheme generates “social collateral”; a

---
orm o
---
 “organized social pressure 
---
rom group members 
---
or its microcredit
extension program”. This social aspect o
---
 the system cleverly uses social dynamics
among the poor to reassure the repayment o
---
 the loans, to “punish one person by
using one’s own people”. In these cases, where borrowers take responsibility 
---
or
recovering the bank’s investment, tensions may arise:
The collection o
---
 instalments in the [microﬁnance] centre by using the bor­
rowers creates 
---
euds among peers. They become hostile to each other, but
the bank loses nothing and gets back its invested loans.
(Rahman 1999: 80)
Critics have also pointed to a gender-related aspect o
---

crucial – but not necessarily deserving – role in the leveraging o
---

and this is acknowledged by the issuers o
---

64 Corruption

(2006) reports on how a district-level oﬃcial o

---

Women cannot go anywhere, they can be located easily; they cannot run away

eel shame
more quickly and consider non-repayment as a betrayal o
---
 
---
amily honour.
(Sharma 2006: 18)
“Group-lending” or collective liability thus tends to be distributed in small
communities o
---

---

---

inasmuch as the costs associated with asset reviews, project monitoring, and
repayment en
---

---
. Armendariz 2005). Still, even though the loan
de
---

---

microcredit banks) (Epstein & Smith 2007). The microloans are supposed to 
---

In this way, Rahman (1999) notes, the common narrative o

pattern o
---

women are used as hostages between the bank and the patriarchal men:
Most women borrowers are not the direct bene
---
actors o
---

to them. Instead, these women appear to be mediators between their male

---

the village society and the positional vulnerability o
---

passive) 
---
or timely repayment and distribution o
---

(Rahman 1999: 23)
To Rahman, this process o

---
orm o
---
 
---
reedom. Here,

---

---

---
orm o
---

---
emale

Corruption 65

submission. As Rahman sees it, through microcredits patriarchy is intertwined

---

---

In a similar vein, while the main narrative o

Critics argue that it is exactly this social mechanism that is the asset-turned-capital

---

---
all behind realistically 
---

---

munities, as the social capital is drained, and the meager income gained by the
newly 
---
