Anti-political innovation
Whereas much o
---
 the criticism leveled at microﬁnance 
---
ocuses on the anti-social
outcomes, there are also anti-political outcomes. Here, we are 
---
ollowing Andrew
Barry’s (2001: 268
---
) delineation between “the political” and “the anti-political”.
According to this distinction, an act or phenomenon is political i
---
 it renders “a pro­
blem or object […] open to contestation and dissensus”. Conversely, an act or phe­
nomenon is anti-political i
---
 it closes down the space 
---
or contestation. For instance,
the case o
---
 PB outlined above showed how the original policy design opened a space

---
or discussing new ways o
---
 governing – another world is possible! – but this space
gradually closed down as PB was neutralized and placed in the World Bank toolbox.
Similarly, critics argue that the microﬁnance phenomenon has created a stiﬂing
consensus on the development debate, ultimately yielding anti-political eﬀects.
Microﬁnance has scarcely – even in the early days – been promoted as an
anti-capitalist endeavor. Nevertheless, some o
---
 the initial hopes 
---
or microﬁnance
were connected to hopes about how the civil society sector, or the so-called
“social economy” or “alternative economy”, would claim a larger space within
the economy as a whole. Indeed, in the early days, Muhammad Yunus’ Gram­
een Bank was run as a non-proﬁt. As it turned out, the spread o
---
 microﬁnance
has buttressed the position o
---
 neoliberal capitalism as the dominant mode o
---

economic governance.

66 Corruption

In order to unpack the claim that microﬁnance has had anti-political eﬀects,
we may start 
---
rom the proposition that the phenomenal spread o
---
 microﬁnance
has come to buttress Muhammad Yunus’ assertion that “all people are entrepre­
neurs”. On that basis, the 2006 Nobel Peace prize laureate argues that credit
should be seen as a “human right”, and the recognition o
---
 such a right would
play a “strategic role in removing hunger 
---
rom the world” (Yunus 1999: 143).
This perspective is embraced not only by policy makers, private sector repre­
sentatives and non-governmental organizations (NGOs) – some artists also
endorse this idea. For instance, the inﬂuential Danish group Superﬂex, draws on
this idea when mixing art, development, and entrepreneurial thinking into a 
---
orm
o
---
 social art practice (Nacking 1999).
However, this consensus has displaced alternative propositions on how to deal
with world poverty. By 
---
ragmenting initiative and responsibility onto individuals,
micro-solutions may render larger political and social change more unlikely.
Feminist economists Susan Feiner and Drucilla Barker write:
The key to understanding why Grameen Bank 
---
ounder and CEO Muham­
mad Yunus won the Nobel Peace Prize lies in the current 
---
ascination with
individualistic myths o
---
 wealth and poverty. Many policy makers believe that
poverty is “simply” a problem o
---
 individual behaviour. By rejecting the
notion that poverty has structural causes, they deny the need 
---
or collective
responses. […] For neoliberals the solution to poverty is getting the poor to
work harder, get educated, have 
---
ewer children, and act more responsibly.
(Feiner & Barker 2006)
Feiner and Barker thus suggest that neoliberals are active proponents o
---
 microﬁnance
programs because they “do not change the structural conditions o
---
 globalization”, by
which they include “loss o
---
 land rights, privatization o
---
 essential public services, or
cutbacks in health and education spending”. For Feiner and Barker, these structural
conditions are the true 
---
actors that reproduce poverty among women in developing
nations. However, the microﬁnance phenomenon has contributed to a political
consensus that tends to overlook such 
---
actors.
Such anti-political eﬀects have already been discussed in the context o
---
 parti­
cipative design. Cooke and Kothari (2001) point to the problems that arise 
---
rom
the idea that i
---
 the designer can just improve the participative nature o
---
 design
practices, such practices will get better and 
---
orm the basis 
---
or “best practice”.
However, Cooke and Kothari suggest that there is a tyrannical potential in this
tendency, as the 
---
ocus on “how the practitioner operates” or on “the speciﬁcities
o
---
 the techniques and tools employed […] embodies the potential 
---
or an unjus­
tiﬁed exercise o
---
 power” (4). Thus, participation has the tendency to mask the
delegation o
---
 power and continued centralization – all in the name o
---
 “democ­
racy” and decentralization. This, in turn, resonates well with philosopher Jacques
Rancière’s ideas on the current state o
---
 democracy. He posits that the type o
---


Corruption 67

participatory regime we normally re
---
er to as “democracy” merely concerns the
tiny space le
---
t unoccupied by the dominant order (1995). For Rancière, then,
democratic participation has become disarmed, so as to deal with minor or
even illusionary choices in matters where citizens do not threaten power.
Thus, participation masks our inability to change the ruling order.
Cooke and Kothari there
---
ore suggest that practitioners o
---
 participatory methods
need to ask themselves some important questions: “Do participatory 
---
acilitators
override existing legitimate decision-making processes?”, “Do group dynamics lead
to participatory decisions that rein
---
orce the interests o
---
 the already power
---
ul?” and
“Have participatory methods driven out others which have advantages participation
cannot provide?” (7
---
). Thus, we need to be more care
---
ul about
the naivety o
---
 assumptions about the authenticity o
---
 motivations and beha­
viour in participatory processes; how the language o
---
 empowerment masks
real concern 
---
or managerialist eﬀectiveness; the quasi-religious associations o
---

participatory rhetoric and practice; and how an emphasis on the micro level
o
---
 intervention can obscure, and indeed sustain, broader macro-level
inequalities and injustice.
(Cooke & Kothari 2001: 14)
In the participation regime, the conceptual tools o
---
 development have been
re
---
ormulated in a way that aligns it with the tenets o
---
 microﬁnance: Action has
become an individual responsibility, and empowerment has lost its political con­
notation 
---
or a struggle 
---
or real power. In both cases, the answer to marginalization
has become a question between perceived 
---
ree and equal individuals, placing the
locus o
---
 political debate 
---
ar away 
---
rom more structural issues.

Anti-inventive innovation
As a third problematic, one may also point to the anti-inventive eﬀects o
---
 microﬁnance. Again, we may draw upon Barry (2001), who posits that the dichotomy
inventive/anti-inventive deals with “the degree to which a technological or poli­
tical change opens up the space o
---
 possibility” (268
---
). For instance, a certain tech­
nology may be anti-inventive i
---
 it “displaces or blocks oﬀ other [technical]
possibilities” – note 
---
or instance how the internal combustion engine blocked oﬀ
the development o
---
 electric automobiles during most o
---
 the twentieth century. In a
similar 
---
ashion, the massive spread o
---
 economic practices and techniques associated
with microﬁnance can be said to have blocked the emergence o
---
 alternative eco­
nomic practices. As such, the outcomes o
---
 the microﬁnance innovations run
counter to the very promise o
---
 innovation itsel
---
 – the idea that innovation always
opens up new prospects 
---
or the 
---
uture.
In recent years, microﬁnance providers have sought to provide business train­
ing along with the ﬁnancial solutions oﬀered to clients (Karlan & Valdivia 2011).

68 Corruption

Some providers account 
---
or this development by re
---
erencing the above-men­
tioned issue o
---
 clients using the microloans 
---
or non-business expenditures. The
Swedish industrialist-turned-microﬁnance philanthropist Percy Barnevik explains:
Un
---
ortunately, too much o
---
 the micro-credits have been spent on personal
consumption, and that does not raise the standard o
---
 living. […] Microﬁnance
is, in itsel
---
, not a solution to the poverty problem, unless it is combined with
comprehensive training.
(Barnevik cited in Palmås 2011: 59)
For this type o
---

---

in a larger project: To teach the recipients o
---
 loans to think in more businesslike
terms, and to 
---
orge an economic mindset among the poor. This economic
mindset is meant to correspond to the classic models o
---

mechanism as the thing that balances supply, demand, and prices; the rational,
sel
---
-interested, utility-maximizing homo economicus as the pre
---
erred model o
---

human subjectivity. The business training oﬀered to the poor serves the purpose
o
---

---
 the market economy, and to supply the receivers o
---

microﬁnance with the tools and techniques that accompany it: Elementary
know-how on marketing, or on business ﬁnance. Through this operation, a
replica o
---
 the classic market model 
---
or governing economies is put in place,
instilled in the minds o
---
 the poor, but also hardwired into the tools that 
---
acilitate
economic action.
While this may not necessarily be a bad thing – well-
---
unctioning market
arrangements can deliver excellent results – this aspect o
---
 microﬁnance invariably
implies that the possibility 
---
or alternative modes o
---
 economic li
---
e is being blocked
oﬀ. Indeed, there is a politics involved in the spread and adoption o
---
 the tech­
nologies that shape the economy. Callon (2005) brings this type o
---

---
ore, and suggest that radical scholars that want to change how the economy
works ought to study the “devices intended 
---
or all Davids dreaming o
---

Goliaths” (Callon 2005: 12). Researchers, he argues, can assist in the experi­

eﬀort to pave the way towards radically diﬀerent ways o
---

li
---
e. Indeed, given that the economic arrangements based on sel
---
-interest 
---
ollow
a European lineage (Hirschman 1977), one may argue that alternative arrange­

---

microﬁnance currently seems to have the opposite eﬀect, cementing the mode
o
---

---
e that prevails in the richer parts o
---

social innovation like microﬁnance may have eﬀects that are anti-inventive.
Though innovation is invariably associated with the creation o
---

equally – paradoxically – buttress the old.
So, to recapitulate, we have seen how the microﬁnance case sheds light on
three problematics that social designers and innovators must relate to: Social

Corruption 69

innovations may produce anti-social eﬀects (corroding social capital, generating

---
rictions); anti-political outcomes (displacing debates on political alternatives); and
anti-inventive outcomes (suspending the development o
---

---

economic li
---
e).
At this point, it is important to note that Yunus does not seem to endorse this
aspect o
---

contemporary capitalism is promoting a narrow-minded and sel
---
-interested

The biggest ﬂaw in our existing theory o
---

representation o
---

---

human beings engaged in business are portrayed as one-dimensional beings
whose only mission is to maximize proﬁt. Humans supposedly pursue this
economic goal in a single-minded 
---
ashion. This is a badly distorted picture o
---

a human being.
(Yunus 2010: xv)
Yunus’ remarks suggest that the most recent mutation o
---

eﬀects that run counter to the original aims o
---

he can be placed alongside the PB innovators in Porto Alegre, and the hackers
that Söderberg and Maxigas study. Again, innovations are, by their very nature,
unpredictable, and original intentions and objectives may well become displaced
as they meet the corrosive eﬀect o
---
 the socio-political environment. This takes
us back to the Machiavellian problematic raised earlier in the chapter: How can
designers maintain the integrity o
---

mantenere lo stato?

Virtú and values in social design
We are now in a position to return to what Machiavelli may teach designers
that engage with social ends and means. As the reader may recall, we put the
question o
---
 the real meaning o
---
 virtú aside, simply stating that it is the quality o
---

the prince who manages to keep the twin threats o
---
 
---
ortuna and corruzione at bay.
Success in statecra
---
t is, Machiavelli points out, a matter o
---
 luck – or lack thereo
---
.
Hence, virtú is the capacity to get lucky, and to prepare 
---
or cruel twists o
---
 
---
ate.
It is also the capacity to deal with the general tendency 
---
or decay. Still,
Machiavelli does not provide a detailed list o
---
 the characteristics o
---
 such virtú,
and this is because he is making a negative argument. Indeed, the chie
---
 con­
tribution o
---
 The Prince – and the thing that has made the book in
---
amous – is his
description o
---
 what virtú is not.
As pointed out by Quentin Skinner, Machiavelli writes in the context o
---
 an
already existing literature on princely virtues, and this literature essentially extol­
led that the cardinal virtues should guide princely conduct. Midway through The

70 Corruption

Prince, Machiavelli satirizes elements o
---

sense o
---
 being generous) and clemency (in the sense o
---
 being merci
---
ul). While
accepting these virtues as worthy, he nevertheless argues that in practice, no
prince has ever accomplished anything by 
---
ollowing them in a slavish manner.
On the contrary, such virtues are likely to get in the way o
---
 producing good
social outcomes. This, in turn, is due to his bleak view o
---
 the social world:
Those who “in all respects, will carry out only [their] pro
---
essions o
---
 good, will
be apt to be ruined amongst so many who are evil”. In order to mantenere lo
stato, one “must learn to be not always good, but to be so or not as necessity
may require”.
In other words, Machiavelli presents a dark view o
---
 politics and society –
one that had previously not been presented. This, in turn, became a 
---
ounding
principle o
---
 the realist school o
---

book sought to shi
---
t the debate on statecra
---
t 
---
rom a previous preoccupation
with intentions, to instead 
---
ocus on political outcomes. In so doing, the book
challenged the Christianity-based ediﬁce upon which polities had been built.
That being said, Machiavelli’s position still represents a challenge to the
design discipline. I
---
 we turn to current debates on values in design, they still
tend to 
---
ocus on intentions. Note 
---
or instance the interest in Value Sensitive
Design (Friedman & Henry 2019), and in how to devise prescriptive design
methods which moderate the values o
---
 participants, while also actively instilling
human values into a design process. Here, we may also note how the notion o
---

intentionality is central to contemporary interpretations o
---
 design thinking
(Buchanan 1992; Nelson & Stolterman 2012).
In summary, this chapter has reviewed two cases o
---

alternatively, two social designs – that both promised to remake society. Both
o
---

---
ailures, as they did
not maintain the integrity o
---
 the original design, meaning that the original
objectives were displaced. As such, they 
---
ell victim to the corrosive eﬀects o
---

the socio-political environment. The chapter argued that Machiavelli’s ideas
on statecra
---
t and innovation are use
---
ul as means to make sense out o
---

processes. Moreover, the argument showed that in the case o
---

quintessential “social innovation” – good intentions ended up generating socially
detrimental outcomes. Thus, the chapter pointed to how Machavelli’s 
---
ocus on
social outcomes, rather than intentions, may be used as a means to counter the
tendency 
---
or the social design discussion to privilege intentions over outcomes.
Machiavelli is notorious 
---
or his supposed amorality. Here, though, it is worth
distinguishing between personal morals, on the one hand, and the morals o
---
 the
sovereign, on the other. As we shall see in the discussion about hypocrisy in
Chapter 6, political theorists have suggested there are good causes 
---
or not con­
ﬂating the morals o
---
 a person with that o
---
 a polity. This, in turn, leads to the
question o
---

---
 the designer – is the designer just another
person, or should the designer be understood more as a sovereign? While

Corruption 71

designers o
---
ten see themselves as the 
---
ormer – as the designer on the side o
---

people – one may equally suggest that design has come to play an instrumental
part o
---
 statecra
---
t. The next chapter will thus problematize the notion o
---

designer who stands on the side o
---
 the weak, against power structures. This will
put us in a better position to ﬁnd a new ethical position o
---

with a realist mode o
---

Empathy is a trap
“How do you know i

The proposition that “empathy is a trap” is, some may have realized, a nod to

According to Foucault, the trap means that once subjected to a ﬁeld o

Another 
---
ootnote to Plato

As Benedict Singleton has suggestively observed in his 2014 dissertation, On

---

---

Singleton argues that mêtis – not poiesis, nor techne- – is the term that best

---

rom.
However, Singleton is not alone in seeing the connection between design and

the term. “Design” can be understood as a scheme, plot, concoction or simula­

ignoble. Like so many other thematics in Western thought, this trope owes a lot

It is commonly held that Plato, in the texts Republic and Laws, lays the 
---

Mêtis sits uneasily within such a program. The Greeks construed it as a cunning
intelligence which could prove eﬃcacious in relation to means. However, it was

This juxtaposition between a per

---

designs, plots and schemes, concocted and played out in the mundane world o
---

---

---

world o
---
 
---
orms. As such, it is precisely the kind o
---

quintessential to the understanding o
---

So, let’s return to the government practice o
---

---

---

relationship between designer and user sympathetic, but the trap is still laid. Aﬀect
is the bait. It is the honeytrap bringing designers and users together, luring them
into shi

---
rom – the propensities o

The art o
---
 the strong
Again, one o

---
rom Singleton’s work is that

---

Cunning 79

great degree, this is because Plato removed this mode o

However, the concept o

---

Scott’s Seeing as the State, in which mêtis represents an adaptive, context-based

---

---

---

The most

However, in his hugely inﬂuential The Practice o

the tactics o

---

In other words, while Plato removed mêtis

---

---

---

This implosion o

recent developments within urban design – incidentally the context in which he
introduced the schema. Here, the trend has been 
---

---

---

For one, there are concerns that the interest in temporary use in the cities o
---

---

---

policy embraces temporary use, it may relegate previously in

Along with these general and sweeping concerns, it is worth examining the ways
in which such tactical urban design projects incorporate elements o
---

---

---

For instance, the notion o

and strategy – is sourced 
---

the calculation (or manipulation) or power relationships that becomes possible
as soon as a subject with will and power (a business, an army, a city, a scientiﬁc
institution) can be isolated. It postulates a place that can be delimited as its own
and serve as the base 
---
rom which relations with an exteriority composed o
---

targets or threats (customers or competitors, enemies, the country surrounding
the city, objectives and objects o
---

(36)
Conversely, a tactic is “a calculated action determined by the absence o
---

locus” (37), which implies that the “space o
---

---

Here, the original idea is that in a city, strategy is projected onto places proper,
be they controlled by municipalities or private organizations. Tactics are then
employed by the citizens, who are sneakily making do in the space o
---

However, in the case in which the service design-inspired government is probing
grandma’s needs regarding a hearing aid, it is the strong who is per
---
orming tactics
in the “proper place” o
---

her personal cognitive space has to be charted. Empathy is a trap, because it
constitutes a tactics 
---

---

In aﬀective proximity, power-plays disappear in the 
---

Sandström suggests that urban prototyping represents “a more general tendency

Taking its cue

So, what urban design shares with the lean start-up scene is the proﬁciency in

This turn to tactics in urban design has a number o

---

enrolls civil society organizations in urban development. Second, Harvey pointed to
the “speculative construction o
---

speculative element in urban governance also entails the kinds o

However, the comparison with Harvey’s work on cities leads us on to the

---

---

Neuroliberal experiments
As hinted above, the tendency to chart and mobilize the aﬀective realm stretches
beyond urban design, to the point where the use o
---
 design-in
---
ormed experiments
in public governance has been dubbed “the new spirit o
---
 policy-making” (Kim­
bell & Bailey 2017). Thus, design is not only deployed as a practical means to
improve and streamline wel
---
are provision, as in the case o
---
 grandma’s hearing aid.
It has come to in
---
orm the very modus operandi by which states govern. Here,
one could point to the introduction o
---
 various 
---
orms o
---
 nudging in government.
However, nudging practices – though phenomenally success
---
ul in terms o
---
 their
proli
---
eration across the world – need to be understood in the context o
---
 a broader
shi
---
t in statecra
---
t.
Among other things, the neoliberal project entailed wholesale attempts to
privatize assets, marketize relations, and incentivize individuals. The aim was to
secure social coordination and achieve policy goals through the harnessing o
---

actors’ supposed rational sel
---
-interest. However, the current position o
---
 this
mode o
---
 governing has been called into question. There are, o
---
 course, a
number o
---
 critiques o
---
 neoliberalism, put 
---
orward by those who are ideologically
opposed to such policies. Arguably, there is a growing recognition that negative
outcomes o
---
 neoliberal policies, such as rising personal debt or climate change, may
need to be addressed in a more state-interventionist manner. Thus the allure o
---
 the
“libertarian paternalist” aspects o
---
 nudging, which allow 
---
or more interventionism,
while staying clear 
---
rom harder, more politically unpalatable 
---
orms o
---
 regulation.
However, the waning inﬂuence o
---
 neoliberalism also stems 
---
rom the 
---
act that the
neoliberal account o
---
 human nature has 
---
allen out o
---
 
---
avor. Famously, behavioral
economists have long argued that in actual, everyday situations, people tend to not
act rationally – placing a huge question mark over any kind o
---
 neoliberal policy
re
---
orm. Given these developments, Rhys Jones and Mark Whitehead suggest,
we may be witnessing somewhat o
---
 an existential crisis among state practitioners
as they come to realise, belatedly, that they have lost their capacity to know,
predict, control and discipline their populations and economies in eﬀective and
politically acceptable ways. A behaviourally in
---
ormed and experimentally

Cunning 85

predisposed state is said to provide a technocratic and seemingly neutral solution
to these conundrums.
(Jones & Whitehead 2018: 326)
The governing principle o
---
 this behaviorally in
---
ormed and experimentally predis­
posed state is neuroliberalism. As suggested above, this may be understood as the
successor to the neoliberal project – as a reaction to perceived shortcomings o
---

neoliberalism. However, more 
---
undamentally, it rejects the neoliberal under­
standing o
---
 humans as rational market actors, and suggests that human behavior is
composed o
---
 a much more varied set o
---
 rational and “irrational” drivers. As such, it
deliberately targets and mobilizes “the desire lines o
---
 human behavior”, including
inertia, herd instincts, emotional responses, and short-term thinking. Again, this pits
it against neoliberal policies, which eﬀectively set out to suppress these very irra­
tionalities (Whitehead et al. 2017: 25). In short, neuroliberalism entails an
increasing capacity o
---
 states, corporations, and non-governmental organizations
to govern through a series o
---
 more-than-rational registers o
---
 human action
(including habits, heuristics, emotions, aﬀects, and social and environmental
contexts), and to skil
---
ully 
---
use behavioural power with liberal notions o
---


---
reedom.
(Whitehead et al. 2019: 633)
This ambition is made possible by the introduction o
---
 design approaches in the
process o
---
 governing. I
---
 previous modes o
---
 governance 
---
ocused on changing
minds, thus promoting a certain kind o
---
 rationality, neuroliberalism is a matter o
---

changing the context in which decisions are made. This shi
---
t in emphasis has
been borrowed 
---
rom the ﬁeld o
---
 cognitive design. So, while behavioral econo­
mists demonstrated predictable 
---
orms o
---
 irrationality hardwired into our brains,
cognitive designers like Donald Norman showed how our ergonomic environ­
ments can shape our behaviors (66). So, a designerly approach to thinking about
human action is at the heart o
---
 neuroliberal modes o
---
 governing.
This link between design and the experimental state can be demonstrated by
studying the case o
---
 “Experimental Finland”: A high-visibility initiative to promote
an experimental culture in Finnish government, rolled out by Prime Minister Juha
Sipilä’s Government during its 2015–2019 term. Early on during the initiative, the
vision o
---
 a design-in
---
ormed experimenting state was set out in reports such as
“Place to Experiment! Finland on the road to an experimentation society” (2013)
and “Design 
---
or Government: Human-centric governance” (2015). The latter
report proposed an “operating model” 
---
or how to run experimentation programs,
suggesting that the Sipilä Government should devise a two-year window to run
one or several such programs.
The Design 
---
or Government operating model implied a three-stage approach:
First, understanding the policy design problem, second, running an experiment,

86 Cunning

and third, evaluating the outcomes. During the ﬁrst stage, the “design o
---
 the
experiment would ﬁrst concentrate on building empathy and broad under­
standing o
---
 the problem by using qualitative methods and design thinking”. The
later phases would “emphasise measurability o
---
 its impact”, and a “thorough
evaluation o
---
 its results”. In order 
---
or this to work, the proposed operating model
speciﬁed that experiments would be run by external parties who had experience

---
rom design and behavioral science.
The ﬂagship experiment that was implemented on the basis o
---
 the Design 
---
or
Government operating model was the much-publicized basic income trial.
Implemented during a two-year period – between January 2017 and December
2018 – the experiment caught the attention o
---
 international observers, pre­
sumably because it 
---
used utopianism with scientiﬁc rigor. It was a mandatory
state-led endeavor, involving 2,000 randomly selected unemployed individuals.
The selected persons received a monthly basic income o
---
 560 Euros 
---
rom the
National Social Insurance Institution (Kela). In true RCT (randomized controlled
trial) 
---
orm, a control group was set up, consisting o
---
 178,000 citizens who
received regular unemployment beneﬁts.
As described by Mona Mannevuo (2019) the trial was positioned somewhat
uneasily between being a project about well-being and health projects, while also
aiming to create stronger work incentives, encouraging citizens to take responsi­
bility 
---
or their own lives. The design-in
---
ormed experiment thus set out to explore
a key question that tends to re-emerge in any discussion about the idea o
---
 a basic
income: How do unemployed individuals react to receiving a basic income, in
which the amount is equivalent to the regular unemployment beneﬁt, but the
income is unconditional, does not require means testing, and is not reduced 
---
or
earned income?
As it turned out, the results 
---
rom the experiment were inconclusive, at least as
regards unemployment outcomes. This is partly due to the 
---
act that midway
through the experiment, the government introduced a new activation policy.
However, it seems the basic income recipients enjoyed better economic well-being
than the control group, which received the traditional unemployment beneﬁt. It
seems they also grew more trusting o
---
 institutions and o
---
 other people. Still, the
breakthrough insight that emerged 
---
rom this experiment was not about basic
income, but about the very experiment itsel
---
. Mannevuo suggests that
the experiment was actually a trial not about basic income but about using
randomised controlled trials in public policy-making. Finnish economists
have 
---
requently stated so, most recently in an interview published in The
Guardian: ‘People think we’re launching universal basic income. We’re not.
We’re just trialling one kind o
---
 model, with one income level and one target
group’, meaning that this is not a breakthrough 
---
or basic income but more ‘a
real breakthrough 
---
or ﬁeld experiments’.
(Mannevuo 2019: 5)

Cunning 87

For one, the very 
---
act that the government was able to run a mandatory ﬁeld trial
with unemployed – one that probed their socio-psychological response to an
experimental policy – was a 
---
eat in itsel
---
. Indeed, Mannevuo observes, the Finnish
Constitutional Law Committee had to bypass Article 7 o
---
 United Nations
Document A/2929, which prohibits states 
---
rom conducting medical and scientiﬁc
experiments without the 
---
ree consent o
---
 the individuals involved. Article 7 was
deemed irrelevant, as it is intended to prevent abuses in medical experiments.
This experiment, they argued, was intended to increase well-being – and it
appears that this rhetoric o
---
 good intentions won the day.
Further, it is worth noting that this is – to borrow the words o
---
 Roger
Martin – an exceptionally “sophisticated intervention” i
---

---

It gets in

ormation, and then it does something with it”. In concluding this

eedback
As hinted above, there is a clear connection between the emerging neuroliberalism,
on the one hand, and cybernetics, on the other. To some degree, the missing link
is provided by the ﬁeld o
---

cybernetics and the ﬁeld o
---
 design. In the 1950s, the design education at the Ulm
School o
---
 Design (Hochschule 
---
ür Gestaltung Ulm) set oﬀ on an ambitious attempt
to turn design into a scientiﬁc activity, linking it to the new science o
---
 cybernetics
(Glanville 2007: 1176).
Cybernetics, as deﬁned by Norbert Wiener (1948), is “the scientiﬁc study o
---

control and communication in the animal and the machine”. Wiener 
---
ounded
this new science o
---
 control and communication, which hinges on how to close
the gap between the desired behaviour o
---
 a system, on the one hand, and the
actual behaviour o
---
 that system, on the other. Cybernetics can thus be con­
strued as a generalization and universalization o
---
 the ﬁeld o
---
 control engineer­
ing: What i
---
 everything – machines, plants, animals, humans, man-machine
interactions, societies – can be understood through the principle that any kind
action is a mere response to 
---
eedback 
---
rom the environment? I
---
 the world can
be understood in this way, how can order be created – in technological systems,
and in socio-political systems?

88 Cunning

In 2010, as a response to this cybernetic vision, an anarchist collective called
Tiqqun published a text that can be thought o
---
 as a precautionary tale. The Cyber­
netic Hypothesis is an account o
---
 the pro
---
oundly political eﬀects that have emerged as
polities have sought to embrace principles 
---
rom cybernetics. For Tiqqun, the
cybernetic hypothesis constitutes “a new 
---
able that a
---
ter the Second World War has
deﬁnitively supplanted the liberal hypothesis”. Whereas the liberal hypothesis was
based on the idea o
---
 the essentially animal spirits o
---
 sel
---
-interested individuals, the
cybernetic hypothesis imagines human behaviour as subject to reprogramming.
Here, there is a signiﬁcant overlap with the above-mentioned accounts o
---
 a nascent
neuroliberalism.
According to Tiqqun, the cyberneticist sees “each individual behaviour as
something ‘piloted’, in the last analysis, by the need 
---
or the survival o
---

that makes it possible, and which it must contribute to”. In this way, the survival
o
---

---

---


---
eedback. “Retro-action” is “key to the system’s regulation”, where signals o
---

disturbances are 
---
ollowed by actions that push the system back to an equilibrium
state. Cybernetics, then, “is the project o
---

---
eedback loop”.
This nightmare vision o
---

which treats all signals o
---

---

which in turn are brought into a monstrous apparatus o
---
 
---
eedback loops – does
read like science ﬁction. However, beyond the hyperbole, there is one key lesson
to be taken away 
---
rom this account: Polities’ use o
---

---
eedback 
---
rom citi­
zens is in no way a guarantor o
---

---

aim is to use this technique to secure the current order. Indeed, the Communist
Party o
---

---

their user-citizens, experimenting with opinion polls and 
---

This connects to the argument 
---
rom which this chapter set out: What are the
actual eﬀects o
---
 design in government? Is it a guarantor o
---

---

---
are provision, or even in somewhat
sinister modes o
---

---
t?
Here, one may revert back to the nightmare vision o

---

---

---
ramework can be interpreted as 
---
eedback
signals that necessitate a cybernetic response. While statecra
---
t has always been
about pre-empting such protests, the “neuro” aspect o
---

level o
---

should be read as a cautionary tale. Still, this is just to reiterate the point that
design may be used in both democratic and undemocratic government. Again:
Mêtis can serve any political program, hence the suspicions o
---
 Plato.

Cunning 89

A more modest take on this question may be arrived at by revisiting the 
---
amous
1920s debate between Walter Lippmann and John Dewey, which concerned the
role o
---
 the public in the ever-more complex, and ever-more mediated, technolo­
gically advanced modern societies. The exchange by the two public intellectuals
started 
---
rom Lippmann’s (1922) critique o
---
 the idea that citizens in democracies are
capable o
---
 making in
---
ormed decisions. For Lippmann, ordinary citizens cannot
perceive all the intricacies o
---
 “the real environment” in which they live, and must
there
---
ore live their lives according to mental images o
---
 the world. The 
---
act that
they have to resort to such “ﬁctions”, with only a limited connection to the real
world raises a number o
---
 risks. There
---
ore, Lippmann called 
---
or the abandonment o
---

“the theory o
---
 the omnicompetent citizen” (Lippmann 1922: 364) which under­
pins traditional notions o
---
 democracy. Instead, he proposed that “centres o
---
 man­
agement” should be engaged in “the manu
---
acture o
---
 consent”, preparing a certain
number o
---
 well-in
---
ormed opinions. These readymade opinions would then, in
turn, be presented to the citizenry, which would choose the appropriate one. The
resulting system would be a 
---
orm o
---
 “guided democracy”.
Dewey (1927) responded to Lippmann’s critique, arguing that citizens can be
active participants in the deliberations that lead to well-in
---
ormed opinions.
According to him, joint issues o
---
 concern “spark a public into being” (Marres
2005), drawing select citizens into one o
---
 a multitude o
---
 concurrent deliberations.
In these, the citizen is a creative participant, not merely choosing 
---
rom the well
---
ormed opinions supplied by elites. In this way, Dewey promotes a “democratic
experimentalism”. Given his pre
---
erence 
---
or direct 
---
orms o
---
 participation rather than
representative modes o
---
 democracy (Sabel 2012: 40), this implies that the individual
citizen is the agent o
---
 experimentation, actively participating in a cooperative
inquiry. Thus, rather than being dependent on molders o
---
 opinion that elicit the
voices o
---
 citizens, Dewey insisted that democracy implies that citizens develop their
own vocabularies. This enables them to speak 
---
or themselves, even on technical or
complex issues, using their own words.
The contemporary discourse on design in government tends to use a rhetoric
that seems close to the Dewey “democratic experimentalism”. However, the
cases laid out in this chapter show how design in government seems to be even
more compatible with Lippmann’s “guided democracy”.1 All the cases described
above – the use o
---
 service design in the Swedish public sector, the sauna in the
Gothenburg harbor, the Finnish basic income experiment, and the broader neu­
roliberal movement – use the term “co-design” when describing their practices.
However, it seems diﬃcult to get away 
---
rom situations in which the designer acts
as the prime experimenting agent. User-citizens invariably play a secondary role,
with their inputs being elicited by the iterative interventions o
---
 the designer.
Again, this is not a condemnation o
---
 this development. There are clear merits
to Lippmann’s analysis o
---
 the shortcomings o
---
 democracy, and most democracies
need some element o
---
 technocratic guidance. However, there are nevertheless
problems that arise 
---
rom the idea o
---
 a guided democracy. The “manu
---
acturing o
---


90 Cunning

consent” that is a prerequisite 
---
or this democratic model does invariably place power
in the hands o
---
 elites. This implies that designers in government are – to varying
degrees – complicit with power structures, and this 
---
act must be internalized in the
sel
---
-understanding o
---
 designers.
Here, one may note an analogy between how designers, on the one hand, and
public relations pro
---
essionals, on the other, may support existing power structures.
One key contributor to the discussion on guided democracy was Edward Bernays,
the 
---
ather o
---
 modern public relations. In his landmark text on propaganda, Bernays
(1928) argued that the “chaos” o
---
 diverging views among the citizenry had to be
“organised”, in order 
---
or democracy to work. The new task o
---
 organizing this chaos,
he suggested, was to be ﬁlled by the emerging public relations (PR) pro
---
ession. This
analysis proved prescient: Since then, the political realm has become permeated
by these techniques o
---
 persuasion and coercion. Today, one may discern a similar
connection between design and political power.
This tendency needs to be recognized within the design pro
---
ession, as well as
among design scholars. I
---
 designers in government have become power
---
ul persons,
what kind o
---
 power do they wield? Are designers the quintessential technocrats o
---

the twenty-ﬁrst century? Alternatively, are they the well-speaking, truth-agnostic
people that Plato saw as the main reason democracies 
---
ailed to produce Good
governance: In short, are they present-day sophists? Most likely, they are both, and
the next chapter will explore ways o
---
 addressing this 
---
act.
In summary, this chapter has placed contemporary design in the context o
---

recent developments in statecra
---
t. First, there is the tendency 
---
or governments to
put service-design methods to work, using them to cut wel
---
are costs by construing
citizens not as subjects with rights but as users with needs. Second, contemporary
modes o
---
 governing can no longer be construed – 
---
ollowing de Certeau – as stra­
tegies, which can be countervailed by the tactics o
---
 the weak. Instead, tactics have
become an art o
---
 the strong, to which designers sometimes contribute. Third, there
is the tendency in which governments translate insights 
---
rom design and behavioral
sciences into “neuroliberal” policies o
---
 guiding citizen behaviors. Again, these
observations are not cause 
---
or condemning such practices: Like it or not, this is
how society is made today. However, these developments suggest that designers
may need to revise their views on their place in relation to centers o
---
 power.

HYPOCRISY
O
---
 virtue and vice

“Dare to be naive”. Those are the ﬁrst words in the ﬁrst volume o
---
 Buckminster
Fuller’s Synergetics. Published in 1975, the book 
---
amously outlines a distinctively
Fulleresque approach to geometry, energy and design. The incitement to naïveté is
meant to counter skeptical claims that in the modern world, there is limited scope

---
or 
---
urther discovery. The “moral o
---
 the work”, then, is that the solving o
---
 one
problem always yields a great number o
---
 new problems to be solved. Continued
experimentation and discovery is always possible.
However, when reviewing the argument so 
---
ar, we can see how participative
and socially oriented design can be a sordid business. Chapter 3 showed how codesign processes are ri
---
e with betrayals – how they operate on the principle that
participants’ interests are likely to be traded away in the process o
---
 goal translation.
The subsequent chapter outlined how social designs 
---
all prey to corruption – their
integrity is compromised by social processes o
---
 decay, causing good intentions to
lead to negative social outcomes. Further, the previous chapter showed how the
place o
---
 design in contemporary societies is not necessarily one 
---
rom which power
is challenged: A designerly worldview has increasingly reshaped statecra
---
t, assisting
power structures with new 
---
orms o
---
 cunning.
I
---
 one accepts these dark realities, is it really advisable to be naïve about design?
Indeed, some readers may surmise that the moral o
---
 this work runs counter to old
Bucky: “Don’t be naïve!” Well, that is a good starting point, but it hardly suﬃces
as a guideline 
---
or how to proceed as a designer. This chapter will thus search 
---
or a
realist sensibility that does not err towards a wholesale cynicism – towards a critical
position that undermines any attempts to do designerly work. How does one
nurture skepticism and still carry on designing?
As will be evident, this sensibility will inevitably have to engage with the
problem o
---
 hypocrisy. Indeed, i
---
 one accepts the power relations inherent in

92 Hypocrisy

design, one may be best served by accepting a disconnect between the actions o
---

the pro
---
essional designer, on the one hand, and the personal belie
---

 the person
behind the pro
---
essional mask, on the other. Designers should not be worried
about maintaining such a disconnect; they ought to worry about the risks o

---

The chapter will ﬁrst examine the stakes o
---
 this problem, returning to the

---

or users, in the emphasis on designerly empathy, and in the idea o

 the designer as

 – hypocrisy

As hinted above, the chapter ends with various accounts o

“With, not 
---
or, citizens” – the stakes o
---
 designerly virtues
In 2007, the Cooper Hewitt Design Museum in New York launched their

---
amous exhibition “Design 
---
or the other 90%”. This marked a pro
---
ound shi
---
t in
the ethics o
---
 design and reignited discussions around design and international
development. As the concept trans
---
ormed to speciﬁcally examine cities, the
exhibit changed names to “Design with the other 90%: Cities” – the “with”
underlining the co-design and participatory elements o
---
 urban design. Being
much discussed across the design ﬁeld, the exhibits were celebrated 
---
or high­
lighting the urgency and desperate need 
---
or design to tackle real-world situations.
However, the exhibitions were also criticized 
---
or othering populations who have
no direct access to the system most designers in the Global North take 
---
or gran­
ted. But as many noticed, the shi
---
t 
---
rom designing 
---
or to designing with also put
the spotlight onto another important aspect o
---
 design; who is really served by
design, and who has a say?
The shi
---
t in perspective made apparent at Cooper Hewitt corresponded to an
overall shi
---
t across the cultural ﬁeld at the time, with relational aesthetics and a gen­
eral push 
---
or participation and co-design. The switching o
---
 prepositions – 
---
rom “
---
or”
to “with” – also highlights a move that we touched upon in the previous chapter – a
move towards empathy as the primary quality and operational methodology o
---

Hypocrisy 93

This story also elucidates another aspect o
---
 how designers seek to position
themselves. Not only is empathy a key virtue, so is the ambition to act as an
“agent o
---
 change”. As in Tim Brown’s (2009) bestselling book Change By Design,
the world is just an incomplete and chaotic arrangement o
---
 interests eagerly
waiting 
---
or a designer to come and reorder it in an innovative way. It is a per­
spective that acts under the proposition that as design changes things, it makes
things better, and i
---
 it somehow does not live up to our expectations, then we
can say that it is bad design. Bad design, bad outcomes, can always be ﬁxed by
new iterations, new editions.
This perspective also holds that change is good. Thus, the term does not
include the kind o
---
 social corrosion and decay that Machiavelli was concerned
about (see Chapter 4). Instead, design is construed as the key act o
---
 making
change. From the perspective o
---
 designers, change means good design, and is
never corrupt. Change has a sense o
---
 
---
unction, honesty, and transparency that the
tensions and politics o
---
 human relations can never have. Design can be optimized,
whereas the politics o
---
 relations is a 
---
orm o
---
 
---
riction per se. Here, then, is a desire
to circumvent the dirtiness o
---
 politics – the kind o
---
 grubbiness that a realist tends
to construe as the key 
---
act o
---
 social li
---
e.
Attentive readers may recognize the idea o
---
 design as something that cuts
through the tangles o
---
 politics: Indeed, it resonates with the original claims o
---
 the
technocratic movement o
---
 the 1920s and 1930s. As Howard Scott writes in his
1933 Introduction to Technocracy: “In contrast to the devious ways o
---
 politics, […] we
have the methods o
---
 science and technology” (Scott 1933: 37). However, the
contemporary design thinking discourse cannot be accused o
---
 such a crude account
o
---
 politics. In 
---
act, this discourse does have an account o
---
 design and social conﬂict.
This is where the “
---
or” and “with” comes into the picture. In the traditional
modes o
---
 designing “
---
or” people, the agency and interests o
---
 the users are not
taking the 
---
ront seat. The contemporary 
---
ocus on designing “with” is a means to
engage with the question o
---
 social relations. The problem o
---
 the social – and the
political – is thus solved by a repositioning o
---
 the designer. The designer is no
longer a help
---
ul dictator or all-power
---
ul social engineer – the designer should
strive to be your 
---
riend.
Nevertheless, this repositioning disguises something more tricky: Design is, a
---
ter
all, a service pro
---
ession. The designer is paid to orient the user in alignment with
the client’s intentions. The designer says the work is 
---
or the user, but the demands
are set by the client. Designers want materials malleable by their own visions and
actions, and the users are a part o
---
 this material. This holds particularly true 
---
or the
design o
---
 services, which is a “design o
---

---
 things 
---
or
people” (Penin & Tonkinwise 2009: 1–2). Thus, in a human-centered approach o
---

design, change means aligning the needs and demands o
---
 the users to the intentions
o
---
 the client.
In design literature, this is o
---
ten noted without much controversy, as the
designer moves 
---
rom being a disconnected engineer towards a socially engaged

94 Hypocrisy

and empathic collaborator, partner, and 
---
riend. For example, design theorist Brigitte
Borja de Mozota posits:
Design has an avant-garde spirit. While not generating major innovations,
the designer anticipates new needs and creates new answers to meet con­
straints, while integrating progressive technologies. Design is a partner and
initiator o
---
 change in society. There
---
ore, it is a partner in the management o
---

change in organizations.
(Borja de Mozota 2003: 38)
The proximity to the user creates a complex situation. The designer “partners”
with the user, even though he or she is paid by a client to manipulate the user.
Borja de Mozota uses the example o
---
 
---
amous industrial designer Philippe Starck
to underline her point about “change in society”, and Starck desires to make
design more about change than mere aesthetics.
The urgent thing today is not to create a car or a chair that is more beauti
---
ul
than another. The ‘beauti
---
ul’ is a concept that is obsolete 
---
or the time being
and does not respond to the urgency 
---
acing society today, where barbarity
has reared its head again. What we need to do today is to replace aesthetic
objects with semantic objects, which results in replacing the beauti
---
ul with
the good. We must start again 
---
rom scratch so that these objects and
machines serve us, so that the objects is good 
---
or us, in order to live better.
(Starck, quoted in Borja de Mozota 2003: 38)
I
---
 designers acknowledge any political problems in design, it is usually 
---
ound in
the methods, not in the very ontology o
---

---
. For example, the classic
paradigm o
---
 design-based problem-solving may have an elitist or engineer-like
touch. Such connotations can be shunted by a more open approach where design
is more evenly distributed around the planet through social design or open design
and where “best design is replaced by just design” (Laitio 2011). Taken together,
these perspectives suggest that both design methods and design practitioners are
per deﬁnition good: Design rids the world 
---
rom 
---
rictions, conﬂicts, and injustices,
as well as problems. Thus, designers are the good ones, they are the heroes o
---


---
unctionality, progress, justice, and peace.
At this point in the argument, the reader will recognize that this idea o
---
 virtue sits
uneasily with previous points made in this book. As we saw in Chapter 3, co-design
processes are political processes, which invariably means that some interests are likely
to be betrayed. Again, 
---
rom a realist perspective, politics cannot be circumvented. As
we saw in Chapter 4, the social world tends towards decay, which means that design,

---
rom a realist perspective, should be less about disruption, and more about “mante­
nere lo stato”. In the previous chapter, we saw how one can scarcely say that design
is always on the side o
---
 the user, or the citizen 
---
or that matter. There
---
ore, it is worth

Hypocrisy 95

searching 
---
or another positioning o
---
 the designer, or – alternatively put – 
---
or another
designerly sensibility.
The issue o
---

Chapter 4. Again, Machiavelli’s contribution to political theory amounted to a
revision o
---

---
oundation o
---

---
 such virtues, he proposed
his own conception o
---
 princely virtú. Be
---
ore moving on to examine what some
kind o
---
 designerly virtú may mean today, let us examine the origins o
---
 today’s
designerly virtues. Indeed, the notion o
---

the designer as a part o
---

that constitutes an obligatory point o
---

---
or such an examination is Victor
Papanek’s Design 
---
or the Real World.

Idealism 
---
or the real world?
Papanek’s 1971 classic is widely recognized as the 
---
ounding text o
---
 the notion o
---

social design, and remains hugely inﬂuential. It is still read by design students, and
as suggested above, the imperative o
---
 the designer as activist and changemaker
constitutes the dominant mode o
---
 designerly engagement with the socio-political
realm. That being said, one can equally argue that today’s operationalization o
---

this imperative constitutes a pale imitation o
---
 the real thing.
Indeed, the notion o
---
 “the real” is crucial when assessing Papanek’s work today. It
is reasonably clear what kind o
---
 real that Design 
---
or the Real World is addressing. On
the one hand, design-as-we-know-it is 
---
undamental in creating the dream worlds
and illusory needs o
---
 the consumer society. On the other hand, this very potency o
---

design allows it to be reoriented so as to change the real world – the one existing
outside the consumerist bubble that tends to be the object o
---
 industrial design.
Although these twin proportions are clearly 
---
orged in the context o
---
 a broader
sociological critique o
---
 the consumer society, they remain broadly correct.
In political theory terms, the argument presented back in 1971 can be characterized
as part realist and part idealist. The book is realist in its attempt to present a hard-nosed
view on the constraints on design practices, placing them squarely in the context o
---

socio-political struggles. Nevertheless, it is also idealist, partly due to the ways in
which the “how it is” (the ﬁrst part o
---
 the book) is connected to the “how it ought to
be” (the second part o
---
 the book). Here, some observers would suggest that the
second part is too idealistic – it is too unrealistically detached 
---
rom the “how it is”.
Moreover, there is a moralist slant to Papanek’s project: It may be understood as
countering waste
---
ul and consumerist industrial design with good activist virtues.
Furthermore, as we have seen, it seems the mainstream o
---
 design has been able to

100 Hypocrisy

---

transport, thus excluding racial minorities and the poor 
---
rom the beach. Thus,
the two hundred or so low-hanging overpasses on Long Island were deliberately
designed to achieve a particular social eﬀect. Robert Moses, the master builder
o
---

---
rom the 1920s to the 1970s in
New York, had these overpasses built to speciﬁcations that would discourage
the presence o
---

Winner’s objective is to use the Moses story to argue that arti
---

---
s, which I know to be imper
---
ect, but that is all I
can do” – prevents him 
---
rom accepting responsibility.
Runciman also points to how the same politics o

Franklin is, Shklar concedes, a role model that is diﬃcult to emulate: He was a

As such, Shklar suggests, Franklin knew exactly what democratic politics

The next section will explore the proposition that in the end – whether one

How to be a better kind o
---
 hypocrite
In their respective discussions on hypocrisy, Shklar and Runciman demarcate
their projects 
---
rom those o
---
 Machiavelli- or Nietzsche-readers, who merely
attempt to demask liberal democracy. Shklar, on her end, warns o
---

who – as “disappointed idealists” in the search 
---
or purity – have been driven out
o
---

---
 all visible poli­
ticians” (85). Similarly, Runciman’s book on hypocrisy consciously sidesteps
Machiavelli and Nietzsche, even though they have much to say about the topic.
Instead, he is more interested in pragmatic approaches to hypocrisy, written 
---
rom
inside the liberal tradition. These accounts go 
---
urther than simply point out that
there is an underlying reality that is uglier than the shiny sur
---
ace. The problem –
what he re
---
ers to as “the ‘Shklar’ problem” – is how to “tread care
---
ully” and
“avoid lumping all hypocrisies together”, thus deciding “which are the ones that
are worth worrying about” (Runciman 2008: 41).
The best starting point, Runciman suggests, is the work o
---
 Hobbes. As noted
above, the concept o
---
 hypocrisy has connotations to theater, and as such, it
resonates well with Hobbes’ conception o
---
 politics. Indeed, in describing his
conception o
---
 a sovereign – the one that, a
---
ter the Westphalian peace, ended up
serving as a model o
---
 the modern nation state – Hobbes did not invent a new
technical term. Rather, Hobbes borrowed one 
---
rom mythology. The Leviathan is
a monster, but also an artiﬁcial being, a composite o
---
 ordinary human beings –
which in turn are protected by this monstrous and artiﬁcial creature. In describing

106 Hypocrisy

how this arrangement is made operational, Hobbes re
---
ers to the importance o
---

representation and the per
---
orming o
---
 roles. Thus, Hobbes’ Leviathan includes a
chapter on “persons, authors, and things personated”, in which political
representation ultimately implies wearing a mask:
Persona in latine signiﬁes the disguise, or outward appearance o
---
 a man,
counter
---
eited on the Stage, and sometimes more particularly that part o
---
 it,
which disguiseth the 
---
ace, as a Mask or Vizard.
(Hobbes, 1651, cited in Runciman 2008: 38)
The sovereign representative, then, is ultimately a person wearing a mask – a
mask o
---
 power. In so doing, the representative is engaging in the artiﬁcial per­

---
ormance o
---
 a theatrical role, which is necessary 
---
or the operation o
---
 the artiﬁcial
being that we call the Leviathan. As such, dissimulation is a necessary part o
---
 the
power arrangement, and there must necessarily be a distance between the perso­
nal being and the external representation o
---
 the pro
---
essional persona.
Nevertheless, there was one type o
---
 deception that Hobbes warned against –
the one where people in power do not recognize the terms o
---
 power. Thus, he
wanted such people to accept their proper place in the power hierarchy, and play
their roles as power-holders honestly. Hypocrisy – the separation o
---
 the belie
---
s o
---

the private person, and the actions o
---
 the public persona – may be inevitable,
since politics is a wholly artiﬁcial per
---
ormance. However, when those in power
are hypocritical about this 
---
act, the system o
---
 power starts to corrode. In other
words, we should not worry about hypocrisy, but rather about hypocrisy about the
need 
---
or hypocrisy. Runciman thus suggests that
we need politicians who are sincere, but that does not mean that we should
wish them to be sincere believers in everything they do. Instead, we need
them to be sincere about the system in which they ﬁnd themselves, and
sincere in their desire to maintain the stability and durability o
---
 that system,
even i
---
 it comes at the cost o
---
 their own ability to say what they mean and
mean what they say.
(Runciman 2008: 213)
Again, Runciman’s and Hobbes’ warnings against this hypocrisy about hypocrisy
are clearly 
---
ounded in politics. This goes in particular 
---
or the concern 
---
or the
maintenance durability o
---
 a political system o
---
 representation deeply reliant on
mask-wearing. The design pro
---
ession does not have to bear such considerations in
mind. Still, “second-order hypocrisy” does constitute a problem in design. We do
need designers who are sincere about the system o
---
 power in which they may
ﬁnd themselves. This entails recognizing the problems o
---
 good intentions out­
lined in Chapter 4: Social means does not necessarily produce social ends. Just as
in the case o
---
 the politics o
---
 good intentions epitomized by Tony Blair, re
---
erences

Hypocrisy 107

to sincerely held convictions, personal values or grand ambitions are not valid
excuses 
---
or bad outcomes.
Being sincere about power structures also entails recognizing some o
---
 the pro­
blems raised in Chapter 5: Even though empathy and participation are worthy

---
oundations to start 
---
rom, such virtues should not distract our view 
---
rom the 
---
act
that design does not necessarily empower the disempowered. The point here is
not that designers should only engage in truly radical work; to engage in the art
o
---
 the weak. Nor is it to state that it is wrong 
---
or designers to engage in the
emerging modes o
---

---
t. The point is that designers should be
sincere about the roles that they play, and the masks that they wear. Their good
intentions will not save them 
---
rom betrayal or corruption, or acting like
hypocrites to some degree.
So, to summarize, Hobbes’ work gives us some hints to solve the “Shklar
problem”: Which types o
---

quietly accepted, but also taken into account – ugly as they may be. Still, there is the
other problem that was raised in the introduction to this chapter: How does one
balance the recognition o
---

---
 power without succumbing to wholesale
cynicism? In concluding this chapter, we will address that question, while also
picking up a loose end: The question o
---
 the vice o
---

Dare to be naïve – redux
When introducing Ordinary Vices, we suggested that among the vices covered by
Shklar, hypocrisy and misanthropy serve as re
---
erence points 
---
or this chapter.
Hypocrisy has been discussed at length, but what about misanthropy? This vice is the
last one that Shklar discusses, and her treatment o
---
 it is – as the reader may expect at
this point – one that emphasizes its ambiguity. On the one hand, she places hersel
---

squarely on the pessimist side on the debate on human nature. Humans are, sadly,
ﬂawed creatures: “Most moral psychology o
---
 any worth is a scream o
---
 disgust, as is
much o
---
 what philosophers since Plato have told us about ourselves” (Shklar 1984:
193). Thus, those intellectuals who express a “loathing o
---
 humanity” do, in 
---
act,
provide us with essential knowledge: They teach us how to be suspicious towards
people in power, since they may be capable o
---
 cruel conduct. However, when we
engage in misanthropy ourselves, we risk becoming “intolerant, cruel, and, at the
very least, socially impossible”. There
---
ore,
misanthropy is politically a paradox. Disdain and 
---
ear could and did serve as
the basis o
---
 political decency, legal restraint, and the eﬀort to create limited
government that would attenuate the eﬀects o
---
 the cruelty o
---
 those who
rule. Misanthropy can, however, also initiate slaughter.
There are 
---
urther grounds 
---
or avoiding misanthropy. For one, misanthropy may
be expressed in the unproductive kind o
---
 anti-hypocrisy that Shklar warns against.

108 Hypocrisy

Further, misanthropy must be moderated, so as not to produce aimlessness. She
re
---
ers back to Montaigne’s objections to Machiavelli. As we saw in Chapter 5,
Machiavelli suggests that virtú must replace traditional virtues, so that the prince
can complete great causes and attain glory. However, i
---
 excessive misanthropy
prevails, how can there be a collective understanding o
---
 what great causes and
glory entails? “Montaigne was right. Machiavelli’s disdain is, in the end, sel
---
de
---
eating” (213).
In other words, misanthropy must be moderated. We must learn 
---
rom
Machiavelli, yet avoid becoming all-out “Machiavellians”. Rather than 
---
ostering a
“disappointed idealist” sensibility that leads us away 
---
rom the grubby business o
---

doing either politics or design, we must ﬁnd a nuanced and pragmatic position.
This is not just a matter o
---
 retrieving some element o
---
 hope that something good
comes out o
---
 our labors; this is not merely about 
---
ooling ourselves that politics or
design can produce meaning
---
ul change in the world. It is also the case that too
bleak a vision o
---
 these activities can simply give a 
---
alse impression o
---
 the very
workings o
---
 either politics or design.
Here, one may learn 
---
rom another philosopher that Runciman discusses in his
book on hypocrisy: Jeremy Bentham. One o
---
 the things that Western philosophy
has inherited 
---
rom Plato was the idea that democracy may look nice, but it is
much worse than it looks. It may be wrapped in a language o
---
 ideals and virtue,
but that veneer merely hides the ugly truth: Under the shiny sur
---
ace, there is only
lies and deceit. Runciman suggests that Bentham was wary o
---
 how “high-ﬂown
language o
---
 democratic principle can be a mask 
---
or the power relations that lie
behind it” (Runciman 2008: 206), but equally suggested that such a view is too
simplistic. This is because it, in turn, conceals “the extent to which democracy
relies on a set o
---
 ﬁctions about its own essential character”. So, while politicians
may overindulge in “high
---
alutin cant” about democratic ideals, it is also the case
that critical analysts may overindulge in “technical cant” that reduces such ideals
to mere ﬁction. Bentham’s contribution – at least as Runciman sees it – is to
point out that democracy cannot operate without such ﬁctions. In a subsequent
book, Runciman (2013) makes a similar argument about democracy: It rests on
the 
---
act that citizens are conﬁdent that democracy actually delivers – that their
participation is worthwhile, that they have a voice, that democracy produces
good results. This conﬁdence may, at times, rest on a ﬁction, but that ﬁction is
nevertheless 
---
undamental to the very 
---
unctioning o
---
 democracy.
In the end, a similar understanding may underpin a realist understanding o
---

design. For sure, the notion o
---
 design as “devising courses o
---
 action aimed at
changing existing situations into pre
---
erred ones” (Simon 1996: 111), or “the
planning and patterning o
---
 any act toward a desired, 
---
oreseeable end” (Papanek
1971: 3), can be interpreted in overly idealist or moralist manners. In so doing,
the design process may be understood in all too voluntaristic terms. Still, a belie
---

in the value o
---
 attaining the pre
---
erred, desired, and reasonably 
---
oreseeable 
---
uture
state must be retained, even 
---
or the hard-nosed realist. So is it then, a matter o
---


Hypocrisy 109

suspension o
---
 disbelie
---
 – o
---
 a cautious embrace o
---
 “high
---
alutin cant” and use
---
ul
ﬁctions? Is it, a
---
ter all, a question o
---
 daring to be naïve? The naïveté that
Buckminster Fuller wrote about was supposed to instill a belie
---
 that creation and
innovation is still possible, even a
---
ter the great advances o
---
 modern li
---
e. For the
realist designer, it may be the case that an element o
---
 naïveté is necessary 
---
or the

---
ostering o
---

---
rom zero-sum experience.

CLOSING PROPOSITIONS
A
---
ter empathy, Realdesign

Given the previous chapters’ recurring re
---
rain – that contemporary design practices
un
---
old in an imper
---
ect world, in which the existence o
---
 betrayal, corruption, cun­
ning, and hypocrisy, is an incontrovertible 
---
act o
---
 social li
---
e – we hope to ﬁnish this
book with a set o
---
 propositions 
---
or how designers can view and relate to such a
world. Thus, as hinted earlier, we suggest an approach to participatory design that has
- - tek/).
a taint o
---
 Ludwig von Rochau’s original take on Realpolitik (/raˈälpoliˌ
The aim
here is to sketch the outlines o
---
 the perspective we call Realdesign (/raˈäldəˈzın/),
that
is, a design that does not stand solely on ideals, a bit less sentimental, and 
---
oremost,
with a sober perspective on power.
With the concept o
---
 Realdesign, we strive to bring von Rochau’s warning
against the implicit predominance o
---
 Ge
---
ühlspolitik (sentimental politics) and Prin­
zipenpolitik (principled politics) to bear with design: The unquestioned dominance
o
---
 sentimental and ideal designs stripped o
---
 power. Again, Realdesign is not
trading in impossibilities or distanced armchair cynicism. In alignment with von
Rochau, we are concerned with the two overarching points tackled in the pre­
vious chapters – ﬁnding a realism not bogged down with cool and critical pas­
sivity, and a drive towards utopianism that is not moralist – with the aim o
---

emphasizing the creative and imaginative elements inherent in design, elements
that we argue can also be present in realism. Thus, with the term Realdesign we seek
to imply a designerly realism, utopic, imaginative, and visionary; yet backed with a
sober perspective on the Machiavellian characteristics o
---
 politics and the recalcitrance
o
---
 social relations. In
---
ormed by a strategic insight, bolstered by alliances, Realdesign
does not shy away 
---
rom aﬃrming a need to take a realist perspective on power, or

---
rom leveraging or claiming power where needed.
A perspective o
---
 Realdesign is not meant to celebrate the Machiavellian or
authoritarian auteur. Its purpose is to de
---
end and develop the democratic
DOI: 10.4324/9781003281443-7

Closing propositions 111

elements in participatory processes or co-design. Here, democratic contrivances
work in the Nietzschean sense as quarantine measures against the lust 
---
or power:
As necessary as they are boring (Flyvbjerg 1998: 225). The realist but democratic ele­
ments o
---
 co-design are tedious yet essential to limit the dri
---
t towards the Machiavellian
auteurship o
---
 unilateral decision-making and project authoritarianism.
As pointed out in previous chapters, with the interest in socially engaged processes
growing over the last decade, designers must be care
---
ul how they utilize processes o
---

participation, social innovation, and co-design. Processes o
---
 user engagement are not
a panacea 
---
or producing socially beneﬁcial outcomes. Instead, there is a recurring risk
that the 
---
