---
act that such processes o
---
ten 
---
ail to
deliver equitable results. The ideal o
---
 how designers want things to be, ob
---
uscates the
con
---
usion, tensions, and corruption taking place within the “material” o
---
 social
sculpture. With voluntary blinders on and dazzled by democratic virtue, the 
---
act that
outcomes so seldom match intentions is an urgent problem 
---
or developing more
eﬀective and equitable 
---
orms o
---
 public design processes. The agnosticism regarding
social outcomes o
---
 social design is curious, given that design history and theory tends
to 
---
ocus heavily on the outcomes o
---
 the design process.
Summing up, social design processes need to tackle a series o
---
 underlying issues
present within social relations. We aim to recount some o
---
 them here. The prin­
cipal question to be addressed in this concluding discussion is then “what basic
relations o
---
 social power undermine the visions designers set out with and come to
shape co-design processes?” Echoing Flyvbjerg’s (1998) examination o
---
 rationality
and power, we set out to suggest a series o
---
 propositions 
---
or Realdesign.

Proposition 1: Design must get real
Designers suﬀer 
---
rom the common conviction that the best idea wins, and thus
see design as a game o
---
 persuasion. Designers need to be better at recognizing that
it is not virtuous ideas or intentions that get made or have real eﬀects. Design
backed by power becomes reality, and this new reality upholds the powers
invested. Be aware; as Latour has pointed out many times, in the social processes
o
---
 actants and design, more o
---
ten than not, Might is Right. Again, in line with
Latour’s point in the construction o
---
 scientiﬁc proo
---
, design proposals get acti­
vated in tensions between networks o
---
 power, incentives, and inﬂuences.
As a means to prevent social practice-in
---
ormed design 
---
rom generating negative
outcomes, designers ought to acknowledge the limits o
---
 idealist “what i
---
?” start­
ing points. “What i
---
” questions are strong visionary starting points, but not
enough to make things real. In order to balance such idealism, designers ought to
place more 
---
ocus on the realist “who whom?” – who beneﬁts 
---
rom the social
innovation, and who pays the price 
---
or the change in order?
As Geuss stresses, the basic political question has to be an extension o
---
 Lenin’s
realist “who whom”, to become “Who <does> what to whom 
---
or whose ben­
eﬁt?” (Geuss 2008: 23). The doing part above is not limited to the actual practice,

112 Closing propositions

but also signiﬁes the potential threat o
---
 doing, as in the peril o
---
 punishment, or
withdrawal o
---
 doing, as in termination o
---
 support, agency, or care. Here, in re
---
er­
ence to the discussion above on the universalism o
---
 participatory approaches, it is
worth remembering that the “who whom” question includes an interrogation o
---

whose perspective remains dominant in a process o
---
 participation, in whose
interests the participants are doing what they do.
Designers could attune their practices to the propensity o
---
 social relations to be
aﬀected by Machiavellian politics. In the end, also in social design processes “
---
orm

---
ollows 
---
unction”, as the 
---
amous dictum goes. But 
---
or social processes, this means

---
orm is dictated under the 
---
unctional values o
---
 the most power
---
ul. The ultimate
“
---
unction” o
---
 most designs is to preserve the status quo, serving the client in
alignment with power politics.
“Who whom” remains a key question 
---
or the realist designer.

Proposition 2: The process o
---
 change is a process o
---
 conﬂict
Someone’s utopia is someone else’s dystopia. The desired “change” that designers
play with through design thinking and visioning is not a neutral process. Change
alters power dynamics; someone’s position and interests will be advanced, and
most o
---
ten to the disadvantage o
---
 some other stakeholder. Realist politics, as
Geuss (2010: 23) notes, means “to think politically is to think about agency,
power, and interests, and the relations between these”. Seen 
---
rom the perspective
o
---
 power, what is right, and even laws, are minor politics: It is the en
---
orcement o
---

laws that is the real political matter, that gives some stability to political action.
Politics then is about exercising power to attain various ends; it is not an
inherent rule-governed activity, but one better understood as the conjunc­
tion o
---
 diﬀerent actors making context-speciﬁc judgments, taking advantage
o
---
 unexpected opportunities, and innovating. There is nothing unnatural in
exercising power […] and part o
---
 the process o
---
 exercising such power might
be to subject those under one’s control to rules o
---
 one’s choice.
(2010: 51)
A designer must not shy away 
---
rom the power that comes with the pro
---
ession,
but also not hide his or her responsibilities behind users or participants, or
toothless principles. Engaging in social processes means diving into conﬂict with
imper
---
ect and unpredictable collaborators.
Rather than idealist “what i
---
?” questions, designers – so used to thinking in terms
o
---
 “the user” – may ask questions like: Who is the “user” and who is being “used”?
As Geuss suggests, every social order is always a social achievement, and such an
undertaking is “generally achieved only at a certain price” (Geuss 2008: 22). A new
order inevitably comes at the price o
---
 one or several persons’ disadvantage, and “one
person’s disorder is sometimes another’s 
---
reedom”, not too unlike how US Secretary

Closing propositions 113

o
---
 De
---
ense Donald Rums
---
eld 
---
or instance called the civil war in Iraq as an instance o
---

“untidy 
---
reedom” (Geuss 2008: 22
---
).
“Disruption” is a trendy 
---
orm o
---
 untidy 
---
reedom, which all too o
---
ten does not
disrupt the capitalist and colonial paradigm under which it operates. Instead, the
disruption tends to 
---
racture the social structures that make labor stable and
threatens to dissolve long-established solidarities. Disruption in design-speak is
another word 
---
or political destabilization, in correspondence with how William
Galston summarizes politics as the tension between stabilization and destabiliza­
tion: “politics is always and everywhere a tension between the drive 
---
or and the
goods o
---
 stabilization and consensus, on the one hand, and the drive 
---
or and
goods o
---
 destabilization and conﬂict on the other” (Galston 2007: 9).
One o
---
 the reasons change is slow in social structures is that social bonds take a long
time to establish. People notice shi
---
ts in power and when 
---
acing threat 
---
rom the out­
side, and tacit bonds o
---
 loyalty between peers tend to 
---
ray slowly. Disruptive designs
bypass and break apart established loyalties, overriding social emotions (such as shared
empathy) with technological and organizational streamlining, leaving social bonds
shattered and redundant. The realist reminds us that change is a business 
---
ar untidier
than most designers tend to recognize; think twice be
---
ore you disrupt social relations.
At its base, social design is an engagement conﬂict that breaks up and rearranges
relationships. Even in what looks like win-win situations, there will most certainly
be losers down the line. A realist designer must ask: Who cleans up the mess and
heals the social wounds a
---
ter the “design disruptors” have le
---
t with the valuables?

Proposition 3: There is something rotten in design, and the user is
the victim
Designers need to engage with the negative valence o
---
 power politics, a necessity o
---

dealing with Machiavelli. That is, a designer engaging in social processes needs to
practice power literacy in design. As mentioned earlier, empathy and creative processes
o
---
 visioning, as in “design thinking”, are not enough. The realist perspective here
acknowledges how design processes arrange and allocate agency, assets, and power,
between client and user, and this process remains largely distant by the idealist-driven
“what i
---
” questions o
---
 the designer.
The types o
---
 co-design that emerge 
---
rom a lens o
---
 Scandinavian social-democratic
and consensus-driven perspective on politics suﬀer 
---
rom the common delusion that
the user (or citizen) stands at the 
---
oundation o
---
 power. When not congratulating
themselves by associating with the genius o
---
 turtleneck-clad entrepreneurs, the more
democratically oriented designers tend to think there is a “We the users”, or rather,
“We the consumers”, as the spirit guiding design progress. In a history o
---
 social
design, it may appear that power emerges 
---
rom the conscious consent o
---
 the user,
seduced to change behavior by the grand visions proposed by designers or entre­
preneurs. The user, or “stakeholder”, is in charge, designers tell themselves, or at least
write so in their project applications.

114 Closing propositions

Do not 
---
ool yoursel
---
. Users are hostages. They are held by designers as human
shields. I
---
 the vision works, it is to the merit o
---
 the excellent and good-hearted
designer, stripping users 
---
rom praise. But when the project 
---
ails, the users are le
---
t
adri
---
t, exhausted and betrayed, while the designer searches 
---
or a new trendy issue to
address, 
---
resh calls 
---
or project applications, or better participants to curate towards
photogenic results. Under the delusion o
---
 user-power, both use-situations and users
themselves are designed to align with the designated distribution o
---
 power and
agency set by the stakeholders in power, such as clients, institutions, or 
---
unding
agencies. “User-centric” means the shaping o
---
 user agency, and the actions o
---
 the
user are at the center o
---
 the process. As Steve Woolgar (1990) has so poignantly
shown, it is users who are themselves designed in what is called user-driven processes.
Eﬀective social design work must not be mistaken as good-hearted volunteerism,
where the designer does good 
---
or a speciﬁc user, hoping the eﬀort makes a di
---
­

---
erence in this isolated instance while 
---
ailing to push the bigger issue. Nor is social
design solutionism, where a cybernetic and data-driven ﬁx can bypass power games
as the situation is optimized in 
---
avor o
---
 the user (or at least it seems so because it is
“transparent”).
By shying away 
---
rom how users are continuously abused in contestations o
---

power, designers betray the people they are saying they support. Recognizing the
unavoidable conﬂicts users are stuck in must be the starting point 
---
or engagement.

Proposition 4: Good is dead
Even i
---
 inhabiting a world where it seems Might is Right, designers should not
abandon a quest 
---
or empathy and participation. So just because designers may use
a realist lens to view the world and see their practice in relationship to invested
interests and power dynamics, does not mean ethics are jettisoned. But it is a 
---
air
question to ask; is Good dead?
First, empathy, as the guiding principle o
---
 design, is not a cure-all. Rather, we
must remember that perverted 
---
orms o
---
 empathy are also present in torture, as
Elaine Scarry (1985) points out, where 
---
eeling is used as a weapon needed in
unmaking the physical and psychological world o
---
 others. An eﬀective torturer
knows, and 
---
eels out, how to break down and unmake the emotional world o
---

the victim to reach the desired outcome.
Second, empathy may not produce the emotional bandwidth between designer
and user as is commonly suggested. While empathy does strive to connect with
others, it is still sel
---
-re
---
erential: The 
---
oundation o
---
 the connection emerges 
---
rom
my own 
---
eelings and experiences. Similarly, the others that I build my experience
on, are the ones I normally connect with, who are within my own “ingroup”, or
at least beings onto whom I can project my own sensibilities. That is, I empathize
with my peers, and perhaps mammals, but it is harder empathizing with people I
disagree with, with enemies or organisms whose bodies and senses are 
---
oreign to
me, such as insects.

Closing propositions 115

Third, as pointed out by Cameron Tonkinwise (2010), the 
---
ocus on the good
experience 
---
or the user also misses contexts o
---
 aggregation, systemic issues, and
political conﬂict. The lens o
---
 empathy 
---
ails to recognize questions o
---
 scale, historic
struggles, gains or compromise. For the armchair-design thinker, empathy does
not register sacriﬁces made in violence and negotiations. It is too easy to overlook
the perspective o
---
 silenced voices and trauma, as they may not appear on the
readable emotional spectrum to the outsider designer. Instead, driven by his or
her own 
---
eelings (or the current social, economic, or political agenda) o
---
 what
appears just and right, the designer quickly moralizes the subject empathized
with, searching 
---
or the “right” empathy with the “right” people. Whereas
empathy presents itsel
---
 as neutral and ethical, we must remember that what is
called “populism” is empathetic politics, en
---
orcing itsel
---
 on the public realm by
provocative, irrational, and 
---
eeling-driven struggles over power (Ahmed 2004).
Along the lines o
---
 Ahmed, Philippe Mengue (2005: 393) sees the use and
exploitation o
---
 the passions as instrumental in order to rule:
Politics as an activity does not consist o
---
 the elimination o
---
 the passions
(which is impossible), but in knowing how they can be used, how to play oﬀ
one against the other, in order to assure a minimum o
---
 unity, o
---
 stability and
agreement in civil society. This emotional core […] needs continually to be
reproduced, because by itsel
---
 it is unstable, changing and transitory.
Along the lines o
---
 Flyvbjerg’s (1998) warning o
---
 the political use o
---
 rationality as a
disguise o
---
 power politics, (what is “
---
unctional” is that which supports the type o
---

power which thrives under that 
---
unction) also empathy is easily converted into a tool
to 
---
urther the designers’ own status and prestige. Similarly, it is easy to attack the use
o
---
 empathy 
---
rom a similar angle as Flyvbjerg’s critique o
---
 rationality, that what is
considered empathic is only that which translates to operational guidelines and can
increase the power and status o
---
 those inside the organizations. To the point where

---
ew proponents o
---
 empathy will use it i
---
 the empathy works against them.
So a reader may ask, what are designers to do with their empathy, the capacity
they have come to believe is their superpower? The point o
---
 Realdesign is; when
it is not misguided or deluded, empathy is not enough. The methods seeking
empathy, such as participation, inclusion, and co-design, are too easily nulliﬁed
when it comes to actually representing user interests in a ﬁnal design. Without a
backing o
---
 political agency and action, virtuous ambitions are edgeless. From this
standpoint, Good is dead. Good, as a 
---
ree-standing vision o
---
 designerly practice
guided by high-minded intentions, does not per deﬁnition win the competition.
In this way, Realdesign does not put 
---
ocus on the “why”, thinking that good
enough reasons will convince reality to take 
---
orm along virtuous courses o
---

action. Instead, Realdesign strives to move beyond the “why”, to the crueler
emphasis on how to mobilize power to leverage and change the outcomes in

---
avorable ways.

116 Closing propositions

The Machiavellian perspective o
---
 Realdesign highlights how we have to separate
empathy and morals 
---
rom political power. This is a delicate strategy in a time
where politics, in many places, has become the extension o
---
 morals; as today,
anyone with an opinion is a meme-politician, each staking out their own moral
claims rather than mobilizing a realistic political program. I
---
 Realpolitik is a sphere
separate 
---
rom morals, delineating the public 
---
rom the personal; Realdesign 
---
ocuses
on the distribution o
---
 power and agency, over ethics and the empathy o
---
 personal
experiences and opinions.
It is not that virtue as a goal is disqualiﬁed. But remember, as both Machiavelli
and von Rochau showed with their sordid perspectives on politics, that the quest

---
or the good needs to be backed by a realistic perspective on power.

Proposition 5: Realdesign is a creative 
---
orm o
---
 realism
The idealist designer would complain and say, “I
---
 you remain stuck in zero-sum
power-games, you limit the creative visioning and potential o
---
 design!” While the
possibilities o
---
 Post-its may appear inexhaustible, we must not limit the realist
perspective to merely a trade in impossibilities or cynical modes to close down
creative processes with an appeal to “stay realistic”.
First, power is at least as creative as the various 
---
orms o
---
 resistance to it. Even
conservation o
---
 power needs creativity. While “holding onto power” may seem
intrinsically de
---
ensive and reactive, maintaining the status quo requires relentless
innovation. As Geuss (2010) warns, we must not mistake idealism as being more
“creative” or “imaginative” than realism just because it deals in possibilities.
Realists invent enemies and excuses 
---
or war, 
---
uel conﬂict with creative work,
circumvent embargos and create alternative networks o
---
 diplomacy and smug­
gling, in the end remaking whole new ideological realities, just to name some
examples. Realists operate vibrant 
---
orms o
---
 imaginative storytelling (not least
imagining “weapons o
---
 mass destruction” and inventing “enhanced interroga­
tion”). But compared to the idealists, they see their creation within a 
---
ramework
o
---
 contestation and conﬂict, not sunk into the radiant mire o
---
 virtue.
In a similar vein, we must not think that realists cannot “speculate” about the

---
uture like critical designers do. Realists make no more claims to truth than the
idealists do, and the suspension o
---
 disbelie
---
 in “speculation” should raise as much
suspicion as skeptical suppositions. The idealist’s guidance by virtue looks away

---
rom how the same sentiments and cultural norms spur many 
---
orms o
---
 relational
violence in the name o
---
 virtue (Fiske & Rai 2015). As Geuss (2010: x) notes,
conservation and con
---
ormism require imagination just like innovation, even i
---
 it is
an imagination o
---
 a diﬀerent kind. Claims and works o
---
 power are just as creative,
imaginative, innovative, and possible, as resistance, critique, and virtuous 
---
antasies.
So, while realism may o
---
ten appear as the antonym o
---
 speculative or imaginary
it must not be seen as relentlessly negative or a cynical retreat to Might is Right.
Realdesign is not more or less imaginary than any other design. The plasticity o
---


Closing propositions 117

the world is still there, even i
---
 tied to power struggles. While a Realdesigner may
have a stark realization o
---
 the 
---
orces that shape both possibilities and impossi­
bilities, these are like most other obstacles or problems in design; they 
---
orm the
parameters 
---
or the innovation space the designer has to deal with. Here, the
obstacles emerge 
---
rom social relations o
---
 power, and cannot be dreamed or
argued away with beauti
---
ul renderings, appeals 
---
rom users or citizens, or claims o
---

moral superiority.
The creative part o
---
 design likes to see itsel
---
 as dealing in “constructive criti­
cism”. Design deﬁes the negative, as the very 
---
oundation o
---
 design lies in its
shaping o
---
 the artiﬁcial, suggesting change, however small, as its core activity, and
here lies also its political potential (Dilnot 2015). Nevertheless, any 
---
orm o
---
 cri­
tique must analyse, evaluate, compare, and judge, as Geuss (2014: 70) notes.
Thus, the primary question 
---
or the designer must be “constructive under what
conditions and constraints?” That is, constructive 
---
or whom? Geuss notes,
What might count as ‘constructive’ 
---
or us, that is, what we, given who we are,
could do about something, given our identities and possibilities, need not be
the same as what is constructive 
---
or them (given their identity and situation)
(Geuss 2014: 80)
For designers, valuable constructive critique can judge things both positive and
negative, and, perhaps more importantly, open, enrich, and reconﬁgure the issue
at hand 
---
or better 
---
uture decisions to be made. But the Realdesigner must know
the limits to the 
---
rameworks used to judge things “constructive” in the ﬁrst place
to circumvent the repressive attempts to limit critique to that which aligns with
the current dominant political program or ideology.
So, to sum up, Realdesign is not more or less utopian than idealism, but acts
on “utopian realism” (Geuss 2016), that is, building on visions while backed by a
real perspective on power. This means not 
---
alling 
---
or the situationist trap o
---

restraining realism, claiming it deals merely in the impossible (or “be realistic,
demand the impossible” as the situationists would say). The project o
---
 the Realdesigner is supported by political principles, not moral ones, it is “one o
---
 ‘wish
---
ul
visions,’ (rather than ‘wish-dreams’)” (Geuss 2016: 48).

Proposition 6: Ask realist questions
Taking a realist approach to design should not mean an endorsement o
---

Machiavellian power politics, but our main concern is in line with the perspective

---
ramed by von Rochau and discussed in the second chapter. While visions o
---

design can concern realization o
---
 ideals, the realist must put 
---
ocus on the attain­
ment o
---
 concrete ends, to back visions with a sober look on power. Realdesign is
design that builds towards a political program and to one’s best abilities eliminates
sel
---
-delusion.

118 Closing propositions

“How” and “what” are recurring questions in design processes, and they take
diﬀerent 
---
orm in traditional linear design brie
---
s versus the more complex issues at
hand in socially entangled issues. Instead o
---
 putting the “how” question at the
center o
---
 design processes, socially aware design practices have cultivated a culture
where questioning the brie
---
 is a recurring theme. “What” is really needed is a
question that opens up to more socially aware 
---
orms o
---
 inquiry to reach the “real
problem” sought by Papanek. Whereas the “how” question may point to best
practices, the “what” question is explicitly context-driven and requires a designer
equipped with curiosity and ample time 
---
or listening and empathy. However, to
once again paraphrase Papanek, a practitioner o
---
 Realdesign needs to engage with
the realist tensions that underpin the designer’s share o
---
 the problem. Underneath
the traditional “how” and “what”, a chasm o
---
 power now opens.
As noted in Bew’s (2016) discussion on von Rochau’s ideas, engaging with
power requires a clear-sighted understanding o
---
 who holds, and is given, power in
the current arrangement (which does not mean power is necessarily in the right
---
ul
hands). But power games in the social realm are complex as populations align with
the prevailing cultural context and dominant ideological undercurrents o
---
 their
time. Mobilizing enough social momentum to make an idea real means aligning
purpose
---
ully with these currents, adapting means to ends. Social interventions
require clear-sightedness; when suggesting and implementing a new design, this
will most certainly be a manipulation o
---
 the status quo that in turn will rearrange or
cement diﬀerent positions o
---
 power – the question is how and to what degree.

What to design?

Who/Whom?

Closing propositions 119

Under these conditions, what is the room aﬀorded 
---
or realizing one’s design
visions, and what are the risks?
Though seemingly simple, such questions may act as a use
---
ul starting point 
---
or
engaging with social design, serving as an antidote 
---
or detrimental eﬀects that may
inadvertently emerge 
---
rom endeavors that start 
---
rom a purely idealist position.
The Realdesign perspective on social design practices can be elicited by a series
o
---
 questions:
�

�
�
�
�
�
�
�

Who is the user or stakeholder? What is in their interest?
Who is the client? What is in their interest?
Who do you report to? What is in their interest?
Who is invited/included and who is excluded in present and historical context?
What are the structural and institutional 
---
rameworks around this action?
How is agency and power redistributed, who is oﬀered power, who is it
withdrawn 
---
rom?
How is the “social” (relations o
---
 loyalty and commitments) redistributed or
re
---
ormed?
Who earns what in the end?
How can reimbursement/compensation 
---
or the weakest be guaranteed, or
how must they mobilize to enhance their leverage?

What we hope the concept o
---
 Realdesign brings about to the design ﬁeld, is a
more earnest review o
---
 the possibilities that participation and social 
---
orms o
---

design have to actually implement the visions emerging 
---
rom the community
engaged in the design process. The purpose is not to suggest an all-knowing
technocratic designer to decide simply because he or she “knows best”.
Rather, just like social relations are a recalcitrant and unruly material, power is
slippery material to engage, work with, and give shape to as a designer. While
power is a material that will not go away, it is a material that can be taken, secured,
and distributed, using design agency. Betrayal, corruption, cunning, and hypocrisy
are inherent parts o
---
 political power games and must be accounted 
---
or without sel
---
deception. This is the important lesson o
---
 von Rochau to designers; 
---
or visions to
be implemented, they need to be backed by a clear-sighted political perspective
and accompanied by mobilization o
---
 power. Relying on the benevolence or mercy
o
---
 the surrounding world leaves visions toothless. But what the republican tradition
teaches is that to avoid the abuse o
---
 power, it is better distributed and controlled
through the dissemination o
---
 agency and control, checks and balances.
Despite the critique against prevailing idealisms put 
---
orward in this book, we
strongly believe that social 
---
orms o
---
 design are much needed across societies to
enhance the agency o
---
 users and citizens. To the practitioner o
---
 Realdesign, the
challenge is to persist in asking that uncom
---
ortable question: “Who whom?”
